HEADLINE: Market Place;
Soviet Gold News Has Little Impact

BYLINE: By Jonathan Fuerbringer

 BODY:
THE recent disclosure by a top Soviet official that his nation had far less in gold reserves than was thought in the West has had surprisingly little impact on the price of gold, which has jumped by just over $11 an ounce, or 3.3 percent, since mid-September.
Gold is still near the bottom of its recent trading range, and, at $358 an ounce yesterday, is far from the peaks of $400-plus that it touched a few times since February 1990. And gold analysts do not expect the price to move significantly higher, although the Soviet acknowledgement was one of the first pieces of positive news for the gold market in some time. 
 Some estimates call for a possible price of $400 an ounce, or a bit more, next year, but there is no expectation of a monster rally that could bring gold back to the center of investors' attention.
Prices of gold stocks have risen much more than the price of bullion recently, but this is normal. An increase in gold prices goes directly to the bottom line, once production costs are covered, and has a multiplier effect on earnings that is greater than the percentage increase in the price of gold.
In the United States, Merrill Lynch's gold index is up 14 percent since mid-September. The gold index for Canadian shares is up 10.7 percent in United States dollars; the Australian index is up 11.5 percent in American dollars, and the South African index is 6.2 percent higher in the period.
But the rally in gold stocks can turn around quickly if the price of gold does not hold. Prices could also fall if the rally is powered by the anticipation of higher gold prices that do not materilize.
"It is like surfing," Warren Myers, vice president at Merrill Lynch Capital Markets in New York, said of the stock prices. "It is a wonderful ride. The trick is to get off at the right time."
The reason the gold price has not moved much is because the market has become a backwater in the financial market; just a tributary in the flow of money around the world. The market has been depressed by years of bad price performance and undermined by oversupply. And it will take more than the bullish Soviet news to bring back enough investors to push the price back up.
"We have found that the investors and the gold bugs of the early 1980's have been driven out," said Bruce L. Kaplan, president of Kaplan & Company, a precious-metals consulting firm in Santa Monica, Calif. "It is going to take a long time before people get re-interested in gold.
"In years past," he added, referring to the Soviet disclosures, "this news would have sparked the gold investors. Then they were hanging on every word. But they have canceled their gold newsletters and they have moved to the stock market."
Stephen Pepper, senior vice president of the Republic National Bank, added: "If it is true, it should be bullish for gold in the long term. But it has not brought back the kind of buying that I would have thought of with this kind of news."
Gold watchers do argue that the Soviet disclosure has put a new floor under gold, meaning that recent predictions of $325 an ounce, or even less, are just about out of the question now.
The disclosure by Grigory A. Yavlinsky, the senior Soviet economist and architect of a plan to move the Soviet Union toward a market economy, was made in early October and has since been refined. Mr. Yavlinsky said that Soviet domestic gold reserves had fallen to 240 metric tons, with another 150 metric tons outside the country but already pledged against hard-currency loans.
The figures shocked the gold market, since, for years, analysts -- including those at the Central Inteligence Agency -- had thought the Soviets had up to 800 to 3,000 metric tons of gold. Because of the Government's desperate need for hard currency to support its disintegrating economy, many market analysts had feared that much of this gold would be sold to raise money. These expected gold sales, which would add to the supply of an already depressed commodity, were the threat that was overhanging the market and were helping to hold down the price of gold.
The actual reserves available are still not known, although after the early reports there were several experts, both inside and outside the Soviet Union, who thought the new disclosures were more accurate than the old assumptions.
Theodore Arnold, a metals specialist for Merrill Lynch in London, said that the exact amount did not really matter because it seemed that the number was below the general old consensus of around 1,500 metric tons. And, more important, so much of the gold has probably already been pledged for loans to support the economy that it cannot be sold anyway. So the threat to the market of huge sales is diminished, even if there turns out to be more gold than Mr. Yavlinsky said.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Boeing Gets Japan Jet Order; Earnings Rise

BYLINE: By LAWRENCE M. FISHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Oct. 28

 BODY:
Underscoring the success of the Boeing Company's newest jetliner, Japan Airlines said today that it had placed 20 orders and options for the mid-sized 777's. The order, valued at $2 billion, comes two months after a $3.6 billion order for 777's from British Airways, and brings the number of customers for the new plane to six.
United Airlines is to take first delivery of a 777 in May 1995. 
 Also today, Boeing reported improved earnings and revenue for the third quarter, which it attributed primarily to improved commercial aircraft sales and decreased losses from defense and aerospace business. Boeing shares rose 75 cents today, to close at $49.375 on the New York Stock Exchange.
 
Replacing DC-10's
 The Japan Airlines Company selected the 777 to replace aging McDonnell Douglas DC-10's on domestic and regional routes, with deliveries scheduled between 1995 and 1999. As configured for Japan Airlines, the 777 will seat 380 passengers in two classes.
The airline's choice of an engine manufacturer was not disclosed in the announcement. Japan Airlines has traditionally purchased engines primarily from the Pratt & Whitney Company, but it has recently turned to the General Electric Company. Rolls-Royce is a third candidate.
"The willingness of this airline to continue to consider twin-engined equipment is very promising" for Boeing, said Wolfgang Demisch, an analyst with UBS Phillips & Drew in New York. Japan Airlines "is not about to buy a pig in a poke, but this does suggest that if Boeing can make the 777 do everything they think it can, it will be a very formidable force on trans-Pacific routes," he said.
Japan Airlines is the largest user of Boeing's jumbo jet, the 747; even so, its purchase of the 777 represents a victory for the Seattle-based company. Japan Airlines had already ordered a number of MD-11's, McDonnell Douglas's newest edition of the three-engined DC-10, and a direct competitor to the 777.
 
Growth Despite Recession
 "We're proud to have Boeing's largest wide-body customer put its stamp of approval on our new airplane," said Frank Shrontz, Boeing's chairman and chief executive, in a statement. "This order from Japan Airlines confirms that we have designed an airplane preferred by major airlines worldwide."
Boeing also reported a 6.1 percent rise in third quarter earnings to $401 million, or $1.17 a share, from $378 million, or $1.10 a share, in the period a year ago. Sales rose 6 percent to $7.66 billion from $7.18 billion.
Despite the continuing recession, few airlines have asked for delays in delivery of new aircraft, and these have to some extent been balanced by requests from other customers for accelerated delivery, Boeing said. No firm orders for jet transports have been canceled, but several airlines have said they would not exercise some of their options, the company said.

HEADLINE: Blue Chips Lead a Rally; Dow Adds 40.70

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:
Stocks rose strongly yesterday on moderate trading volume, with blue-chip issues leading the broader market. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 40.70 points, to close at 3,045.62.
Although trading ended on a strong note, many investors remained noncommittal and held neutral positions as they awaited economic reports due out this week; data on third-quarter gross national product is to be released today. 
 According to a survey released yesterday by Bloomberg Business News, economists expect that real G.N.P. rose at an annual rate of 2.3 percent in the third quarter. Optimism about the report pushed the dollar higher yesterday, and that spilled over into stocks, traders said.
Investors are still concerned with corporate earnings and the economy, but afternoon trading received a boost from news reports of remarks by the chairman of the Federal Reserve, Alan Greenspan. In a speech at the University of Rhode Island, he said the economy was slow in coming out of the recession, raising hopes in the stock market for yet another cut in interest rates.
His comments, which were less pessimistic than early reports had indicated, also helped to firm bond prices.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was 161.6 million shares, down from the 167.3 million shares Friday. Issues that rose in price outpaced losers by 1,001 to 596.
The Big Board composite index rose 2.62, to 214.44, while the Standard & Poor's index of 500 stocks gained 5.32, to 389.52. The American Stock Exchange composite index rose 2.86 points, to 379.29.
Trading in smaller stocks was also active, as the Nasdaq composite index of over-the-counter stocks gained 4.28, to 529.41, and the Russell 2000 index was up 0.80, to 180.74.
Norman J. Shapiro, a senior vice president for equity trading at Vanguard Securities, said yesterday's rally was led by blue chips that pulled along the rest of the market. He noted strength in the drug sector, where he said investors usually take defensive positions in uncertain markets.
The positive tone was set by the overseas markets and by buying in selected United States issues. Traders had expected the early gains to fade, but Mr. Greenspan's remarks fueled further buying.
Among the closely watched issues yesterday were Comerica and Manufacturers National, two Detroit banks that announced plans to merge. Shares of Manufacturers National were up 3 3/4, to 37 1/2, while Comerica rose 5, to 47 7/8.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY
The economy has taken a turn for the worse, the Fed chairman said. Alan Greenspan told executives in Rhode Island that the economy was still growing slightly, but that "in recent weeks, it's turned demonstrably sluggish." He gave no indication, even indirectly, of his intentions on interest rates. [Page D1.]
A Federal prosecutor has traveled to Egypt to interview the former director of Saudi Arabian intelligence about the Bank of Credit and Commerce International scandal. [D1.] 
 Consumer groups accused the Transportation Department of manipulating crash tests to support the auto industry view that stricter fuel standards would produce more dangerous cars. [A24.]
Hospitals and nursing homes are successfully suing states to force them to increase Medicaid payments. [A1.]
Private employers may not discriminate against homosexuals as job applicants or employees, a panel of California judges ruled. [A19.]
 
COMPANIES
Two strong Detroit-based banks agreed to merge. The combination of Comerica and Manufacturers National will create the largest banking company in the Detroit market. [D1.]
Japan Airlines has placed 20 orders and options for Boeing's 777, underscoring the success of the new jetliner. Boeing also reported improved earnings and revenues. [D1.]
ARCO lost $156 million in the third quarter after taking a $340 million charge against earnings. [D6.] Eastman Kodak lost $118 million after taking account of restructuring costs of $435 million. [D5.] Xerox's third-quarter earnings fell 9 percent. [D5.]
Digital Equipment has suffered through two grim years, but the company's announcement of two fast new computers has analysts talking about a turnaround. [D1.] A new supercomputer from Thinking Machines will be the first in a new wave. [D9.]
Mario Perillo has traveled much of the world, taking more than a million others with him. Now, at age 65, the tour operator is striking out in new directions. [D1.]
CBS executives expressed delight and relief at the results of the 1991 World Series telecasts. Even so, the network expected to lose heavily on its deal with Major League Baseball. [D24.]
A Federal District Court jury upheld the validity of Xoma's patent on the use of monoclonal antibodies to treat septic shock. [D5.] A Japanese court has ruled in favor of Genentech in a suit against Toyobo. [D5.]
 
INTERNATIONAL
The Mexican Government sold a controlling interest in Bancomer, the nation's leading retail bank, for $2.55 billion. [D2.]
 
MARKETS
Alan Greenspan's pessimistic remarks on the economy helped to lift the prices of Treasury notes and bonds. [D22.]
Stocks rose strongly on moderate trading volume. The Dow Jones industrials rose 40.70, to 3,045.62. [D10.]
Wheat futures prices fell sharply after a weekend of heavy rain in wheat-growing areas. [D23.] The dollar closed higher against most major currencies. Gold prices fell. [D23.]
 
TODAY'S COLUMNS
Executives of companies that move from states like New York to lower-cost areas like south Florida are sometimes shocked to find health-care costs there as high as, or higher than, the states they are leaving. Milt Freudenheim: Business and Health. [D2.]
The disclosure that the Soviet Union has far less in gold reserves than was thought in the West has had surprisingly little impact on the price of gold. Market Place. [D10.]
Procter & Gamble's chairman issued a challenge to his peers, asking them to do a better job of demonstrating their faith in advertising. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D24.]

HEADLINE: Greenspan Sees Turn For Worse

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
In his gloomiest assessment since last winter, Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, said today that the national economy had taken a turn for the worse.
The economy is still growing slightly, Mr. Greenspan told a group of business executives in Providence, R.I., but "in recent weeks, it's turned demonstrably sluggish." 
 Early news service reports characterized Mr. Greenspan's speech as even more pessimistic than it was, and traders in the stock and bond markets took the remarks to mean the Fed would act soon to lower interest rates. The Dow Jones industrial average jumped 40.70, to 3,045.62, and prices of Treasury notes and bonds rose. [Pages D10 and D22.]
 
No Indication on Rates
 Mr. Greenspan, however, gave no indication even indirectly of what his intentions might be on interest rates. He has recently been under increasing pressure from Treasury Secretary Nicholas F. Brady and others in the Administration to drive rates lower. The Federal Open Market Committee, the policy-making arm of the Fed, meets next on Nov. 5.
In July, when he gave Congress his official midyear review of the state of the economy, Mr. Greenspan proclaimed the recession over, reported signs that inflation was slowing, predicted progress on the Federal budget deficit and was otherwise remarkably optimistic.
Today's speech, which he delivered from notes, had a much different tone. The "signs of spark" that could be observed in the economy in the spring and early summer, Mr. Greenspan said, were no longer apparent.
 
'Credit Crunch' Cited
 In addition to not tipping his hand on interest rates, Mr. Greenspan did not say whether other steps should be taken to lift the economy. Many lawmakers of both parties have offered tax-cut proposals in recent days, saying that a cut was necessary to generate a surge in the economy.
Mr. Greenspan referred often today to what he called a "credit crunch" and suggested that it was now the main factor dragging the economy down. "The economy is moving forward, but in the face of a 50-mile-an-hour head wind," he declared.
When bank lending is tight, the growth in the nation's money supply is inhibited, and that inhibition acts as a drain on the economy.
The Federal Reserve has been pushing interest rates down steadily, so that they are now at their lowest point since the 1970's. But since that decline has not led to more bank lending, it has not caused the economy to grow as much as Mr. Greenspan and other officials hoped.
Some Bush Administration officials have been arguing that the main cause of the credit restrictions is overly zealous restrictions placed by bank examiners.
But Mr. Greenspan suggested that more important reasons were the reluctance of banks to lend freely when they have many bad loans already on their books and the disinclination of businesses to borrow money in the face of a weak economy.
 
Focus on Credit
 Most of Mr. Greenspan's speech today was devoted to the credit situation, a problem that is especially severe in New England. Mr. Greenspan implied, although he did not say so directly, that he was not sure whether the inability of creditworthy customers to find loans was a short-term or a long-term phenomenon.
The problem had its roots, he said, in the tax law of 1981, which allowed generous new tax breaks for real estate construction. Many parts of the country experienced a significiant overbuilding of commercial real estate, and banks found that their loans for these buildings were not repaid.
"We just overbuilt to a point that is quite incredible," he said.
Eventually, Mr. Greenspan said, credit will once again become more available. He said that availability would begin when banks see that they have fewer nonperforming loans in their portfolios. Banks will then realize that unless they lend more aggressively, they will lose their share of the market to competitors.

HEADLINE: In Debate on U.S. Poverty, 2 Studies Fuel an Argument on Who Is to Blame

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
For seven consecutive years beginning in 1983, the celebrated American economic expansion brought disappointing results in the fight against poverty, defying historical experience and leaving the country with poverty rates unusually high among prosperous Western nations.
The question has been why. 
 In recent months, two academic studies have addressed that question and come to an unsettling conclusion: America has high poverty rates not because it must, but because it chooses to.
Comparing the United States with selected Western European countries and Canada, the studies contend that with comparable patterns of economic growth, other nations reduced poverty to a far greater extent. The difference, the studies say, is that other countries have more generous and effective social policies.
 
'Tolerating' More Poverty
 "It's simple: we choose to tolerate a lot more poverty than do other countries," said Timothy M. Smeeding, an economist at Syracuse University who wrote one of the studies.
Mr. Smeeding, who is drawing on previously unavailable data, argued that by adopting similar policies, the United States could make comparable inroads against poverty. Contending that the United States needs to spend more and design its programs better, he advocates a system of national health care, a program that guarantees child support for single parents and universal cash payments to families with children. He also calls for an expanded program of unemployment benefits and for more Government day care.
"I think it's hokum," said Douglas Besharov, a resident scholar at the American Enterprise Institute, a Washington research organization.
 
Studies Termed Flawed
 Mr. Besharov said the studies had huge technical flaws that make their comparisons misleading. He said they compare the United States only with the most prosperous nations of Europe, conveniently omitting poverty-stricken areas like Romania and Bulgaria.
Above all, he contended that this country's highly unusual position as a nation of stunning ethnic diversity, constant immigration and loose social norms rendered European comparisons meaningless.
Mr. Besharov argued that America's high rates of poverty were more difficult to solve than Europe's because they involved the failure of some poor people to seek work. Providing increased benefits without imposing obligations to work or study might only worsen the problem, he said, by encouraging more dependency.
"Liberals always say, 'Gee, if we could only do it as well as the Europeans,' " he said. "But the problem is we don't have the same kind of poor people. Our problem is with poor people who don't work."
 
Legislation Is Considered
 The debate over the European experience comes as American poverty rates, stubbornly high even during a time of national prosperity, are once again on a recession-driven rise, and some lawmakers are urging variations of Western European solutions.
Among the European-style policies being considered by Congress are variations on a national system of health care, and proposals to provide cash allowances to families with children.
"The United States is the only Western industrialized nation without a child allowance policy," said Senator John D. Rockefeller 4th, a West Virginia Democrat who is pushing such plans. "And it shows."
Lawrence Mead, a political scientist at New York University, said the timing of these proposals was disturbing since they came as some Western European countries were cutting back, for fear that they were producing dependency. "In Europe, you're beginning to see an ethnically and behaviorally distinct underclass," he said.
Mr. Smeeding contended that in the mid-1980's, market forces alone produced a similar amount of poverty here and in the other countries he studied: Canada, Australia, Sweden, Germany, the Netherlands, France, and Britain.
But he said that huge differences emerge when Government programs are counted. Ambitious social programs in the other countries eliminated about three-quarters of their poverty, Mr. Smeeding said, but social policy in this country reduced poverty by only about one-third.
After Government programs are counted, the poverty rate of 10.9 percent for the American elderly was nearly four times the average rate in the other countries, he said. The poverty rate of 20.4 percent for American children was about three times as high.
Amid this debate, there is substantial agreement that economic growth alone is less of a prescription for fighting poverty than it used to be.
Liberals tend to emphasize changes in the economy that have depressed wages for the least-skilled workers. Conservatives emphasize the failure of many poor people to seek work.
In the second new study, comparing the two longest economic expansions in modern American history, Rebecca M. Blank, an economist at Northwestern University, documented the change in the ability of economic growth to reduce poverty.
During the longest expansion, from 1961 to 1970, the percentage of Americans living in poverty dropped 9.6 points, to 12.6 percent. During the second-longest expansion, from 1983 to 1989, the drop was slight by comparison, a 2.4-point dip to 12.8 percent.
Had the same correlation held true between economic growth and the reduction of poverty, Ms. Blank said, the 1989 poverty rate would have been 9.3 percent, meaning that 8.7 million fewer Americans would have lived in poverty.
"Trickle down did not work in the 1980's as well as it did in the 1960's," she said. Ms. Blank attributed the difference to a collapse in wages for the lowest-paid workers.
Comparing the United States with Canada, Ms. Blank found that similar economic trends held true in both countries for the past decade but that Canada's poverty rates fell much faster.
Examining data for 1986, Ms. Blank found that market forces alone left 17.5 percent of Canadians poor. But once welfare assistance and other Government programs were counted, the rate fell to 11.8 percent.
By contrast, the United States started out with less poverty but wound up with more: market forces produced a poverty rate of 15.5 percent, but Government programs cut that figure little, to 13.6 percent.
"The difference is almost entirely due to Canada's more generous public assistance program," she said.
Ms. Blank relied on the official American definition of poverty, which is based on a minimal food budget and is updated every year for inflation. In 1990, for example, a family of four was considered poor if it received less than $13,359. To arrive at the Canadian poverty figures, she simply converted the thresholds into Canadian currency.
 
Method of Study Attacked
 Some American conservatives are sharply critical of this method, saying it exaggerates the extent of American poverty since it does not count Government programs that provide noncash benefits like subsidized housing, food stamps and medical care.
Analyzing previously unavailable figures from Western Europe, Mr. Smeeding found similar patterns. He said other countries started with comparable or even higher amounts of poverty than the United States, but used policy much more effectively in reducing it.
On average in the mid-1980's, the economies of the countries he studied produced a poverty rate of 22.4 percent. But after social programs were counted, that figure fell to 5.9 percent.
The drop was much more modest in the United States, falling from 19.9 percent before Government assistance to 13.3 percent after it. Mr. Smeeding defined as poor any household receiving less than 40 percent of that nation's median income, a measure that he said approximated the United States standard. He did not count noncash benefits.
Mr. Besharov said Mr. Smeeding's definition of poverty was meaningless. Given differences in international standards of living, he said, someone defined as poor in America may in fact have more material goods than French or Swedes who are not considered poor.
Most of all, Mr. Besharov questioned the idea that European solutions could be imported without producing harmful side effects.
For instance, he contended that in most European countries there were strong social pressures for teen-agers not to have babies. He said European countries could therefore provide more money to single mothers, with fewer fears that they would increase teen-age pregnancy.
If the United States gave single young mothers more money, he argued, it might just get more single young mothers. "We are a much freer society," he said.
Mr. Smeeding argued that the differences between the societies was not as large as Mr. Besharov said and that policies should be modified for an American context.
"What he's saying is that all international comparisons are illegitimate," Mr. Smeeding said. "I just don't agree."

HEADLINE: BUSH RULING OUT TAX CUT FOR NOW

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
President Bush pledged today to fight "tooth and nail" to revive the nation's sluggish economy, but he indicated that he would not propose a tax cut at this time.
Mr. Bush accused the Democratic-controlled Congress of panicking with a welter of tax cut measures and suggested that he was not about to follow suit with hasty steps that he said could do more harm than good. 
 After meeting with the President, Representative Newt Gingrich of Georgia, who is the House Republican whip, told reporters, "There's no indication of being able to produce a package in the next couple weeks," with Congress scheduled to adjourn for the year on Nov. 22.
"If, on the other hand, Congress stays in during Thanksgiving, I think the President's pressure to get something passed would increase substantially," Mr. Gingrich said.
 
Meeting With G.O.P. Leaders
 Mr. Bush and Richard G. Darman, the director of the Office of Management and Budget, discussed the economy at a luncheon meeting with Bob Dole of Kansas, the Senate minority leader; Representative Robert H. Michel of Illinois, the House minority leader; Mr. Gingrich, and Senator Alan K. Simpson, Republican of Wyoming.. Although White House officials said earlier that the President would propose his own legislation in an effort to promote economic growth, Mr. Bush appeared to rule out such a course, both at the luncheon with Republican leaders and at a speech to a group of officials in the natural gas industry.
"I know there's a lot to be done," the President said in a teleconference with an American Gas Association convention in San Diego. "I'm not relaxed about the economy."
He blamed Congress for slowing the pace of economic recovery by ignoring his domestic proposals, measures that he contended would have helped create jobs. He mentioned legislation intended to promote highway and mass transit projects and to give tax breaks to businesses that locate in low-income urban areas.
But, referring to the tax cut bills on Capitol Hill, Mr. Bush said: "I'm not going to jump in and take steps out of some Congressional panic that might make the situation worse. It might burden future generations more."
The President and other officials in his Administration have complained that many measures they consider to be economic stimulants, including a proposal to lower the rate on capital gains taxes, have been languishing on Capitol Hill for some time.
"Throughout the coming legislative year, we will fight tooth and nail for economic growth, opportunity and jobs, and I will fight against legislation that will bust the budget agreement and further burden the young people of this country with more and more debt, with bigger and bigger Federal deficits," Mr. Bush said.
Republican leaders said the President indicated he would redouble his efforts to get a trimmed-down bill passed to extend unemployment benefits.
His veto of a Democratic bill to extend the benefits for 20 weeks beyond the 26 weeks under existing law was sustained by a two-vote margin in the Senate. Democrats have accused the President of being indifferent to the plight of the unemployed.
Mr. Bush has said he favors a bill sponsored by Senator Dole that would extend the benefits for 10 weeks, but no vote has been scheduled on that bill.

HEADLINE: U.S. Agency Accused of Manipulating Crash Tests

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
Two consumer groups today accused the Transportation Department of manipulating car crash tests and selectively making the results public to support the automobile industry's contention that stricter fuel economy standards would produce more dangerous cars.
In a caustic rejoinder, the head of the department's traffic safety agency dismissed the accusations, lending the debate over fuel efficiency and safety an increasingly bitter tone just as the Senate prepares to consider tougher standards for gas mileage. 
 The consumer groups, the Center for Auto Safety and Public Citizen, said that Jerry R. Curry, head of the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration, was seeking to influence Congress by frightening drivers when he ordered the agency to conduct tests and make a videotape that would demonstrate the safety of larger cars. The groups said Mr. Curry suppressed other tests that produced less dramatic results.
 
'No Creditability'
 Mr. Curry responded with an attack on Clarence Ditlow, head of the Center for Auto Safety, whom he called "as nonobjective a person as you can get," and someone with "no creditability in the traffic safety community." But Mr. Curry avoided criticizing the head of Public Citizen, Joan Claybrook, who headed the traffic safety agency in the Carter Administration. The two groups have long been critical of the agency.
The safety agency's pictures of a head-on collision between a large and a small car have been widely shown in recent televised and printed advertisements sponsored by the automobile industry and its allies, who contend that requiring Detroit to build more efficient cars would compromise safety by putting smaller cars on the road.
The safety argument is at the heart of the debate over legislation that may come before the Senate this week, calling for a 40 percent increase in the average car's efficiency within 10 years.
Throughout the debate, the safety agency has contended that big gains in fuel economy would come mainly from making cars smaller and subjecting passengers to added risks in collisions and rollovers.
The agency meant to underscore that message with its videotape, which was issued last May with a letter emphasizing the dangers of small cars that might accompany new legislation.
But according to the consumer groups, the agency did not publicize other tests it had conducted that did not support the claim, at least not as vividly. Nor did it make public detailed test results showing that the driver of the smaller car in the video could have survived the impact, the groups said.
In some tests conducted by the agency, the occupants of smaller cars fared better than those in larger cars. That could be the case, for example, when testing pairs of medium-sized cars whose weights did not differ very much, or when the tests involved cars equipped with air bags or when a car skidded sideways into a utility pole.
"These tests do not clearly demonstrate the safety problems of small cars," said one Government memorandum describing some of the tests that the safety agency conducted. The memorandum and related test results were obtained by the consumer groups under a law governing the release of official documents.
The cars shown in the advertisement were selected by Mr. Curry from a list provided by his staff in a series of tests. The list included large, small and medium-sized cars, some of them equipped with air bags.
Mr. Curry selected the Ford Crown Victoria, a very large car, as one test vehicle, and arranged collisions with a pair of much smaller cars built by Suzuki and Subaru. The small cars weigh about half the large car, and get about twice as many miles per gallon.
The result of the crash was as spectacular as it was predictable. The big car plows through the front of the smaller one, tossing the car to one side.
Mr. Curry said that nothing in the testing program was deceptive, and he dismissed the suggestion by the consumer groups that by producing and disseminating the video the department had engaged in illegal lobbying.

HEADLINE: Salvadoran Foes Debating a New Police Force

BYLINE: By SHIRLEY CHRISTIAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN SALVADOR, Oct. 28

 BODY:
Political and military leaders and diplomats have begun to debate and study the creation of the broad-based national police force that is to be an important element in the Salvadoran peace accord, even while the guerrillas and armed forces attacked and taunted each other without letup in recent days.
Archbishop Arturo Rivera y Damas said in his Sunday homily that El Salvador was hearing "songs of peace," played to "music of war." 
 The guerrillas of the Farabundo Marti National Liberation Front continued to sabotage economic infrastructure, including the bombing of two bridges on main highways in the eastern part of the country, further attacks on the electric power network and damage to thousands of coffee trees. The army continued to pursue the rebels in scattered combat operations across the mountains in the north and east of the country.
The Archbishop, while lamenting the guerrilla sabotage, said two civilians had also been found dead and appeared to be victims of right-wing death squads. He said these "old things" seriously threatened peace.
 
Criticism Exchanged
 The armed forces command criticized the guerrillas for inconveniencing civilians, and the guerrillas responded on their radio station by asserting that the "dogs of war" -- the army -- were not ready for a Christmas at peace after 12 years of war.
The guerrillas added that they were attacking civilian targets because the army had abandoned its responsibilities to guard the cities, highways and bridges in the populous areas of the country to mount operations in mountainous areas that the insurgents consider theirs.
Although the latest round of United Nations-sponsored negotiations, which ended last week in Mexico, did not produce dramatic results, both sides indicated that they still expect to agree on a cease-fire before the end of the year.
Under partial accords signed in New York last month, the new national police force is to replace three existing security forces -- the national guard, the national police and the treasury police -- and is to be open to former guerrillas as well as to those who fought for the Government, and to noncombatants.
This arrangement was agreed on as a means of guaranteeing to the guerrillas that their lives will be respected after a cease-fire and that other commitments made by the Government -- including electoral and judicial reforms and reduction and reform of the army -- will be kept.
 
Longstanding Demand Dropped
 In exchange, the guerrillas agreed to drop their longstanding demand to be incorporated into the army itself, or for the disbanding of the army.
Nevertheless, the organization and makeup of the new police force have set off sharp disputes in recent days among guerrilla field commanders, the Government, the army and opposition political parties.
The Government of President Alfredo Cristiani and the army leadership assert that the accords provide only for the guerrillas to be admitted to the future police academy without ideological prejudice, without guaranteeing the insurgents a leadership role.
But the guerrillas talk of moving into some command positions in the police as soon as the force is created. That was reinforced in the recent talks when a group of guerrilla field commanders left their mountain hideouts here and went to Mexico City for a meeting with the guerrilla leaders conducting the negotiations with the Government.
Gen. Orlando Zepeda, the Deputy Minister of Defense, asserted on Thursday that the guerrillas were expecting to take command of the new police posts in zones of the country where rebel forces consider themselves in control. He called that "offensive" and said hardly anyone in El Salvador was in agreement with such a move.
When some of the guerrilla field commanders who went to Mexico returned to their areas Friday, they told journalists that they were determined to see to it that "no one will be able to monopolize" the new police force.

HEADLINE: Irate Dinkins Defends Trip To Germany

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM

 BODY:
Bristling with indignation, Mayor David N. Dinkins yesterday defended his plan for an expenses-paid "working vacation" to a German tennis tournament in December, questioned the motives of his critics and insisted that whatever his popularity or problems at home, he still stood tall around the world.
Mr. Dinkins said that he had accepted the invitation to the Compaq Grand Slam Cup in Munich, Germany, at the behest of its German promoter because, "among other things, they're going to pay for it," and that he saw nothing wrong with taking free trips. He said that the promoter, Dr. Axel Meyer-Wolden, had no financial ties to the United States Tennis Association, which is seeking to expand its tennis center in Queens, and that the trip posed no conflict of interest.
 
'Best Interests of Our City'
 The Mayor said he also intended to visit the Dachau concentration camp and other historical sites to honor the memory of Holocaust victims and to court European businesses with a side trip to Berlin on what he said would be a three- to five-day visit, starting Dec. 10, just a month after his planned weeklong trip to South Africa. 
 The mayor is to be accompanied by his Deputy Mayor for Finance and Economic Development, Sally B. Hernandez-Pinero, his aides said. His side trip to Berlin would be financed by the New York City Alliance for International Business, a nonprofit group set up by the city to foster international business ties.
"I don't expect that I should get gold stars because I'm willing to make such a trip," he told reporters at City Hall. "But I certainly don't expect to be condemned for doing what I think is in the best interests of our city."
The Mayor said that if he went to "lie on a beach for four days," he would get no flak, and he professed confusion at why the trips had drawn scrutiny when other jaunts by his predecessors had not. "I'm not going to raise with you questions of how come people are asking me about this, when they didn't raise these questions heretofore.
"I wish I knew," he said.
Mr. Dinkins also took pains to tell the reporters how patient he believed he was in answering irritating or unfair questions. "I figure sooner or later you're going to give me a little credit for that. Sooner or later you're going to acknowledge what the facts are because you're going to have to look yourselves in the mirror. And sooner or later you're going to say, 'Damn, the guy does stand up and answer.' "

HEADLINE: Our Towns

BYLINE: By Andrew H. Malcolm

DATELINE: SOUTH ORANGE, N.J.

 BODY:
Here's the scenario: A well-dressed lady who knows everything that's fit to print about the community leaves her car in the fading afternoon suburban sunshine. She walks toward the house along a leaf-strewn sidewalk, never cutting across the lawn. She approaches the door of the house on one of an endless number of streets lined with substantial private homes owned by hard-working professionals and their mortgage companies.
The woman rings the doorbell, then steps back to leave some nonthreatening distance in front of the door. She shifts the huge wicker basket with the pretty bow to her left hand to permit a handshake with these strangers. When the husband appears, the woman smiles broadly, introduces herself and is welcomed into the home just like that, without a badge or references. But first, she wipes her feet. 
 A dream? A reunion of the Donna Reed Show? Harriet Nelson seeking Ozzie? Nope. The Welcome Wagon lady has struck again.
It still happens thousands of times a day across the country, even in the crowded New York area where many people don't even notice their neighbors' departure, let alone their arrival.
For 63 years now, Welcome Wagon representatives have been calling on households for any number of reasons. The best-known excuse is the family's recent arrival from somewhere else (some frontier American towns used to dispatch welcome wagons to greet wandering pioneers, hoping they would choose to settle in their communities).
Welcome Wagon is, of course, still a privately held business. The Memphis headquarters charges a fee to every merchant recommended by its legions and then pays a percentage to the lady with the basket. But today, nearly half the average Welcome Wagon representative's calls are for other reasons: an engagement, a marriage, a new baby, a new business, a 50th anniversary. And that's not all that's changed.
Sigrid Novak has been smiling her way all over South Orange from doorbell button to doorbell button for nearly 10 years now. "It's fun," she says, "plain and simple."
When she began, every call was made in the daytime. Now, few are. With so many two-career families, virtually every call today is in the evening or on Saturday mornings. Families are more leery of strangers at the door now; they want an appointment and no unannounced drop-ins. Trouble is, to protect their privacy, many more families now have unlisted phone numbers.
So Mrs. Novak leaves her big cheery business card in the door asking them to call her for an appointment. Most do. With few local daytime contacts in the community, a family's only link to local services and activities is often Mrs. Novak.
Even when a recession trims the number of corporate moves, South Orange's Welcome Wagon lady keeps busy welcoming new professionals who may eventually add their free coupons and consultations to her basket. Mrs. Novak even works the Seton Hall University campus, calling on new employees. They may live in another town, her theory goes, but they spend their working day and lunch money in South Orange.
Altogether, she makes about 40 calls a month. Late the other afternoon she was prompt for her call on James and Rebecca Ries over on Warwick Avenue. Seven years ago, Mrs. Novak remembered, she welcomed these newcomers from Idaho. This time she welcomed the couple's two new adopted babies, Katherine and Dragos.
Her basket was specially equipped for the occasion. Mrs. Novak spent 75 minutes handing out refrigerator magnets and pens, booklets on cultural and municipal services and coupons for free baby portraits, a week's worth of cloth-diaper deliveries, free restaurant desserts, haircuts, make-overs, cable hookup and a mystery gift from a hardware store.
She naturally cooed over the 3-month-old babies. She recommended dentists and doctors with evening hours. She urged loyal patronage of local businesses and expounded on a variety of activities for children. When the trio discovered some mutual friends, they discussed them.
But when Mrs. Ries complained about crime -- "It seems to me these days you have the right to be shot in your driveway" -- Mrs. Novak suddenly discovered more items to pull from her basket. Before leaving, she handed the new parents a list of every business she had mentioned. Then, wishing them well and issuing compliments on the renovations since her first visit, Mrs. Novak was down the sidewalk, in the car, restocking her basket.
The visit was a little long because of the baby distractions, but otherwise a standard Welcome Wagon visit, she said. Right down to the VCR.
Like most of these ubiquitous machines that Mrs. Novak glimpses during her visits, it was flashing the wrong time, waiting for some teen-ager to re-program its memory without reading the directions.
Something else that never changes.

GRAPHIC: Photo: "It's fun, plain and simple," said Sigrid Novak of her Welcome Wagon work in South Orange, N.J. (William E. Sauro/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: The Hard Times Roll on Syracuse Campus

BYLINE: By SAMUEL WEISS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SYRACUSE

 BODY:
By any measure, the 1980's were good to Syracuse University. Enrollment flourished, 10 new buildings were opened, the university upgraded many of its academic programs and its basketball and football teams became national powerhouses.
Syracuse seemed to be the academic equivalent of a bull market that had driven the bears into hibernation. But, like so many institutions and industries that flourished in the 1980's, it is now retrenching -- prompting a critical re-examination of the university's strengths and weaknesses that some Syracuse officials say is long overdue. 
 That re-examination, in response to falling enrollment, has already produced a budget cut that includes a salary freeze for all employees. It has also inspired studies that could lead to the elimination of major academic programs over the next several years.
"Ordinarily, there is a strong sense of inertia on university campuses, and Syracuse did not need to make hard choices in an expansionary period," said Dr. John Palmer, dean of the Maxwell School of Citizenship and Public Affairs here, adding that Syracuse, which has 16,500 students, is being forced "to take the kind of hard look at ourselves that all institutions of higher education should be doing."
Syracuse is far from the only university trying to make do with less these days.
Yale University's president, Benno C. Schmidt Jr., for example, has announced cuts of 5 to 10 percent for academic and administrative departments this year. At Stanford University, officials announced budget reductions ranging from 7 to 12 percent in its seven schools.
 
Fewer Undergraduates
 What Syracuse is feeling is similar to "grieving," said Dr. Kenneth A. Shaw, who became the Chancellor in August. The school has lost about 1,700 undergraduates in the last two years, the primary cause of a $9.5 million budget deficit for the fiscal years 1990-92. Undergraduate enrollment, now about 11,500 students, is expected to drop another 1,200 to 1,900 students over the next several years, translating into an additional budget deficit of $28.5 million in the next three years. .
To deal with those problems, Dr. Shaw is expected to announce in February a restructuring that could eliminate 500 to 600 people from the university's 4,000-member work force. An advisory group of administrators and faculty members has already suggested the elimination of some academic programs and the consolidation of some of the university's 14 schools and colleges.
Thomas F. Cummings Jr., vice president for enrollment management and continuing education at Syracuse, said enrollments at Syracuse and many other private schools where tuition has rapidly increased were being hurt by the recession's pinch on family incomes and stiffer competition among colleges and universities for the fewer students now available.
Demographers had projected in the early 1970's that the number of high school graduates, and thus potential college undergraduates, would drop beginning in the 1980's.
At Syracuse, enrollment actually rose. At the same time, fund raising hit an all-time high, construction boomed and the state built the 50,000-seat Carrier Dome.
"We knew we were running countertrend," Mr. Cummings said. "But all you can do is plan on the basis of the present, and all the elements were coming together in a positive way in the 1980's. We did not see any pattern suggesting an enrollment downturn."
 
A 'Pruning' Begun
 Now that the enrollment picture has changed, Syracuse has rejected the solutions of lowering admission standards to fill its classrooms or of using its cash reserves. Instead it has begun a "pruning" that officials say will ultimately make the institution stronger.
Dr. Richard E. Anderson, vice president for finance at Washington University, said that restructuring a major university was "much trickier" than reducing a big corporation. "In a business," Dr. Anderson said, "your inputs and outputs are all calculated in dollar terms. In a university, the inputs are calculated in dollars but the outputs have to be measured in educational value."
Decisions about which values to emphasize will fall largely on Dr. Shaw, who succeeded Dr. Melvin A. Eggers, who resigned as Chancellor after 20 years. Dr. Shaw, who is 52 years old, has tried to relieve some of the anxiety over proposed cuts by opening up the discussion.
"Ever since the announcement, students have been worried about how much of a say they are going to have in all this," said Jodi Lamagna, editor of the student newspaper, The Daily Orange. "So far, the Chancellor has shown real concern for our input."
Prof. Michael A. Sickler, chairman of the local chapter of the American Association of University Professors, said that even though faculty members are being consulted, there is still some "mild hysteria" about the results.
"If you are a member of a school not in high demand by students," he said, "the question is, will your program be closed, or will it be melded with another program with a different set of priorities, curriculum and personnel practices? The concern is particularly acute among the nontenured faculty members hoping to have a future here."
Dr. Shaw, who came to Syracuse from the University of Wisconsin, where he was president for four years, said in an interview that he would be guided by three principles in evaluating the academic programs: educational quality, the "centrality" of a program to the university's basic objectives and student demand. Dr. Shaw said one consequence of restructuring would probably be to reduce some of the university's 77 doctoral programs and some research work as well.
Syracuse has 14 different schools and colleges, and while some units like the Maxwell School of Citizenship and Public Affairs and the S. I. Newhouse School of Public Communication are believed to be likely to be left unscathed because of their academic stature and popularity among students, some may not.
 
Consolidations Proposed
 An advisory committee to the Chancellor suggested last month that some schools with declining enrollments be looked at as candidates for consolidation. One suggestion called for combining the schools of education, human development, nursing and social work. Another called for the consolidation of the School of Information Studies with the School of Management.
Dr. Shaw refused to say which, if any, of these ideas he might seek to implement. But on one thing he was definite: Neither the university's football team, which has made four bowl appearances in the last decade, nor its basketball team, which came within a hair of winning the national championship in 1987, would be harmed.
"Why should they?" Dr. Shaw asked. "They make money."

HEADLINE: Suits Force U.S. and States To Pay More for Medicaid

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 28

 BODY:
Hospitals and nursing homes around the country are successfully suing states to force them to increase Medicaid payments to levels that more nearly reflect what it costs to treat patients.
The multimillion-dollar judgments, propelled by a 1990 Supreme Court decision permitting such suits, are severely straining the budgets of the Federal and state governments, which jointly finance the program to provide medical care to low-income people. 
 In state after state, from New York to Indiana to Washington, judges are finding that Medicaid payments to health-care providers do not meet the standard of "reasonable and adequate" compensation under the law. Instead of reimbursing hospitals and nursing homes based on the costs incurred by efficient health-care providers, states often base their payments simply on budget considerations, the courts say.
 
78 Cents on the Dollar
 According to a survey of hospitals by the American Hospital Association, on average, hospitals receive 78 cents for every dollar it costs to care for Medicaid patients. Judges in several cases have reached similar conclusions.
Federal and state spending for Medicaid has increased by several billion dollars a year as a result of court judgments, settlements and rate increases granted by states anticipating lawsuits.
Costs have been estimated at $62 million in Washington State, $120 million in Virginia, $65 million in Oregon and $90 million in Michigan. One institution that serves many poor people in Philadelphia, Temple University Hospital, is getting an increase of $13 million a year, or 34 percent, in its Medicaid revenue as a result of its lawsuit against Pennsylvania.
State officials around the country say the Medicaid increases come at a time when they are cutting other programs and raising taxes to eliminate budget deficits. Michael H. Hershock, the Budget Secretary of Pennsylvania, said "there just isn't enough money in our tax base to pay" all the rate increases required by Federal court decisions.
Even as lawsuits are forcing increases in Federal and state Medicaid spending, they could mean lower charges for some consumers. By forcing states to raise their Medicaid payments, the lawsuits could slow the increase in charges for people with private health insurance. Currently, hospitals increase charges to people with private health insurance, in a practice known as "cost-shifting," to make up for underpayments by Medicaid.
If the court judgments reverse that trend, "that is going to displease state budget directors, but it will probably please consumers and corporate benefits managers who pay for insurance," said Prof. Mark V. Pauly, a health economist at the Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania.
But health-care experts warn that to offset the costs of complying with court orders, some states might restrict Medicaid eligibility or cut back on optional services rather than increase taxes or shift money from other programs.
Fredric J. Entin, senior vice president and general counsel of the American Hospital Association, said the lawsuits were a partial solution. They curb "the more egregious and transparent attempts" by states to slow the growth of Medicaid by underpaying hospitals and nursing homes, he said.
Medicaid provides health care to 27 million Americans. It pays for the delivery of more than one-third of the babies born in the United States, and it helps finance care for more than two-thirds of all nursing home residents.
 
Rate-Setting on the Bench
 In theory, states still set Medicaid payment rates, commonly paying flat amounts for each patient, depending on the diagnosis. But Jeff E. Harris, senior research analyst at the Medicaid Management Institute, a project of the American Public Welfare Association, said, "The days of judicial rate-setting have begun."
Hospitals have filed lawsuits in 21 states. Nursing homes have brought court challenges in 20 states. Some of the suits were filed before the Supreme Court decision in June 1990, which increased the likelihood of success for hospitals and nursing homes.
The Federal Medicaid law says payments to hospitals and nursing homes must be "reasonable and adequate to meet the costs which must be incurred by efficiently and economically operated facilities." In addition, it says, Medicaid must pay enough to insure that poor people have "reasonable access" to hospital care.
In June 1990 the Supreme Court ruled, 5 to 4, that hospitals and nursing homes were "intended beneficiaries" of the Medicaid law and could sue states to enforce their right to "reasonable and adequate" reimbursement.
 
Millions in Settlement
 When Federal or state judges scrutinize rates, they almost invariably find that Medicaid pays less than the costs incurred by efficiently operated hospitals and nursing homes. The judges often find that state decisions on reimbursement were made strictly for budgetary reasons, to save money, without considering the costs of care.
In Indiana, a state court ruled last month that the state had illegally and systematically underpaid nursing homes, with the result that the "quality of Medicaid patient care is declining due to inadequate rates."
Indiana nursing homes expect to win more than $120 million as a result of that decision. Gov. Evan Bayh, a Democrat, has denounced the court-ordered payment as "a raid upon the taxpayers' pockets" and has vowed to appeal the decision, saying it would enrich the owners of nursing homes without improving patient care.
In Washington State, a Federal district judge ruled in July that state officials had violated the Federal Medicaid law by setting hospital rates substantially below the amounts needed for "reasonable and adequate" compensation. Hospitals there will get an increase of about 10 percent, or $62 million over the next two years.
Last year's Supreme Court decision came in a case brought by the Virginia Hospital Association. The Court said health-care providers could use a Reconstruction-era statute to enforce their right to adequate Medicaid payments. The measure, originally passed in the 1870's to protect former slaves, permits lawsuits against state officials for violations of "any rights, privileges or immunities secured by the Constitution and laws" of the United States.
In Virginia, after the Court decision, 91 hospitals negotiated a settlement under which they will get rate increases totaling $120 million over four years.
Laurens Sartoris, president of the Virginia Hospital Association, said, "Even with the settlement, all our hospitals will still be paid less than the cost of providing care to Medicaid recipients." But he said the additional payments were as generous as could be expected, given the state's fiscal problems.
The United States Court of Appeals for the 10th Circuit, in Denver, has ruled that Colorado violated the law by arbitrarily reducing Medicaid payments to hospitals by 46 percent.
As a result, it said, "no Colorado hospital, no matter how efficiently and economically operated, is reasonably and adequately reimbursed."
While the Federal Department of Health and Human Services is supposed to review state Medicaid payment rates, the court said such review was "cursory at best."
In New York, a Federal district judge ruled in July that the state had to increase Medicaid payments to the Rye Psychiatric Hospital Center. Frederick A. Nicoll, a lawyer for the hospital, said it now receives $423 a day for each Medicaid patient, as against $233 in the past.
Federal District Judge Gerard L. Goettel, who sits in White Plains, ruled that New York had violated the Federal Medicaid law by reducing its rates for private psychiatric hospitals without any formal findings that its payments would be adequate.
In Washington State, Federal district Judge Thomas S. Zilly ruled that state officials had violated the Medicaid law by setting hospital rates substantially below those needed for "reasonable and adequate" compensation. The state negotiated a settlement under which hospitals are to receive $62 million extra over the next two years, representing an increase of about 10 percent.
 
State 'Completely Failed'
 Oregon recently agreed to settle a similar lawsuit by increasing payments to its hospitals by $65 million, or about 30 percent, over two years.
In Michigan, Federal District Judge Robert Holmes Bell concluded that the state had "completely failed to meet the requirements" of the Federal Medicaid law and had "no idea" whether its Medicaid payments were adequate.
In February, Judge Bell approved a settlement expected to increase state Medicaid payments to hospitals by $70 million a year, or 17 percent.
In a separate lawsuit, nursing homes in Michigan won rate increases of about 10 percent, or $20 million to $25 million a year.
In Pennsylvania, the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit ruled that the state had violated the Federal Medicaid law by setting hospital payments without any analysis of their adequacy. The ruling insures higher Medicaid payments for more than 140 hospitals.

HEADLINE: In Brooklyn, Congress Hears From Artists

BYLINE: By WILLIAM H. HONAN

 BODY:
Kathleen Turner, Alec Baldwin, Eric Bogosian and Chuck Close led a roster of 17 artists and arts administrators who testified yesterday at the Brooklyn Museum against stronger anti-obscenity restrictions on the National Endowment for the Arts.
"Artists cannot create within a prison of vague threats, wondering what is decent, what is acceptable," Ms. Turner told a Congressional subcommittee; the actress expressed a view that was to be repeated by others throughout the day. 
 On Thursday last week, the House of Representatives narrowly supported a measure to drop anti-obscenity language inspired by Senator Jesse Helms, Republican of North Carolina, in exchange for maintaining grazing subsidies for Western ranchers. The measure has become known as "corn for porn."
Mr. Close, the painter, said yesterday that although he had gratefully received a grant from the Federal agency in 1973 and served on a grant-making peer panel in 1982, he would refuse to join a panel today if asked to evaluate applications "on the basis of what Senator Helms considers indecent."
The Senate, which in September voted 68 to 28 to support Senator Helms's measure to prohibit the arts endowment from subsidizing "patently offensive" art exhibits and theatrical presentations, is expected to take final action on the House-Senate conference committee report this week.
The hearing at the Brooklyn Museum was run by Representative Barbara Boxer, Democrat of California, who is the chairwoman of a House subcommittee of the Committe on Government Operations. It will prepare a report on the National Endowment for the Arts.
"The far right has set its sights on American culture," Ms. Boxer said as she opened the four-hour hearing. "The politics of hate and fear must be challenged."
 
McCarthy Is Invoked
 Mr. Baldwin, the actor, said that Senator Helms was "attempting to get a choke hold on artistic expression," much as Sen. Joseph McCarthy did a generation ago.
Mr. Bogosian, the actor and monologuist, brought laughter from an audience of 100 in the museum's Cantor Auditorium when he said, "To put restrictions on art is like endowing a football team but refusing to let them wear helmets or cleats."
He was applauded after saying: "When a Congressman makes a decision about obscenity, the Congressman becomes a collaborator with the artist, and for me to collaborate with Senator Helms is obscene."
Arthur Levitt, the chairman of the Economic Development Corporation of New York City, predicted that the hostility of Congress would be a major factor leading to the demise of many cultural institutions in the country by the end of the century.
Robert T. Buck, the director of the Brooklyn Museum, showed slides from an exhibition called "The Play of the Unmentionable" presented by the museum last year. It included much nudity and political comment, including letters of warning to German artists from Nazi officers in the 1930's.
"Everything in the show," Mr. Buck said, "was drawn from the museum's permanent collection. We wanted this exhibition to demonstrate that museums across this land are filled with works that would not pass muster under the standards now being discussed in Washington."

HEADLINE: Far From Fearsome, Bats Lose Ground To Ignorance And Greed

BYLINE: By JANE E. BRODY

 BODY:
BATMAN'S youthful imitators may forever try to terrorize those who refuse them Halloween treats. But real bats are not the aggressive, dirty or dangerous creatures of lore. They are, in fact, industrious and invaluable assets to people and the planet. And they are succumbing worldwide at a frightening rate to human ignorance, greed and destruction.
In misguided efforts to protect people, crops and livestock, bats are being poisoned, blasted with dynamite, asphyxiated by burning tires or entombed alive by the millions in caves and mines where they seek a day's or a winter's rest. Many others are felled by hunters or die when spelunkers disturb their dwellings. 
 "Bats are disappearing at a faster rate than any other group of vertebrates," said Dr. Merlin Tuttle, a real-life "batman" who has spearheaded international efforts to save the versatile and vital animals.
As scientists learn more about bats, they are discovering how vital they are to hundreds of environmentally and economically important plants and trees, including major features of the tropical rain forest, African savannah and American desert. And the demise of insect-eating bats has permitted a population explosion among insects that pester people and devour crops.
Bats, the only mammals that can fly, account for nearly a quarter of all known species of mammals. But 40 percent of bat species are listed as endangered or threatened. Several of the nearly 1,000 species have become extinct within the last two decades, some disappearing before scientists could determine their precise role in the chain of life.
Yet conservation organizations, conscious of the creature's charmless image, are loath to intervene.
"When I first approached major conservation organizations 10 years ago, they wouldn't touch bats with a 10-foot pole," said the 50-year-old Dr. Tuttle, who since his high school days has been one of the furry animal's staunchest advocates. "They either didn't understand bats or viewed them as so unpopular as not to be worth the effort."
So Dr. Tuttle, then curator of mammals at the Milwaukee Public Museum, took bats literally and figuratively into his own hands. In 1982 he founded Bat Conservation International to sponsor research and educational programs about bats and their vital roles. Four years later, he left his museum job and moved the fledging organization to Austin, Tex., a state that has the most bat species -- 32 of the nation's 42 kinds of bats -- as well as the world's largest remaining bat colony in Bracken Cave in central Texas, where 20 million Mexican free-tail bats congregate.
Since then, Bat Conservation International has far surpassed its founder's dreams. Having arrived in Austin in 1986 with two employees and a few hundred members, it now boasts 17 full-time staff members and 12,500 members from 55 countries. Dr. Tuttle is the subject of a new biography for young people, "Batman: Exploring the World of Bats" by Laurence Pringle (MacMillan, $13.95), and his own book, "America's Neighborhood Bats" (hardcover, $19.95, and paperback, $9.95), is the University of Texas Press's best-selling volume.
A cartoon by W. Miller in the current issue of The New Yorker is further testimony to Dr. Tuttle's mushrooming success. One of a group of bats hanging in a cave says to a roost-mate: "We're starting to get media coverage. Pass it on."
As bats decline drastically throughout the world, scientists are scrambling to understand more about their unique biology, their critical ecological roles and their basic survival needs. At the 21st Annual North American Symposium on Bat Research in Austin this month, some 300 scientists gathered to exchange new findings and suggest ways of rescuing bats from their only serious natural enemy: people.
At one time, bats were vulnerable only during their nightly feeding frenzy and only to predators like owls with limited appetites. Now people attack them during the day in caves, trees and abandoned mines and under bridges and eaves. Bats are especially vulnerable to mass annihilation because most species congregate in huge groups when not eating.
Chased from most of their traditional roosts in caves, they moved into abandoned mines. Now thousands of mines are being sealed to prevent injury to curious people, and other mines are being reactivated or used for other purposes. In Wisconsin, for example, where 95 percent of the state's bats hibernate in old mines, Dr. Tuttle rescued hundreds of thousands of insect-eating bats by staying the hand of a landowner who was going to fumigate them so he could use the shaft to store cheese.
In tropical Central and South America where the three species of blood-sipping vampire bats do an estimated $50 million worth of damage to livestock, ranchers who cannot tell one bat from another have destroyed millions of fruit-eating and insect-eating bats. Vampire bats, which roost in small groups, are far harder to find than the millions of insect-eaters that gather in caves, where they can be smoked out by burning tires.
 
Reapers and Sowers
For Many Plants, Bats Mean Life
 The critically important fruit-eating bats, however, have not fared well at human hands. In Southeast Asia, for example, giant fruit-eating bats known as flying foxes are routinely hunted for food and export, commanding as much as $20 apiece from wealthy islanders who consider them a gourmet treat. These huge bats, weighing as much as a pound and with wingspans of up to six feet, are easy targets for shotguns while they hang by the hundreds from treetops. Dr. Tuttle said that one of the Philippines' flying foxes, first discovered by scientists in the 1960's, was extinct by the 1980's.
Some 250 species of bats pollinate or disperse the seeds of hundreds of trees and shrubs, including those that bear economically important products like bananas, avocados, vanilla beans, dates, peaches, figs, cashews and agaves, the source of tequila. In a single night, one short-tailed fruit bat can disperse as many as 60,000 rain forest seeds.
A recent study by Bat Conservation International documented that more than 300 plant species in the Old World tropics alone rely on bats. These plants are the source of more than 450 commercial products, including medicines, timber, ornamentals, fiber and cordage and dyes and tannins, as well as foods and drinks.
In Africa, fast-declining flying foxes are the only known seed dispersers for the iroko tree, the source of millions of dollars worth of timber annually. The giant baobab tree, an ecologically critical feature of East African savannas, depends on bats to pollinate its flowers, which open only at night. The fruit bats of West Africa disperse nearly all the seeds of "pioneer plants" that start forests growing again on cleared land.
To Donald W. Thomas, an ecologist at the University of Sherbrooke in Quebec, fruit bats are "the keystone species on many tropical islands," where they are the sole pollinators and seed dispersers of long-lived rain forest trees.
In studies in the Ivory Coast, Dr. Thomas showed that when the seeds of tropical fruits pass through the digestive tract of fruit bats, they nearly always germinate, but those seeds that simply fall to the ground from the trees mostly do not. Furthermore, he found by studying bat droppings that the animals dispersed most of the seeds far from the mother tree, which greatly enhanced a seedling's chances of taking hold.
Migrating nectar-eating bats from Mexico were recently shown to be a key to the survival of the giant cactus and agaves that are the distinguishing features of the Sonoran Desert. Dr. Theodore H. Fleming and colleagues from the University of Miami at Coral Gables found that lesser long-nosed bats, a federally listed endangered species, follow "nectar corridors" -- a succession of blooming organ-pipe and saguaro cacti -- as they head north into Arizona in spring and then follow a reverse succession of blooming agaves when they head south to Mexico in late fall.
With each foray into the nectar trough of a desert flower, the long-nosed bat emerges covered with pollen, which is deposited at the bat's next stop. Without these long-nosed bats, which are ideally constructed for their job as pollinators, the giant cactus and agave could die out.
 
Natural Pest Control
Sonar Serves Up Feast of Insects
 Bats that eat insects find their night's meal by emitting high frequency sounds and locating targets by the returning echoes. All six species of bats that live in and around New York City are insect-eating "microbats," some of which consume up to 3,000 insects a night. Although all can see, their sonar tells far more than eyes can about a potential meal on the wing in the dark.
The high-frequency sounds bats emit and the echoes they process give these voracious hunters detailed information about the movement, distance, speed, trajectory, size and shape of possible prey. Bat sonar can detect an object as thin as a human hair and only eight-hundredths of an inch long.
Dr. Nobuo Suga, a biologist at Washington University in St. Louis, studies electrical impulses generated by individual neurons in the auditory center of the pearl-sized brain of the mustached bat. He has determined that by using pulses with different frequencies the animal derives very precise details about a potential meal. He also found that the bats use harmonics to avoid collisions with other bats while hunting in dense packs.
Dr. Tuttle noted that a half million pounds of insects typically succumb each night to large foraging colonies of insectivorous bats. The million or so Mexican free-tail bats that roost under the Congress Avenue Bridge in downtown Austin eat 14 tons of insects a night from March to November, said Dr. Tuttle, who convinced the city not to destroy them but instead to turn the bat colony's nightly emergence into a tourist attraction.
Dr. James H. Fullard, a zoologist at the University of Toronto, noted that before bats evolved more than 60 million years ago, moths and other nocturnal insects owned the night sky, flitting about unmolested by predators. The appearance of bats forced them to evolve a novel anti-bat strategy -- a way of hearing the echolocating calls of hunting bats, in effect a radar detector. Moths with this strategic advantage are 40 percent less likely to be eaten, Dr. Fullard said. When they hear a bat speed up its sonar beam, indicating that it has begun its final approach, the moths launch into a wild acrobatic act, looping up, down and around to evade the less agile bat. If that is not enough, the moths fold their wings and dive to the ground where the bat is hard put to find them.
Other moths emit their own sonar signal, blasting out high-pitched clicks that startle the bat. Still others use an auditory guise to dissuade bats by mimicking a sound produced by a foul-tasting moth.
But some tropical bats are like Stealth fighters, acoustically invisible to moths. They emit echolocation calls "so high-pitched that even the sensitive ears of moths cannot detect them," Dr. Fullard said.
Dr. Paul A. Faure, a biologist at Cornell University, has studied these "whispering" bats, which forage among vegetation rather than in the air. By implanting electrodes into the auditory nerves of the moths, he showed that the insects were deaf to the bats' high-pitched emissions.
Others hunt without making any noise at all. Dr. Z. M. Fusessery of the University of Wyoming reported that pallid bats are silent hunters. The enormous ears and finely tuned auditory systems of these bats are so sensitive they can hear an insect as it walks and detect the wing flutter of a moth about to take off.
Frog-eating bats have evolved yet another strategy, Dr. Tuttle found. They simply tune into the frog's mating call and silently pick off the amorous amphibian.
 
Rescuing Bats
Charting Paths To Survival
 The extraordinary vulnerability of animals that congregate by the millions in a few well-defined locations demands fast action from those trying to spare the woodsman's ax. Kirk W. Navo of the Colorado Division of Wildlife, who reported that "about 2,000 of the mines in Colorado have already been sealed without any input from wildlife experts," said he and his colleagues have recruited and trained dozens of volunteers to survey mine sites for bat life. Armed with bat detectors that pick up the animals' sonar, the volunteers work night shifts to monitor bat activity before the mines are closed. But with more than 20,000 inactive mines in the state, the volunteers are hard put to keep up with closings.
Two years ago, Dr. Tuttle said, one of the largest remaining bat populations in New Jersey, a hibernating hoard of 20,000 little brown bats, was sealed into a rural mine. A member of Bat Conservation International noticed the closing and called headquarters, which got the mine reopened in a few days. The mine and its surroundings have since been made a protected reserve for bats.
"Bat gates," vandal-proof metal grids that allow access to bats but not people, are increasingly being used to protect caves and mines that are major bat roosts and hibernation sites. Dr. Tuttle explained that each time human visitors arouse a hibernating bat, it uses up 10 to 30 days' food supply. More than a few arousals a winter can easily result in an entire colony starving to death before insects reappear in spring.
Dr. Elizabeth D. Pierson, a bat researcher from Berkeley, Calif., reported the successful relocation of a bat colony by the Homestake Mining Company, which was planning to reactivate an area containing a bat-occupied mine. After the colony emerged at night, the mine shaft was sealed and the colony moved into another mine nearby that was protected by a bat gate.
Finally, Tracey Tarlton of Bat Conservation International has been analyzing bridges to uncover design features that make ideal bat roosts. Instead of killing off bridge-inhabiting bat colonies "because bridge workers are afraid of them," she proposed structural features for bridges and overpasses that could provide safe haven for these valuable animals.
