HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
The economy expanded in the third quarter for the first time in a year, the Government reported. But the Commerce Department's chief economist acknowledged that the recovery had started to lose momentum by Labor Day. [Page A1.] Consumer confidence fell sharply this month, the Conference Board said. [D13.] 
 A former senior White House aide said he had terminated a $600,000 contract to represent the former head of Saudi intelligence who is under scrutiny in the B.C.C.I. investigation. [D1.]
The White House threatened to veto any banking measure that contains a compromise by two key Democratic chairmen. [D2.]
Nine Eastern Governors and Washington's Mayor agreed to adopt California's program to require cars that pollute less. [A1.] The E.P.A. proposed rules to cut in half the kind of air pollution that is mainly responsible for acid rain. [A23.]
Automatic dialing is under fire by regulators, and Congress is poised to either ban or severely restrict it. [D1.]
State governments are reducing their services, despite tax increases of $25 billion in the last 15 months. [A16.]
 
Companies
Salomon Brothers announced a $110 million cut in the bonuses it planned to pay employees, largely because it did not believe many of its traders and investment bankers deserved so much. [D1.]
Chrysler agreed to sell its 50 percent stake in Diamond-Star to Mitsubishi for nearly $100 million. [D4.]
Time Warner signed an agreement for Toshiba and C. Itoh to invest $1 billion for a stake in Time Warner Entertainment. [D8.]
USX said its third-quarter earnings from steel dropped 93.8 percent. A rival, LTV, said it had turned a profit. [D1.] HomeFed lost $485.7 million, making a Government takeover more likely. [D2.] Warner-Lambert announced drastic cost-cutting measures. [D4.]
K-III Magazines said Harry A. McQuillen would fill the top posts of the group that acquired eight Murdoch magazines. [D1.] James Silberman is ending his career as president and editor in chief of Summit Books, a Simon & Schuster imprint. [D8.]
Dial announced a proposal to complete its transformation from a bus company to a soap company. [D4.]
 
Markets
Bond prices exploded and long-term rates fell well below 8 percent after the Government reported a decline in inflation. [D1.]
The dollar plunged in heavy worldwide trading on the assumption that interest rates are headed lower. [D18.] Yields on C.D.'s and money market accounts dropped slightly in the last week. [D7.]
Stocks managed to hold onto their gains. The Dow closed up 16.32, at 3,061.94. [D10.] Grain and soybean futures rallied. [D18.]
 
International
The British Prime Minister is not prepared to sign a pledge for a "swift transition" to the final stage of monetary union. [A3.]
The U.S. announced a strict ban on trade with Haiti. [A3.]
 
Business Technology
Technological advances are making gyroscopes smaller, lighter and cheaper, moving them beyond sophisticated military and aerospace instruments and into consumer products. [D9.] American Express plans to purchase two new massively parallel supercomputers from Thinking Machines. [D9.]
 
Today's Columns
A dean of urban planning suggests a transportation policy that exploits opportunities to privatize, asks users to pay their way and subsidizes transit patrons. Peter Passell: Economic Scene. [D2.]
Gambling industry stocks may not be as risky as they are generally regarded. Market Place. [D10.]
Some leading advertisers say they are beginning to see glimmers of light amid the gloom. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D21.]

HEADLINE: Britain and Vietnam to Force Refugees to Return

BYLINE: By WILLIAM E. SCHMIDT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Britain and Vietnam signed an agreement today opening the way for the forcible repatriation of tens of thousands of Vietnamese refugees now being detained in Hong Kong.
The agreement comes one week after Hanoi agreed to accept the return of 222 Vietnamese "doublebackers," people who had come to Hong Kong once before, been paid a cash incentive to return to Vietnam and then re-entered Hong Kong illegally a second time. 
 Officials said the first of those people would be returned to Vietnam sometime next month. They will be flown from Hong Kong on chartered planes under armed guard.
Under the terms of today's accord, the authorities in Hong Kong will begin proceedings immediately to turn back any new illegal immigrants who arrive there from Vietnam. Later, the two Governments say they will extend the plan to deport many of the 63,500 Vietnamese who are confined in detention centers in Hong Kong, and cannot prove they are entitled to asylum as refugees from political persecution.
 
At Odds With U.S.
 The agreement, reached after months of negotiations, seeks to resolve the fate of people whose plight has become one of the more enduring legacies of the Vietnam War. The Vietnamese who ended up in Hong Kong in recent years are among the final wave of the so-called "boat people," the more than one million who fled Vietnam's Communist Government by raft and boat after the war ended in 1975.
But in signing the agreement, London appears to have put itself at odds with the United States, which continues to oppose forced repatriations to Vietnam for any reason. Officials in London said they had consulted closely with Washington during recent weeks, but refused to discuss what, if any, agreements or understanding had been reached prior to signing the agreement with Hanoi.
Earlier this year, President Bush and Prime Minister John Major exchanged letters on the boat people, and the British Foreign Secretary, Douglas Hurd, said earlier this month he was hoping to convince the Bush Administration to acquiese in the matter.
Officials in London have argued that most of the boat people are not fleeing political repression in Vietnam, but rather are economic refugees who left Vietnam because they are seeking a better life in Hong Kong, the British colony that will revert to China in 1997.
Opposition parties in Britain have also said they would oppose any agreement that would require people to be repatriated against their will. George Foulkes, a Member of Parliament and spokesman for Britain's Labor Party, expressed fears that the agreement will mean that Vietnamese will be repatriated "at the point of the gun."
A spokesman for the Foreign Office here refused to say whether the Government was prepared to use force against Vietnamese who do want to return to Vietnam. But in Hong Kong, Alistair Asprey, Secretary of Security for the colony, said the police were authorized to use force if necessary.
"We will attempt to let them return home with dignity," he said. "Whether they do so in the final analysis depends on their behavior."
In an attempt to calm fears among the Vietnamese in Hong Kong's detention camps, the Foreign Office issued a statement today saying that Hanoi had "confirmed" that no illegal immigrant who returns to Vietnam under the terms of the agreement will face persecution. The statement also said that Vietnam promised "to facilitate the monitoring of those who return by United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees, to insure that those guaranteeds are fully respected."
In recent weeks, thousands of Vietnamese inside the camps have staged peaceful demonstrations against the repatriation plan and have threatened hunger strikes, suicides and armed resistance should the Hong Kong authorities try to force them to return to Vietnam against their will.
In 1989, when Britian forcibly returned 51 boat people, most of them women and children, to Vietnam, the action touched off riots in the detention camps and brought sharp protests from the United States.
Under today's agreement, any new arrivals will undergo screening immediately, a process that takes about six weeks, to determine whether they qualify for refugee status. If they do not, they will be returned immediately.
By dealing first with the new arrivals, officials say they can freeze the population already in the camps, a number that has grown by about 20,000 this year alone.
Of the estimated 63,500 Vietnamese currently in the Hong Kong camps, about 5,000 have qualified for official refugee status, meaning they fled Vietnam for political rather than economic reasons, and are eligible for resettlment. The others are awaiting disposition of their requests for asylum.
About 18,000 others have been classified as illegal immigrants rather than political refugees, and are therefore subject to deportation. The status of 40,500 more has yet to be determined, and the disposition of all the cases could take two to three years, officials in Hong Kong say.
A spokesman for the Foreign Office said the details on the deportation of those already in the camps in Hong Kong had not yet been worked out, and discussions were still continuing.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
U.S. Repeats Opposition
 WASHINGTON, Oct. 29 (Special to The New York Times) -- Responding to the British announcement, the United States repeated today that it opposed the use of force to return boat people to Vietnam.
"We oppose forced or mandatory repatriation," the spokeswoman said. "We expect that under this agreement there would be no use of force."
The United States officials were vague about what steps the United States might take if Britain used force to return boat people to Vietnam.
Administration officials said that they understood the problems Britain faced in Hong Kong, with the thousands of boat people who do not qualify for refugee status. But the American officials said that, as far as they knew, President Bush had not given Prime Minister John Major any reason to expect the United States to acquiesce if Britain uses force.

HEADLINE: THE MIDDLE EAST TALKS: Waiters at the Table;
Soviets Serve U.S. Needs for Parley In Hopes of Winning Economic Help

BYLINE: By THOMAS L. FRIEDMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MADRID, Oct. 29

 BODY:
It is too early to say whether the Madrid Middle East peace conference heralds a new relationship between Arabs and Israelis, but it has already highlighted new relationships between Arabs and Arabs, between Israel and the United States and, most important, between the United States and the Soviet Union.
On the United States-Soviet front, it would not be an overstatement to say that the Soviets have literally made themselves an arm of American Middle East diplomacy in recent months, as they did everything possible to facilitate the peace conference to win American good will, which they hope to cash in later for American economic aid. 
 While the Soviets are technically co-sponsors of the conference with Washington, that is in name only. A senior European diplomat recalled today that when he asked the Soviet official supposedly working jointly with the Americans on the conference a question about seating arrangements, the Soviet official just shrugged and said: "You have to ask the Americans. We don't know anything. The Americans are handling everything."
 
A Revealing Whisper
 Indeed, the new Soviet-American relationship in the Middle East was perfectly captured in Jerusalem 11 days ago, when Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d and Soviet Foreign Minister Boris D. Pankin held a news conference to announce that invitations to the peace conference were being sent.
Mr. Baker had written an opening statement about how the United States saw this historic opportunity, and he mentioned the general themes to Mr. Pankin before they took the rostrum. Before Mr. Baker was about to speak, Mr. Pankin leaned over and whispered in his ear, asking if Mr. Baker would tell the journalists that his statement was a "joint Soviet-American statement," as Mr. Pankin had none of his own. Mr. Baker gladly complied.
So it has been from the beginning of this peace conference diplomacy. Washington has written the story line and all the speaking parts, and the Soviets have entered and exited on cue. But what did they actually bring to the game? That is, was Mr. Baker bringing them along just out of habit, or was there really some value added by the Soviets' involvement?
 
Providing Cover
 The Soviets, Baker aides insist, made a definite contribution during the eight months it took to put the peace conference together. Their most important contribution, the officials argue, was not twisting arms of their former patrons; it was providing the Arabs with the symbolic cover that many of them needed to take part in what was essentially a "made in America" diplomatic initiative.
The Palestinians, the Palestine Liberation Organization and the Syrians in particular needed the Soviets to be sitting next to the Americans to justify to themselves and their once-radical constituencies that they were not simply marching to an American tune. Even if that was the reality, the image would have been too hard to swallow for peoples in countries whose own self-identities have so long been nurtured on the notion of resisting American domination and "imperialism" in the Middle East, American officials say. The Arabs did not want to come to an "American" peace conference. They wanted an "international" conference.
"While the Arabs recognized the pre-eminent American role," said a senior American official, "they had an interest in demonstrating that this was not just an American show, because the Americans are perceived in the Arab world as pro-Israeli."
Beyond this symbolic role, the Soviets also wittingly or unwittingly played an important blocking role for Washington. The United States did not want either the United Nations or the European Community involved in its Middle East diplomacy -- particularly the community, which, unlike the Soviets, has real weight. By presenting the diplomacy as a joint Soviet-American initiative, Washington had the best of both worlds: a pliant Soviet partner and a perfect excuse to exclude the United Nations and the European Community.
In addition, the Soviets did use whatever residual influence they still have over their former Middle East clients to pressure the Palestine Liberation Organization leadership in Tunis to make sure that Yasir Arafat did not block Palestinians from the West Bank from participating in the conference, and to encourage the Syrians to take part. The Soviet intervention in this regard may not have been decisive, but given the Soviets' long relationship with the P.L.O. and Syria, American officials believe it was helpful.
"Whether they will be able to provide the same benefits in the future, we will have to wait and see," said the American official. "It will depend on how they operate and who they will decide to be as a nation."
 
Geographical Fact
 Whether there is a single Soviet foreign policy in the coming months or years, or simply Russian, Ukrainian and Georgian foreign policies, the Soviets cannot be written off in the Middle East, American officials insist. The Soviet Union or the individual Soviet republics are simply too geographically close to the Middle East, and have too many Muslims of their own, to be indifferent to events there.
The reshaped Soviet-American relationship has had a profound spillover effect on Israeli-American relations. With the end of the cold war, the Israelis can no longer argue that they are a strategic asset for the United States in the East-West global competition.
It was precisely that role that in the past encouraged Washington, and particularly the Reagan Administration, to overlook Israeli practices in the occupied West Bank and Gaza Strip on the grounds that the situation there was a sideshow compared with the larger geopolitical struggle with Moscow, where Israel was a valuable ally.
What has happened with the end of the cold war is that the East-West conflict is no longer the central barometer of world stability. It has been replaced by smaller regional conflicts.
These regional problems, like the Persian Gulf war, are now viewed as the most immediate threats to world stability. As a result, Israel is now increasingly judged by Washington not by whose side it is on in the East-West conflict, but on the degree to which it is helping to promote regional stability.
The Bush Administration has made such a big fuss over Israeli settlements in the West Bank -- more than any previous administration -- because it views them as an obstacle to regional peace. American officials argue that the Israeli Prime Minister, Yitzhak Shamir, agreed to come to the peace conference precisely because he came to understand that his relationship with Washington, and future American economic help, would henceforth depend in large part on how he is perceived to be behaving in the regional context.
Finally, the collapse of the Soviet Union as a superpower, coupled with Iraq's defeat in the gulf war, has totally recast inter-Arab relations, virtually wiping out the Arab rejection front that always opposed any peace settlement with Israel.
Without Soviet military backing and diplomatic cover, that front -- Syria, Yemen, Libya, Iraq and the radical Palestinian factions -- has simply folded its tent, for now. The pro-American Saudi-Egyptian axis is now predominant. The fact is the only Arab rejection front today is in Iran, which is not even an Arab country.

HEADLINE: Belgian Troops, Defying Mobutu, Stay in Zaire to Assist Foreigners

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: KINSHASA, Zaire, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Belgian troops today defied President Mobutu Sese Seko's order that they leave Zaire immediately, and France and Belgium said their soldiers would stay as long as needed to evacuate foreigners.
Opposition leaders pleaded for the troops to stay. Mobutu loyalists "will massacre the opposition if the Belgian troops leave," said Frederick Mulonjo of the Union of Federalists and Independent Republicans. 
 Belgium said it would continue evacuating its citizens despite President Mobutu's demand. Most French citizens have left Zaire but 2,000 to 4,000 Belgians remain in Zaire, its colony until 1960.
 
Meeting With Opposition
 More than 50,000 people spilled into the streets of Kinshasa today, waving victory signs as soldiers fired into the air, mistakenly celebrating because they thought President Mobutu had agreed to reappoint the opposition leader Etienne Tshisekedi as Prime Minister.
The Sacred Union opposition coalition quickly announced they were wrong, saying President Mobutu had met with Mr. Tshisekedi and other opposition leaders today but there appeared little change in the offing.
This Central African nation of 34 million people is degenerating into chaos, with unpaid soldiers leading civilians in looting and pillaging in the interior. Riots last month in Kinshasa left about 100 people dead.
 
Supported by U.S. for Years
 Zaire's mineral economy is in a free fall after 26 years of President Mobutu's dictatorship.
The Belgian oil company Petrofina stopped its activities in Zaire after military and civilian looters destroyed its plant in southern Mwanza, Belgian radio reported Tuesday. Chevron Oil pulled out on Monday.
For years, Belgium, France and the United States have been staunch Mobutu supporters, seeing him as a bulwark against Communism in the region despite charges of gross corruption and brutal repression.
Their support waned with the collapse of Communism, and all three countries cut aid to Zaire because of human rights violations.
About 950 French and Belgian paratroopers today continued the evacuation, but France said it was "only a question of days" before its 150 paratroopers pull out.
 
Peacekeeping Force Sought
 Belgium said Monday that it would ask the Organization of African Unity to send a peacekeeping force to Zaire. It would replace Belgian and French paratroopers sent last month to safeguard the evacuation of more than 20,000 foreigners after the Sept. 23-24 mutiny.
In Paris, the Foreign Affairs spokesman, Daniel Bernard, said 2,800 French citizens had fled the country, leaving 200, mainly missionaries and French people married to Zairian citizens.
In Nigeria, meanwhile, airport authorities reported the mysterious arrival Monday night of a Boeing 727 identified as a Zairian presidential jet.
An unknown number of people disembarked from the plane and were driven away before security officers rushed to the tarmac of Lagos international airport, according to the airport commandant, Wing Comdr. John Ojikutu. He said the plane had not been confiscated because it was an official presidential jet.

HEADLINE: Despite Billions in Tax Rises, States Slash Services

BYLINE: By MARTIN TOLCHIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Despite tax increases totaling $25 billion in the last 15 months, state governments are reducing their services as they struggle to survive the growing costs of health care and other expenses required by the Federal Government, says a report issued today by the National Governors' Association and the National Association of State Budget Officers.
The states were also battered by a limp economy that has had an effect "much worse than a normal recession," said Raymond C. Scheppach, executive director of the governors' group. 
 Twenty-nine states reduced the 1991 budgets they had previously enacted by a total of $7.5 billion, reducing financing for education and aid to local governments and initiating layoffs and furloughs of state workers, the report said.
"The states are getting whipsawed," Mr. Scheppach said. "On the one hand, we have a slowdown in revenues because of a sluggish economy coupled with the political unacceptability of tax increases. On the other hand we have an explosion in two major spending areas: Medicaid and prison construction."
 
Action in 32 States
 In the 1991 fiscal year, which for most states ended June 30, 32 states acted to address poor budget conditions, the report said.
One was New York, which imposed a wide range of cost-cutting measures, including reducing some programs, layoffs, a hiring freeze, reduced aid to localities, and delays in spending. New Jersey has resorted to across-the-board spending cuts, along with program reductions, layoffs, increased taxes and spending delays, while Connecticut invoked program reductions, layoffs and a hiring freeze, and this year enacted a state income tax.
Although 31 states raised taxes for the 1992 fiscal year, Connecticut, Pennsylvania, California and Texas accounted for almost 75 percent of the $15 billion of the new or higher taxes, the report found. Those increases came after 26 states increased taxes by $10.3 billion in the 1991 fiscal year.
Mr. Scheppach said that because of Federal requirements states now spent an average of nearly 20 percent of their budgets for Medicaid and prison construction, which had eroded their ability "to invest in other key areas such as education, infrastructure and the environment."
 
'Going to Go Broke'
 State payments for Medicaid, the Federal-state program of health care for the poor, increased to an average of 13.6 percent of state budget from 10.2 percent since 1986, according to the report. State spending on prison construction, which frequently is required by the courts in an effort to ease overcrowding, increased by 12.7 percent in the 1990 fiscal year and 11.4 percent in 1991, the report said.
In interviews, several governors underscored the gravity of their fiscal plight. "We'll have to lay off employees this year," said Gov. Howard Dean of Vermont. Gov. George A. Sinner of North Dakota said that since he became Governor in 1985 "we've cut the budget five times."
Speaking of growing health care costs, Gov. Booth Gardner of Washington said, "We're going to go broke at the state level if we don't do something about it."
In the 1989 fiscal year, no state budget was lower than that for the previous year, according to the report, but three were lower in 1990, six fell in 1991 and seven were lower for 1992.
In the 1991 fiscal year, 12 states laid off workers, while seven used furloughs, the report said. A total of 25 states invoked hiring freezes, 18 had travel freezes and 28 cut specific programs.
The governors expect their fiscal problems to outlive the recession. "The bottom line is that 1992 is going to continue to be a very difficult year for states -- perhaps the most difficult in the last decade," Mr. Scheppach said. "There are no easy solutions, and it is likely that many of these problems will not go away with an economic recovery."

HEADLINE: U.S. Will Impose a Trade Ban on Haiti

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Oct. 29

 BODY:
In a sharp increase in pressure for a return to constitutional rule, the United States announced a strict ban on trade today with Haiti, the hemisphere's poorest country. The measure comes nearly a month after soldiers overthrew the nation's first democratically elected President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide.
The trade ban comes after the Organization of American States unanimously approved a resolution this month to impose stringent sanctions as a means of reversing attempts to legitimize the overthrow of Father Aristide, who fled the country at gunpoint Oct. 1. The ban makes exceptions only for narrowly defined humanitarian aid, including some food.
Beginning Nov. 5, according to an executive order signed in Madrid today by President Bush, "all commercial trade" with Haiti, including both imports and exports of goods and services, will be indefinitely suspended. By some estimates, the United States accounts for nearly three-quarters of all Haitian trade, as well as most of its foreign investment.
Since the imposition of limited sanctions against Haiti by the United States this month banning all American transactions with the Haitian Government, diplomats say, Washington has struggled over sending an even stronger message to the provisional leadership here, while not irreparably damaging the fragile economy and further hurting the country's poor.
 
U.S. Urges Repatriation
 A United States statement on the embargo made public here seemed to address these concerns, saying, "The disruptions which these additional sanctions will place on the Haitian economy have been brought upon the Haitian people by those who seized and hold power today."
In anticipation of new hardships and the danger of civil strife, the State Department ordered the repatriation of all "nonessential" personnel from Haiti, and diplomats here stepped up efforts to persuade as many as possible of the 8,500 American citizens registered with the United States Embassy here to leave the country.
Haitian reaction to the embargo was mixed, with people applauding it or denouncing it, depending on their feelings toward Father Aristide. Nearly all, however, expressed trepidation about the effect on the nation, where the annual per capita income hovers at about $360.
"For 187 years we have been used for this kind of action," Jean-Jacques Honorat, Haiti's provisional Prime Minister, said in a telephone interview. "A commercial embargo against a country that produces nothing will be all-out barbarism. We will have to eat each other soon."
 
Legislators Displeased
 Asked if he thought the measure would result in greater accommodation by the Haitian military, Mr. Honorat said, "I don't think this will force any Haitians to their knees."
Haitian legislators who voted to declare Father Aristide's office vacant and appointed an interim Government to replace him expressed displeasure, while allowing that the measures might speed discussions with an O.A.S. mission expected in Haiti on Thursday.
"At least Nov. 5 gives us until the end of the week to do something," said Guy Bauduy, a former Senate leader. "If we came to an agreement with the O.A.S. mission while it was here, the sanctions could be rescinded."
 
Popular With the Poor
 Father Aristide remains very popular with Haiti's poor masses, but is loathed by many in the small educated elite as a threatening figure who, they say, legitimized vigilantism and showed little respect for institutional checks and balances.
In Carrefour-Feuilles, a poor hillside quarter of tin-roofed shacks overlooking the capital's broad bay, residents on Monday spoke with enthusiasm for the sanctions, which have been widely predicted.
"It doesn't matter if we have to go hungry," said one young man as a crowd of Father Aristide's supporters gathered. "If they have to flatten the country, just bring him back.

HEADLINE: U.S. UNVEILS RULES TO LIMIT ACID RAIN

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
The Environmental Protection Agency today proposed regulations to cut in half the kind of air pollution that is mainly responsible for acid rain, while allowing for the first time the widespread buying and selling of pollution rights to cut the costs of compliance.
The proposed rules, required under revisions to the Clean Air Act that passed last year, were welcomed by environmental groups and by industry as a signal that innovation and consultation, rather than obstruction and confrontation, can guide the development of costly new environmental rules. 
 But the electric utility industry, whose coal-fired boilers in the East and Middle West are the primary focus of the regulations, warned that in some areas the costs of compliance would spur double-digit increases in electric rates.
William K. Reilly, the Administrator of the Environmental Protection Agency, said the buying and selling of pollution rights would cut the utilities compliance costs by $1 billion per year, to about $3.8 billion, and that only the most polluting plants would have to seek extraordinary rate increases.
 
'Correcting' Problem
 "For many years the environment has subsidized some very heavily polluting plants," he said. "Now we are correcting that."
The regulations would not begin to take hold until 1995 and the main effect would not be felt until the year 2000. But as the emissions are reduced, Mr. Reilly said, people would be able to see the difference in the clarity of the air in much of the country, especially the eastern half.
Electric companies now have one last chance for public comment before the final rule is issued in May.
The Edison Electric Institute, an association of investor-owned electric companies, said that in general its members supported the Administration's proposals and welcomed the system of buying and selling pollution credits. Buying, selling and trading pollution allowances was one of the central innovations of the 1990 Clean Air Act.
 
Accumulating Credits
 Much of acid rain is produced when sulfur dioxide, a byproduct of coal, oil or natural gas plants, enters the atmosphere. There it is transformed into acidic compounds that drop back to the earth, damaging trees and altering the chemistry of lakes and streams.
Under the proposal released today, a coal-fired utility that does better at cleaning up sulfur dioxide than the regulations require could accumulate credits for sale to another company that failed to clean up enough.
In theory, companies that can clean up pollution cheaply by changing fuel or persuading their customers to conserve energy would recover some of the cost by selling their pollution rights to companies that could only clean up by adopting costlier measures, like filtering the smoke they emit. The air would be just as clean, but the costs would be lower.
In effect, clean air would become a commodity with known production costs, and traded under laws of supply and demand as if a reduction in noxious emissions were a pork belly. Indeed, the futures market at the Chicago Board of Trade plans to trade the pollution allowances.
 
Using Market Forces
 "For the first time, all of these market mechanisms usually associated with profit and pollution will actually be working to clean up the environment," said Daniel J. Dudek, an economist at the Environmental Defense Fund.
The group, along with other environmental and industrial interest groups, helped write the regulations. It is an approach being used more and more to develop regulations by consensus.
A major point of contention remaining in the rules may be the provisions for monitoring emissions at coal-fired boilers. These provisions are considered crucial, since companies will actually be buying and selling the presumed reductions in pollution.
The rules' first phase, taking effect in 1995, will affect 110 utility plants in 21 Eastern and Midwestern states where the problem of acid rain is most severe. The rules will later expand to cover virtually all power plants that burn fossil fuels so as to cut sulfur dioxide emissions by about 10 million tons, or roughly 50 percent.

HEADLINE: Wealthy Couple Repays Alma Mater: $51.4 Million to University of Houston

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA

 BODY:
In a tale of love and money, John J. Moores and Rebecca Baas of Sugar Land, Tex., were high school sweethearts who struggled to make it through the University of Houston in the 1960's and then made it big with their own computer software company.
Now quite wealthy, the Mooreses have given their alma mater a total of $51.4 million this year, the largest donation ever made to a public university in the nation. 
 "If the university had not been in Houston, it is highly doubtful that either one of us would have gotten a degree," said Mr. Moores, the 47-year-old founder and chairman of BMC Software Inc. "We are very grateful and wanted to help the school and help people who are like us, going to school part time."
The Mooreses have made eight donations in 1991 that total $51.4 million. They include $25 million to build a new athletic building and renovate existing sports fields; $11.5 million to the university's College of Optometry, which runs the River Blindness Foundation, and $10.2 million to build a new concert hall for the School of Music. Other, smaller grants went to support superconductivity research, a creative writing fellowship program, library improvements and scholarships.
"When my wife and I were undergraduates, we used to share the thought that someday perhaps we could help one or two students get through college," Mr. Moores said. "We never dreamed we'd be able to do something like this as quickly as we have. But it's fun."
 
Tough Times Still Ahead
 Even with a record donation, the University of Houston, which is state-financed, faces difficult economic times. Tuition at the 33,600-student university is scheduled to increase next September to $20 per credit hour, from $18. Average costs this year for tuition, room, board and fees is $6,400.
Most states cut their higher education budgets this year, forcing many public universities to raise tuition and cut costs. The president of the University of Houston, Dr. Marguerite Ross Barnett, said the Mooreses' donations represent a new trend in higher education, in which state-financed universities are turning increasingly to private support from alumni, corporations and foundations to fill the gaps left by the reduction of state assistance.
"You're going to see more convergence," Dr. Barnett said. Private schools, she said, "are looking to public sources to support scholarships, and the publics will be pursuing more private university strategies to maintain appropriate levels of funding."
Large private contributions once were the sole province of the wealthy and well-established private universities, like Harvard, Yale and others in the Ivy League, whose graduates often went on to successful and lucrative careers. Of the 10 top contributions made to higher education since 1967, all but two have gone to private institutions. Public universities were considered to be well taken care of by public money.
 
Change Over the Years
 But in recent years, as the publicly supported universities improved and state support withered, graduates who have increasingly taken their place at the head of profitable businesses have made substantial donations.
The largest donation to a public university before the Mooreses' was $41 million in 1988 to the University of Texas Southwestern Medical Center.
John Moores and Rebecca Baas met in their American history class in junior year of high school. They married at the end of their freshman year at the University of Houston, where they were part-time commuting students, working and studying at the same time. By the time they graduated in 1970, each with a degree in economics, they had two children.
Mr. Moores went to work for I.B.M., then for the Shell Oil Company, where he noticed that the company's big main-frame computers were sometimes agonizingly slow. He developed programs to speed them up and, in 1978, went to work for himself, developing new programs to increase the speed and efficiency of existing computers. In 1980, with $1,000 in a Houston bank account, Mr. Moores started his company, BMC Software.
It now has 41 different programs and is a $90-million-a-year business with customers like CSX, Pizza Hut and other Fortune 500 corporations.
Mr. Moores said he decided to make the donations now, rather than after he had retired, because he was interested in seeing how the money will be used.
"We always thought it would be kind of fun to watch the buildings go up," he said, "and frankly we intend to do more through the years."

HEADLINE: Florio Tells Voters in '91 to Send Bush a Message for '92

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: NEWARK, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Gov. Jim Florio urged economically battered voters tonight to "send a message" to President Bush by voting against Republicans in the elections next Tuesday for control of the State Legislature.
"If the people who are hurting want to send an advance message to the President, prior to next year, they can vote against Republicans in this year's election," Mr. Florio said in a speech to about 800 Essex County Democrats. "That will send a message." 
 The Governor also broadened his attack on the Republican Party beyond economic concerns, referring at one point to the Republican candidate for governor in Louisiana who was a leader of the Ku Klux Klan. Mr. Florio said that if people "want policies that are divisive and encourage bigotry, so that David Duke can feel at home in their party, they will vote Republican."
 
A Plea to Fight Back
 Although his fiscal policies had been under attack and his name had been used disparagingly in almost every Republican campaign advertisement, Mr. Florio had stayed out of the legislative campaign until about two weeks ago.
Jon Shure, Mr. Florio's spokesman, said the Governor had decided to step into the campaign because he felt that the Republicans were being allowed "a free ride" to attack the Democratic program without anyone fighting back.
Mr. Shure said that in 1990 the Republicans in the Legislature knew about the state's financial problems as quickly as the Democrats did. But the Republicans, he said, "made a political decision to say to the Democrats, 'You provide every vote for the taxes, and we will run against them.' " Democrats now hold majorities of 23 to 17 in the Senate and 43 to 37 in the Assembly.
Governor Florio was not scheduled to address the Essex rally tonight, a traditional pre-election gathering, until an aide called the county party and asked that the Governor be given time to speak.
In a year when most Democratic legislative candidates in New Jersey are running without mentioning their party or taxes, the Governor is now chiding them to "get off their knees." He has urged them to become "javelin throwers instead of javelin catchers" by talking about the benefits that were paid for by the $2.8 billion tax package pushed by his administration and approved last year by the Legislature -- benefits like property tax relief, increased school aid and transportation improvements.
Tonight, in his speech at the Quality Inn in downtown Newark, the Governor changed his imagery, saying: "I'm tired of Democrats being punching bags. I always believed it was better to be a hand-grenade thrower than a hand-grenade catcher."
Mr. Florio said that if people think about it, "they know that the Reagan-Bush economic policies of the last decade are the direct cause of the current national Republican recession that is causing them hardship and eating up their savings."
The recession, he said, "has thrown millions of our people into the unemployment line, depressing the economies of all our states, forcing governors and legislatures across this nation to cut needed services and increase state and local taxes."
 
Of Donkeys and Ostriches
 As he has in the past, Mr. Florio aimed some of his sharpest comments at Democrats who "think that they should try to sound like Republicans." He called that tactic -- one that seems to have been adopted by Democratic candidates across the state -- "wrong in principle and politically stupid."
"Trying to sound like a Republican is not the way for Democrats to help people, especially middle-class people," Mr. Florio said. "Just because the Republicans have changed their symbol from the elephant to the ostrich is no reason why we should ever consider changing ours from the donkey to a chameleon."
Mr. Florio's asking voters to send a message to Mr. Bush is reminiscent of his campaign for governor in 1981, when he asked voters to send a message to President Reagan about their economic worries by voting Democratic. Mr. Florio lost that race to his Republican opponent, Thomas H. Kean, by 1,979 votes, the narrowest margin in state election history.

HEADLINE: House Leaders Say a Compromise Is Now Possible on Jobless Benefits

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
House leaders of both parties moved today to try to make a deal to enact extended unemployment benefits, suggesting that enough political points had been scored and that it was time to get checks to the jobless. But senators and the Bush Administration held back.
Since late July, Democrats and some Republicans have supported programs providing up to 20 weeks of benefits for people out of work more than half a year, to be paid for by increasing the Federal deficit. The Administration has rejected these measures, calling instead for a narrower proposal that would be financed, in part, by selling unused radio frequencies. 
 While the Democrats have used the issue to charge that President Bush does not care about hard-pressed Americans, Republicans have insisted that the dispute shows that the Democrats are heedless of budget difficulties and are more concerned with politics than with the jobless.
Today, at the urging of Representative Robert H. Michel, the House minority leader, Democrats held off action on a third attempt to aid the unemployed, one that would have financed the $5.2 billion measure through additional taxes, though not immediately. Mr. Michel asked Representative Dan Rostenkowski, the Illinois Democrat who is chairman of the House Ways and Means Committee, for a delay to see if a compromise acceptable to the White House could be reached.
 
Time to Compromise
 An aide to Mr. Michel said the idea that it was time to compromise had been discussed briefly with President Bush on Monday at a meeting at the White House and later in the day in a lengthy telephone call between Mr. Michel and Mr. Bush.
But Richard G. Darman, director of the White House Office of Management and Budget, said: "We're not party to any discussions. We're still supporting Senator Dole." Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the Republican leader, has proposed a $3.1 billion measure financed largely through sales of radio frequencies.
Mr. Dole also said there was no evidence of movement. And while SenatorLloyd Bentsen, the Texas Democrat who heads the Finance Committee, said he was interested in making a deal, Senator George J. Mitchell, the majority leader, displayed no interest in compromise.
Mr. Mitchell, a Maine Democrat, said of Mr. Bush, "If he's now prepared to reverse himself on unemployment, we'd be very happy." He said that "the President's position has been adamant and wrong and has resulted in millions of Americans not receiving" benefits they should have received.
Mr. Bentsen said he had some ideas about bridging the gap between the two parties, adding: "I want to get it into the hands of the people who are out of work. We have to reconcile our differences."
Mr. Rostenkowski said he was hopeful that a deal could be struck. "I've got phone calls out," he said. "But they haven't responded."

HEADLINE: Tax-Cut Bill Unlikely In 1991, Speaker Says

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 29

 BODY:
Congress is unlikely to cut taxes this year, the House Speaker, Thomas S. Foley, said today.
"I would personally like to see it this year," Mr. Foley, a Washington Democrat, told reporters. "I want to see it go as far as we can move it this year." 
 But Mr. Foley said Congress planned to adjourn for the year before Thanksgiving Day, and he added that he was not sure a tax-cut bill could make its way through the House and the Senate by that time. In fact, he said, "the possibility of full enactment by Christmas is not great."
Support for a tax cut gained momentum last week after Senator Lloyd Bentsen, Democrat of Texas, proposed a reduction to try to help the economy pull out of recession. Several rival plans surfaced, and the Bush Administration announced it would try to put together a proposal with Republicans in Congress.
But Republican leaders said Monday that President Bush was not eager to rush forward with a plan for fear it could weaken the economy, and Mr. Bush himself indicated he would not propose a tax cut at this time. Some Democrats have expressed similar reservations.

HEADLINE: Ukraine Is Now Getting Serious About Its Drive for Independence

BYLINE: By SERGE SCHMEMANN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: KIEV, U.S.S.R., Oct. 26

 BODY:
Real statehood may still be a distant glimmer and the way to it hazy, but the Ukraine is demonstrating with increasing force that its drive to independence is not a passing whim.
Recently, the Ukraine declined to join eight other republics in signing an economic agreement and refused to send delegates to a new union parliament. A new Ukrainian currency is being printed in Canada, and all candidates for election to a new executive Ukrainian presidency scheduled for Dec. 1 have come out staunchly for independence. 
 Then last week the Ukrainian Parliament passed a flurry of measures further staking out its claims to sovereignty, including authorizations for a 400,000-man army and a central bank.
Western governments that until recently looked at the Ukraine's claims with some skepticism, presuming that in the end the Ukraine will join with Russia and Byelorussia at the core of a new, looser union, are beginning to look more seriously at the independence claims of a republic of 52 million that could become the fourth most populous nation in Europe. If the Ukraine sought the same kind of independence that was won by the three Baltic republics, that would in effect be an end to the Soviet Union.
Political leaders in Kiev acknowledged that many of the measures, most notably the army move, are more symbolic than feasible. They predicted, further, that the Ukraine probably would sign the economic agreement before long, though with several conditions and reservations.
 
Visitors Are Common
 But even those Ukrainian politicians who express exasperation with their Parliament's naive readiness to adopt anything that defies Moscow or plays to Ukrainian nationalism are adamant that full sovereignty is an incontestable goal, which barring some cataclystic change, is likely to win strong approval in a referendum on Dec. 1.
"All the dynamics of Ukrainian politics are toward independence," a Western diplomat said.
The Ukrainian leader, Leonid M. Kravchuk, recently visited the United States, and Western officials now arrive in Kiev with increasing frequency -- and not just at the tail end of a visit to Moscow. Recent visitors from Washington included the Secretaries of Agriculture and the Treasury and a Deputy Secretary of Defense. Foreign Minister Hans-Dietrich Genscher of Germany visited as well.
In the Russian federated republic, too, the idea is reluctantly taking hold that the Ukraine's participation in any new union can no longer be taken for granted. While there are still strong rumbles among Russians that parts of the Ukraine are really Russian, most notably the Crimea and Odessa, the Russian leadership under Boris N. Yeltsin has accepted that Soviet internal borders are best left as is. Further, Mr. Yeltsin has accepted in principle the Ukraine's right to go it alone.
"There is still a Russian psychology that holds that the Ukraine and Byelorussia can never have pretentions to independent statehood," said Les Tanyuk, head of the Ukrainian parliamentary commission on culture and a member of the Rukh nationalist movement.
 
Fighting Revival of Center
 "Some of our Ukrainian leaders, like Fokin and Kravchuk, are still of that old school, always trying to please the Russians," he said. "That's why we have to fight against any revival of the center. That's why we can have an economic agreement with other republics, but never an economic association."
Vitold Fokin, the Prime Minister, was previously head of the Ukrainian branch of Gosplan, the central planning agency, and Mr. Kravchuk was formerly a Communist Party official in charge of ideology.
But a new wave of politicians from the Ukraine, some ethnic Russians among them, are increasingly irritated by any hint of condescension from Moscow or the West. The Russian-dominated central television is accused of treating Ukrainian developments in a patronizing and skeptical tone.
The politicians seem especially irritated by the West's continuing preference for dealing with Mr. Gorbachev.
"People come and tell us, look here, Europe is uniting and you're disuniting," said Viktor I. Antonov, the Ukrainian minister for the defense complex and military conversion. "But for 70 years we lived under the myth of unity. For 70 years Moscow told us, 'Don't meddle, just produce what we tell you to produce.' "
He continued: "There's a huge distrust of Moscow. What we need today is a chance breathe some freedom, to really feel some sovereignty. Then maybe in five years or so we start talking of uniting, or some association."
The idea that the Ukraine will never get a fair break from Moscow unless it establishes its independence runs strong. None of the leaders deny that the Ukraine cannot go it alone economically, given the elaborate links with Russia and other republics and especially its dependence on Russian oil.
But Mr. Antonov argues that a true economic union can come about only when the Ukraine becomes sovereign and puts its relations with other republics on a normal bilateral basis, instead of having its affairs decided in Moscow.
Among issues that must still be negotiated, he said, are the distribution of the property of the former Union, a predominance of which is in Russia, and methods for distributing and financing foreign credits.
But in the Ukraine, as in the Russian Republic and elsewhere around the former Union, the haste to break free of the old empire -- already referred to in official Ukrainian documents as "the former Union" -- is not matched by any concrete idea of how to do it, or exactly what "independence" entails.
 
'Nobody Listens' to Problems
 "When the talk is of creating an army, everyone applauds," Mr. Antonov said. "But when economists speak about the problems of going it alone, nobody listens."
Without a "center" to blame for their woes and against which to unite their forces, the Ukrainians are rediscovering the deep division in their own nation: The western Ukraine, largely Greek Catholic, is a bastion of Ukrainian nationalism and stronghold of the Rukh organization. The eastern Ukraine, including the Don coal basin, is more Russified and has close economic ties to Russia. The south -- the Crimean Peninsula and Odessa -- has only tenuous Ukrainian roots.
Political leaders have also found that the two-year-old Parliament, still heavy with former Communists, is far happier spouting oratory about sovereignty than pushing through real economic or political reforms. Last week, while sidestepping the more tangible issue of privatization, Parliament blithely passed the measure to create an army, not pausing to think what the army would do or who would pay for it.
In the process, some ill-considered comments about control of the nuclear forces based in the Ukraine prompted a flurry of anxiety in the West and in Moscow, compelling Parliament to hastily adopt a reassuring declaration that the goal was to rid the republic of all nuclear weapons.
At the same time, Parliament lambasted Prime Minister Fokin for suggesting that Kiev should sign the economic agreement with other republics, and razzed Vladimir B. Grinev, deputy speaker of the Ukrainian legislature and a respected leader of the political center, when he suggested that it was in the Ukraine's interest to keep the ruble stable.
"Anything that's proposed against the 'center' or for 'sovereignty' is joyously passed," Mr. Grinev said. "The deputies view 'sovereignty' as a glittering star, but they have no idea how to move toward it."
But leaders like Mr. Grinyov are increasingly conscious of the need to shed the image of irresponsible spoilers. He approvingly cited the recent advice of Zbigniew Brzezinski, the foreign-affairs adviser to President Carter, who urged the Ukrainians to adopt their own political program instead of simply resisting programs drafted by other republics.
For all his frustration with Parliament, Mr. Grinev, a Russian from Kharkov who learned to speak Ukrainian only after being elected to Parliament, is as adamant as every other presidential candidate in his opposition to the revival of any new "center."
"No Union structures should survive," he declared. "All should die, including the presidency. Any attempt to reanimate the old center, including this Supreme Soviet, is doomed."
By most tallies, Mr. Kravchuk, who quit the party only recently, is the front-runner in the race, drawing on a respectable record and a conservative populace reassured by the continuity he represents. The champion of Ukrainian nationalists is Vyacheslav M. Chornovil, the candidate from Rukh, with strength in the western Ukraine. Mr. Grinev, who gained respect for his swift and firm stance against the coup in August while Mr. Kravchuk waffled, is considered the candidate of the democratic intelligentsia.
Beyond these distinctions, the race has revealed other outlines of future political discord. Former Communists, for instance, have emerged as some of the most ardent supporters of independence, apparently because they believe that they have a better chance of regrouping in a conservative Ukraine than in a federation dominated by Muscovite reformers.
"With so many political forces, you get totally unexpected results," Mr. Grinev said. "Sometimes it produces diamonds, but sometimes just a swamp."

HEADLINE: 9 STATES IN EAST PLAN STRICT LIMITS ON CAR POLLUTION

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD

 BODY:
The Governors of nine Eastern states and the Mayor of Washington agreed yesterday that they would adopt California's far-reaching program to require cleaner cars in a broad assault against smog in urban America.
Some of the states have already banded together to require less-polluting gasoline, but the move on cars would be the largest joint step against pollution that they have ever taken, proponents say. 
 Most if not all the states can adopt the standards through administrative procedures, experts said, although in some cases the legislatures may take action themselves.
 
Signers of Agreement
 After months of study and debate, the Governors' representatives met in Philadelphia to approve an agreement in which the states pledge to enforce the program, which requires new equipment on gasoline-powered automobiles bought late in this decade, and then the sale of growing numbers of cars that run on alternative fuels like natural gas and methanol. By 1999, 2 percent of the cars sold in areas with the California rules will have to be battery-powered, rising to 5 percent in 2001 and 10 percent in 2003.
Signers of the agreement yesterday are New York, New Jersey, Pennsylvania, Massachusetts, Virginia, the District of Columbia, Maryland, Delaware, New Hampshire and Maine. Connecticut, Rhode Island and Vermont asked for more time to consider the measure. But proponents expect Rhode Island and Vermont to follow soon, and they believe that Connecticut, the only state to express significant reluctance yesterday, will eventually sign on too.
But in Connecticut, officials said they were still studying whether the same benefits could be obtained at lower cost by changes in gasoline formulation, better annual inspection of cars, new equipment in gasoline stations to recapture fumes released in refueling and similar steps.
The states that have pledged to adopt the California standards differ in their progress. New York has completed public hearings and is now in a public comment period, with the Department of Environmental Conservation expecting to issue final regulations soon. In Massachusetts, the Legislature has instructed environmental officials to adopt the standards, and hearings will be held today in Springfield and tomorrow in Boston.
Environmental officials in New Jersey are preparing a regulation and planning to hold hearings in January. In Pennsylvania, Gov. Robert P. Casey directed agencies there to adopt California regulations by the fall of 1995.
Maine plans to issue proposed regulations in January, with public hearings the next month, said Michael Bradley, executive director of the Northeast States for Coordinated Air Use Management, a Boston-based association. Mayor Sharon Pratt Dixon of Washington and the Governors of New Hampshire and Delaware first committed themselves yesterday, he said. Gov. L. Douglas Wilder of Virginia committed himself this week, and the issue may also be taken up by Virginia's General Assembly.
A bill to adopt the California standards was approved by the lower house of the Maryland Legislature last year but did not reach the Senate floor. Gov. William Donald Schaefer favored that bill and and is proposing a new legislative effort.
 
Study in Connecticut
 Mr. Bradley said New Hampshire and Vermont planned to submit the matter to their legislatures. In Connecticut, the General Assembly has already passed a law that instructs the Department of Environmental Protection to provide a study of the costs and benefits, but the same law enables the department to adopt the standard itself, Mr. Bradley said.
Because of the nature of smog, formed over many hours by the interaction of sunlight and pollutants, the quality of the air in any given location is often determined by what is emitted across a state line, rather than what is emitted locally.
The states were also apprehensive about enforcing standards that were substantially stricter than their neighbors, for fear that auto makers would stop selling some models in their borders or raise costs excessively. The agreement by so many states makes it all the more certain that the rules will go into effect.
Proponents say the cost for cars that meet the California standard will be under $200 each, but auto makers have estimated that the cost could be $1,000. In the mid-1990 model years, the California auto standards will not be much stricter than the Federal standard.
Assuming that the three holdouts follow, the California regulations would cover an area where more than one-third of the nation's population lives.
The states are promising to act under authority they were given under the Clean Air Act of 1990. Under that law, California was allowed to write its own rules and the other 49 states can choose between California's standard or the Federal regulations.
The California standard lowers the maximum permissible emissions per mile of hydrocabrons and oxides of nitrogen, two pollutants that react with sunlight to form smog, or, more technically, ozone. California also requires an increasing proportion of "ultra-low emitting vehicles," which would run on something other than gasoline, and then "zero emitting vehicles," or electric cars. These classes of vehicles will require substantial advances in technology.
Yesterday's action represents another in a series of victories by clean-air advocates, who have sidestepped the powerful auto and oil lobbies in Washington and instead slogged through repeated battles in the state capitols.
"It's dramatic," said Thomas C. Jorling, New York's Commissioner of Environmental Conservation, in a telephone interview from Philadelphia, where the group met. "This is the bipartisan states taking the hard decisions necessary to produce clean air."
 
Industry Resistance
 The idea of adopting the California standards has been fought bitterly by auto and oil companies. After the meeting yesterday, Susan Sonnenberg, a planning associate for environmental affairs at Mobil Oil, said, "Obviously, we're disappointed."
"We're not sure it's the most cost effective approach," she said.
The oil companies object mainly because they fear that the next step will be adoption of California fuel standards, which are expected to be much stricter than standards the Federal Government will use for polluted areas.
At the Ford Motor Company, Don Buist, director of automotive emissions and fuel economy, said in a statement, "We aren't convinced that standards established for California's unique air quality problems make sense for other states and regions. In any case, the California standards are not finalized, nor is the technical feasibility determined."

HEADLINE: HOCKEY;
Islanders Push Past the New Guys

BYLINE: By JOE LAPOINTE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: UNIONDALE, L.I., Oct. 29

 BODY:
Millionaires with spare change to throw around might want to look closely at the investment potential of the expansion-level hockey that was displayed tonight at Nassau Coliseum.
The game featured the San Jose Sharks, a first-year team from the end of the earth, against the Islanders, a 20-year-old team that has regressed in recent years to the ways of to a previous life. 
 It would be a cheap shot to accuse both teams of high-stinking this season, but it is true that the newly revamped Islanders entered the match with a six-game streak without a victory and the injury-weakened Sharks had lost nine in a row.
The result, an 8-4 victory for the Islanders, was hardly a work of art, but at least it was mildly entertaining for the 8,969 fans who saw the home team score four unanswered goals in the third period to break a 4-4 tie.
Many of the fans spent much of their time reacting to the score of the Rangers' game flashed on the board over center ice when they weren't cheering the contributions of the six new Islanders. Of the newcomers, five scored and the other contributed two assists.
"They all chipped in," said Coach Al Arbour. "They all got involved."
But the Islanders, who took the second period off, might not have defeated many established teams. The league plans to add two expansion teams next season and four more by the year 2000. If the dilution of talent continues at the current pace, the new teams might be of big-league caliber by the year 3000. By that time, inflation will have made the $50 million entry fee seem like a bargain.
"I don't regard us an expansion team; we're one of 22," said George Kingston, coach of the Sharks. "The players came to the rink believing we could win. We have to keep the spirit. It's important to play with a league mentality. In the final analysis, the rest of the teams don't really give you a lot of assistance to be successful."
 
King on the Move
 Derek King scored twice for the Isles, bringing his season total to nine, the most on the team. Scoring once each were Adam Creighton, Benoit Hogue, Dave McLlwain, Steve Thomas and Pierre Turgeon, all acquired in the trades last Friday with Chicago and Buffalo.
The other newcomer, Uwe Krupp, contributed two assists. New York's David Chyzowski also scored his first goal of the season and displayed some spirited checking. It may have been his best game in parts of three seasons with the team.
Two other apparant Islanders' goals, by Patrick Flatley, were disallowed because they had men in the crease, leading to interference penalties.
The Sharks played without several of their better players, out with injuries: Doug Wilson, Tony Hrkac, Kelly Kisio, Brian Hayward and Jason Moore.
This was the fourth game of a seven-game road trip. The Sharks did bring their popular new uniforms of Pacific teal green with black trim. The style is very popular with fans who wear such things, although one must wonder about a team that uses a broken hockey stick as part of its logo. The stick is in the mouth of the shark, who is biting it on the front of the shirts. The Coliseum organist acknowledged the visitors by playing the theme from the film "Jaws."
The Islanders led by 3-2 after one period and the game was tied at 4-4 after two after the Sharks caught up from a 4-2 deficit.
San Jose's third goal was scored by Wayne Presley, who slapped the puck from the left boards and into the net off the glove of the goaltender.
The Sharks' fourth goal was scored on a rebound by Paul Fenton while Glenn Healy was flat on his back in what appeared to be an exaggerated attempt to force the referee to call an interferance penalty. San Jose's first two goals were scored by Brian Mullen, the former Ranger, and Dean Evason.

HEADLINE: Book Notes

BYLINE: By Esther B. Fein

 BODY:

Battle of the Sexes
Deborah ("You Just Don't Understand") Tannen meets Robert ("Iron John") Bly in a face-to-face, word-to-word confrontation this Friday at 8 P.M., and tickets to the event at Cooper Union's Great Hall have been sold out for weeks. 
 Under ordinary circumstances, a debate between a linguistics professor and a poet would hardly have people stampeding to the box office, but gender anxiety is apparently running deep in the country these days, and the two best-selling authors of treatises on what makes men and women tick differently have become virtual cult heroes. This will be the first time the two writers face off publicly.
The event, sponsored by the New York Open Center at Cooper Union, was advertised only in Cooper Union's lecture series catalogue. But within a week after the catalogue was mailed, every ticket, at $10, had been sold, and thousands of requests continue to flood the office.
Those who can't get in might want to read the books (hers is from William Morrow in hard back and from Ballantine in paperback; his is from Addison-Wesley), or laugh off the whole phenomenon with a new parody by Alfred Gingold satirizing both Mr. Bly's book and "Fire in the Belly," another best-selling examination of men by Sam Keen. Mr. Gingold's book, from St. Martin's Press, is called "Fire in the John."
 
Yeltsin Seeks Bids
 American publishers have received an invitation from Andrew Nurenberg, a London literary agent, to submit bids for a book by his client, Boris N. Yeltsin, the President of the Russian Federated Republic and the hero of the masses who defeated the coup in August. The deadline for submitting a bid is midday today, and in his letter, Mr. Nurenberg indicated that seven figures would not be inappropriate since that is what European publishers have already paid for the book, Mr. Yeltsin's memoirs of the coup.
Executives at several major publishing houses said that given the suggestion of a million-dollar-plus offer, their R.S.V.P. would be "No, thank you." Some said that they would send in low bids, figuring that none of their American colleagues were likely to offer Mr. Yeltsin anywhere near what their European colleagues allegedly paid, and that they might then just walk away with the book for a decent price.
"It's not even to be considered at that level," said Alberto Vitale, chairman, president and chief executive officer at Random House Inc., who said he had been negotiating for the book with Mr. Nurenberg.
Adding to the hesitation about buying the Yeltsin book for such big dollars is the fact that the invitation to bid on it includes a caveat that Mr. Yeltsin will be unavailable to promote the book. "Frankly, I don't expect him to; he has a country to run," Mr. Vitale said. But while understanding his situation, many publishers agreed that Mr. Yeltsin's boisterous personality would be an advantage in trying to sell such a book to an American audience usually reluctant to buy books on foreign affairs.
The high advance Mr. Yeltsin and his agent are seeking is indicative of a trend among Soviet officials who have been shopping their own books about town, asking for what publishers across the board agree are unrealistically high advances.
"If the line forms for sugar on the left, the line forms for memoirs on the right," said Peter Osnos, vice president and associate publisher at Random House Adult Trade and publisher of Times Books, a division of Random House Inc. Mr. Osnos, who is also a former Moscow correspondent for The Washington Post, said it seemed to have become a badge of authenticity for Soviet officials "to prove that they are democrats by having a book contract in America."
The common fee being asked by mid-level, barely known apparatchiks is $100,000, apparently a magical sum that the would-be authors seem to have cottoned to almost uniformly. At the current black market rate, $100,000 fetches five million rubles in Moscow.
 
Greenbacks for Hard Backs
 The Soviet people may be hungry for basics like grain and meat this winter, but they apparently will not be starved for American books. While some Soviet officials were pleading with leaders of the industrialized nations for food aid for the coming months, other Soviet officials were at the Frankfurt Book Fair, cutting deals with American publishers to buy books from them for dollars.
In years past, Soviet publishers would buy the rights to reprint American books for rubles, which are illegal to export and are practically worthless, even in the country. When the authors visit Moscow, the Soviet publishers assured their American counterparts, they will have bank accounts flush with rubles.
This year, for the first time, the Americans insisted on concluding their deals in dollars, and crumbling economy and long lines at home notwithstanding, the Soviet officials complied.
A sampling from the Soviet shopping list: Michael Jackson's "Moonwalk" and Isaac Asimov's "Nemesis" from Doubleday, 15 accounting textbooks from Houghton Mifflin and they are still negotiating for several John Updike books from Alfred A. Knopf.
 
Suddenly Fiction
 It is fairly common for a book that makes it onto the hard-cover best-seller list to land on the paperback list as well. What is virtually unheard of is for a book to jump from nonfiction to fiction.
But on Nov. 10, The New York Times Book Review will shift "The Education of Little Tree" to fiction after its 19 weeks on the nonfiction paperback best-seller list.
The book, originally published in 1976, was purportedly the childhood memoirs of an orphaned American Indian named Forrest Carter. But Mr. Carter, who died in 1979, has been widely accused of being a white supremacist named Asa Carter, who forged his credentials as an American Indian.
The controversy over Mr. Carter's credentials was renewed this year when the book made its way onto the paperback best-seller list. Mr. Carter's widow recently confirmed her husband's true identity, which he had denied.
The University of New Mexico Press, which reissued the book in 1986 as a trade paperback and has sold some 340,000 copies of it this year, has also decided that "The Education of Little Tree" is more literature than autobiography. "We're removing the words 'true story' from the cover," said Elizabeth Hadas, director of the publishing house. "Regardless of what he did as an adult, when we found out that he wasn't really an orphan, we decided it probably was not a good idea to say it was a true story. We're now just saying it's a book."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
It's Official! Yankees Put Showalter at the Helm

BYLINE: By JACK CURRY

 BODY:
Twenty-two days after he was dumped onto baseball's rapidly growing unemployment line, Buck Showalter was hired back yesterday and given a superior position as the new manager of the Yankees.
After the Yankees turned left and right and scratched their heads while searching for someone to replace Stump Merrill, they settled on the man who coached at third base for the last two years and has been affiliated with the organization since he was 21 years old. Excluding the last three weeks, of course. 
 In signing a one-year contract, the 35-year-old Showalter became the youngest manager in the majors and the youngest for the Yankees since 1914. The move left Gene Michael groping for words, for the Yankee general manager had said the next manager would have major league experience. When he was asked if Showalter had been his first choice, Michael chirped, "He is now."
It was quickly apparent why the Yankees' limited partners made a smart move on Oct. 18, when they strongly suggested to Michael that he consider Showalter ahead of retread candidates like Doug Rader and Hal Lanier. At yesterday's news conference at Yankee Stadium, the new manager looked polished while discussing what the club must do to avoid losing 90 games for the third straight season.
 
'Winning Takes Care of a Lot'
 "I have thoughts about what went wrong here," said Showalter. "But you keep getting back to the Al Davis theory. Just win. Winning takes care of a lot."
Showalter appeared to say all the right things and made an uncomfortable situation comfortable for all parties. He even said Michael had done him a favor by letting him go as coach because he could then entertain the calls that came from other teams.
Seattle interviewed Showalter for the Mariners' manager's post, which was filled by hiring Bill Plummer yesterday, and three other teams spoke to Showalter about coaching positions. But 11 days after Michael told Showalter to look elsewhere, the Yankee executives told Michael what to do and started a process that turned Showalter into the 20th Yankee manager since 1973, including the multiple terms of Billy Martin, Lou Piniella, Bob Lemon and Michael himself.
"There wasn't any doubt that this is where I wanted to be," said Showalter, who was accompanied by his 4-year-old daughter, Allie, and his wife, Angela, who is seven months pregnant. "There is no one in this room who feels any better vibes about the organization than me."
Though the Yankees seemed to make the correct choice, problems may still exist. The club did not express much faith in Showalter by giving him a one-year contract worth about $225,000, but the new manager tried turning the touchy topic into a positive.
"I'm on a one-year contract," Showalter said. "That means we're on a one-year plan."
Michael claimed the one-year contract was an organizational policy and did not believe it was an issue, saying that was what the club "gave the last two guys." Both Merrill and Bucky Dent, the last two guys, are no longer around.
 
Conflict and Confusion
 The Yankees remain a team immersed in conflict and confusion. Since Showalter was not Michael's choice, the relationship may be troubling. The limited partners stripped away Michael's first chance to hire a manager and put him under the microscope. How will Michael deal with another manager who was not his selection? He did not defend Merrill, who was also not his hire, when players criticized him last year.
Both the general managing partner, Robert Nederlander, and the chief executive officer, Leonard Kleiman, insisted yesterday Michael was standing on solid footing. Nederlander called Michael, "the best baseball man in the country" and Kleinman said the general manager "had done a wonderful job."
Besides the distractions, the team appears to be stagnating in its crucial off-season plans. Michael has acknowledged that the club needs at least one starting pitcher, a third baseman and another power hitter, however, he did not unveil a plan yesterday. He did not seem to have one yet.
"It's too early to say," said Michael, when asked if the club would try to sign free agent Bobby Bonilla. "We have to sit down and decide who we are going to pursue. We have to figure out our number one and two needs."
Showalter said his first needs include assembling a coaching staff in the next week. Chris Chambliss is a possibility as the first-base coach, either Mark Connor or Billy Connors could return as pitching coach and Marc Hill may be back as the bullpen coach.
The new skipper also plans to call each player a few times during the off season and he said Don Mattingly was one of the first he would contact. The two were teammates in the minors and Showalter said he wants the captain to remain part of the team. Mattingly has expressed doubt about whether he wants to play in New York.
"We want him to be a positive force on this club," Showalter said. "He has been and will be again."
Showalter may conveniently forget to tell Mattingly what he did before flying to New York yesterday. He went out and got a haircut.
