HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
Mideast Tricks or Treaties?

BYLINE: By LESLIE H. GELB

 BODY:
It just doesn't seem possible that President Bush and Secretary of State Baker will wander into the historic Mideast peace conference today without a supersecret game plan. Too much can go wrong too quickly with them left holding the bag of blame. The whole initiative could capsize in six months if Washington simply sat back and waited for the right conditions to ripen.
Mr. Bush and Mr. Baker, whatever they are telling the world to lower expectations, must have a plan. And given their preoccupation with growing Syrian military power, it is likely to be aimed principally at cooling the one conflict that could spark a new Mideast war -- the hatred between Israel and Syria. The plan that fits the pattern of Bush-Baker thinking would call for three small steps to show quick results well short of peace: 
 * A freeze on new Israeli settlements in the West Bank and Gaza Strip, brought about by an enticing collage of carrots and sticks. The goal here is to prove to all early on that the process can work.
* A deal for limited Palestinian autonomy in the occupied territories, with the amount of self-rule to be set by how much Israelis will swallow in one gulp. The more autonomy the better to help local Palestinian leaders build a power base independent of the Palestine Liberation Organization.
* An Israeli-Syrian territorial agreement on the Golan Heights and/or in Lebanon. This makes it easier for the Palestinians to make compromises on autonomy, and clears the way for Damascus to join regional talks on arms and economic development -- areas of great importance to Israel and the U.S.
Mr. Bush and Mr. Baker would not want to show their hand before January or February. Acting too soon would relieve all the parties from coming forward with credible proposals of their own and put the full burden of success or failure on the U.S. But waiting too long has problems too. The window of opportunity cannot stay open beyond the summer, when election campaigns begin in Israel and the U.S.
Mr. Bush has special worries here. If he ends up in a tight Presidential race, the Jewish vote could become very important in big states. So come fall, he must be long finished with whatever Israeli arm-twisting he has to do.
His hope, however, would be to make a freeze on settlements so attractive to Israel that little arm-twisting is necessary. He will not ask Israel to stop expanding existing settlements, just to suspend building new ones. And he will call on Arabs to reciprocate by suspending the trade boycott and the Palestinian uprising.
He would sweeten the pot further for Israel with a monster package of loan guarantees to help Soviet immigrants. That would consist of $10 billion in guarantees from the U.S. and perhaps another $10 billion from the Germans, Japanese and others. Prime Minister Shamir will not find it easy to reject this bundle when he thinks about his own elections.
Nor should Palestinians and Israelis find step two, the autonomy deal, to be beyond their reach. They could agree quickly on transferring a host of governmental functions to the Palestinians. The tricky parts will include deciding who can run in local Palestinian elections, and what the residual policing authority of the Israeli military will be.
Steps one and two mainly help Israelis and Palestinians, the fanatics aside. Step three, defusing Israeli-Syrian tensions, takes care of U.S. strategic interests in reducing the risks of Mideast war and curtailing the proliferation of nuclear and chemical arms and long-range missiles.
Israel will not consider returning the Golan Heights to Syria for many years, and Mr. Bush and Mr. Baker will not ask for the impossible. Instead, the U.S. will look for gestures. One might be to take a sliver of the heights and put it under U.S. control. Another might be to arrange partial withdrawals of Syrian and Israeli forces from Lebanon as local militias are disarmed.
What is most attractive about the likely U.S. plan is that it requires neither political miracles nor serious military risks. Neither President Assad of Syria nor Mr. Shamir has to reverse basic policies. This plan would not trick the parties into dangerous illusions of peace, but convince all but the fanatics that a peace process just might work.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Russian Judges Want Real Justice

BYLINE: By Stephen Breyer; Stephen Breyer is chief judge of the Court of Appeals for the First Circuit.

DATELINE: BOSTON

 BODY:
Twelve days before he called for sweeping economic changes, Boris Yeltsin addressed the first congress of Russian judges in Moscow. At the gathering of 535 judges, the President of the Russian republic declared his desire to restructure its legal system.
The gap between these hopes and reality was painfully clear. As the judges confirmed, they have been seen as little more than low-level servants of the Communist Party. The success of Mr. Yeltsin's programs will require a drastic transformation in judicial temperament and in public perception of the judiciary. 
 The West can help. We can offer experience and expertise in the ways of an independent judiciary. France and Germany have already agreed to provide opportunities for the Russian judiciary to study their legal systems. The U.S., with the help of its bar associations, foundations and law schools, should do the same.
The judges discussed how Russia has been a society without law. The party, the prosecutors and the police, not the judges, controlled the criminal process. The police and prosecutors could arrest suspects and hold them in detention without trial for months, even years. When a case did go to court, the prosecutors had the upper hand.
Armed with the power to appeal and the greater prestige of their jobs, prosecutors could remind judges that judges and prosecutors were on the same side, representing the "state" or the "people." Guilt or innocence sometimes depended on what the judges called the "telephone connection" -- the call from the local party official dictating the case's outcome.
Judges complied largely because they needed the party for food, housing and their children's educations. Paid less than a third the salary of unskilled laborers, the judges had no real independence. As one judge put it, judges cannot be independent "if we have to ask the local bosses for each brick, for each can of paint, for each rusty nail." Another judge said that recent polls showed that the judiciary has the confidence of only 10 percent of the public.
Mr. Yeltsin offered three specific changes that would bring the rule of law back to the Russian state. He called for an independent judiciary that would be an equal third branch of government. The judiciary would be strengthened by immediate salary increases and by lengthy terms of office with safeguards against removal. Mr. Yeltsin had already turned Communist Party buildings in Russia over to the judiciary for use as courts.
Mr. Yeltsin also urged that judges, not prosecutors, take control of the criminal process. Judges would oversee the legality of arrests and exclude illegally obtained evidence from trials. Judges would review the legality of any confinement. Defendants would also gain the right to meaningful representation by counsel, to trial by a Western-style jury and to an appeal of a conviction.
Finally, Mr. Yeltsin proposed the formation of a constitutional court, something like our Supreme Court. The judges, appointed for life, would have the power to hold statutes unconstitutional.
Can these proposals succeed? The Russian Parliament has not yet enacted the reforms. Some are tentative; some are simply nonspecific goals. Many cost money, which is scarce in this time of budget deficits and inflation in Russia.
The reforms may also meet local resistance; in fact, one judge said that when she asked local party officials for use of the party building, they not only refused but locked her out of her old courthouse as well.
The reformers seemed to recognize the obstacles they face. Yet they will make the effort. Explaining why judicial reform is as important as economic reform, Minister of Justice Nikolai Fyodorov said, "If Russia has to choose between a constitution and sturgeon with horseradish, she must choose the constitution. Besides, it will make a very fine wrapping for the sturgeon with horseradish."
We should do our part to aid the Russians in creating a new judiciary. We should bring them here to study. Soon, Russia would have several hundred officials, each with an understanding of a Western system.
A co-equal judicial branch and a system of constitutional review, which lie at the heart of the proposed Russian reforms, are central to our own system of government. For this reason alone, Americans bear a special responsibility to join in the efforts to help create an independent, nonpolitical judiciary in Russia.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Yields on C.D.'s and Bank Funds Off Slightly for Week

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH M. FOWLER

 BODY:
Yields on certificates of deposit and bank money market accounts dropped slightly in the last week, but there are prospects of a much sharper fall in coming weeks, according to The Bank Rate Monitor, a publication that covers interest rates.
"This week's modest decline in savings yields should accelerate quickly as the Federal Reserve moves to ease rates. Negative economic news will force the Federal Reserve to cut its discount rate and the banks to trim their prime rate," Robert K. Heady, publisher of The Bank Rate Monitor, said yesterday. 
 "There already is leeway for a quarter-point cut in the prime rate based on the fact that the banks' cost of funds has dropped by 33 basis points since the prime last came down on Sept. 13." Mr. Heady added. A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point.
He noted that the First Fidelity Bank of New Jersey yesterday lowered its prime from 8 percent to 7.75 percent.
The week's largest cut in yields in money market funds among New York-area banks was at Astoria Federal Savings, which dropped its money market yield 42 basis points to 4.59 percent.
The People's Bank of Bridgeport, Conn., cut the yield on its three-month certificates of deposit by 11 basis points to 5.02 percent, while Midlantic of Edison, N.J., raised the yield on its six-month certificate by 15 basis points to 5.55 percent.
At New York commercial banks, the average yield on money market accounts was cut to 4.64 percent, down 2 basis points, while the average yields on three-month and six-month certificates were 4.83 percent and 4.92 percent, respectively, each off a basis point.
At savings institutions in the New York area, yields on money market accounts were cut to 5.18 percent, down 5 basis points; 5.27 percent for three-month certificates, down 3 basis points, and 5.49 percent for six-month certificates, down 2 basis points.
The Bank Rate Monitor said the average yields on money market accounts at 20 big banks outside the New York area were cut by 4 basis points, to 4.83 percent, and by 3 basis points, to 5.10 percent, on six-month certificates.
By comparison, at the Treasury's weekly bill auction onMonday the three-month bill was auctioned at an average discount rate of 4.99, compared with 5.04 a week earlier.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 31, 1991, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day yesterday about new banking legislation misstated the size of the Treasury credit line now held by the insurance fund that protects bank deposits. It is $5 billion, not $45 billion.

HEADLINE: Pennsylvania Senate Race Tightens

BYLINE: By MICHAEL deCOURCY HINDS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HARRISBURG, Pa., Oct. 29

 BODY:
The Harris Wofford campaign scheduled a tour at a hospital here to promote the Senate candidate's major issue, a call for national health insurance. But so many television crews showed up to quench the state's growing thirst for information about this little-known Democrat that the candidate had to make the tour twice.
Viewers got to see Mr. Wofford, accompanied by his actor friend, Mike Farrell, who played B. J. Hunnicutt on the television series "M*A*S*H." The two chatted with new mothers, and Mr. Wofford said things like, "I hope that when Ashley grows up she'll have national health insurance." 
 He has apparently picked a popular issue, one that could significantly influence voters in their choice for the man best able to serve out the remaining three years of the late Senator John Heinz's term. In a statewide survey released today, 94 percent of the Pennsylvanians polled said that "making sure that everyone is able to receive adequate health care" was an important factor in helping them make that decision. And nationally, Democratic governors and leaders in Congress agreed today to push for a program that would guarantee access to health care.
That survey, conducted by The Pittsburgh Post-Gazette and WTAE-TV of Pittsburgh, spelled trouble for Dick Thornburgh, the 59-year-old Republican candidate, who opposes national health insurance. He dismissed the idea as too expensive and too complicated.
The poll found that Mr. Thornburgh, a popular two-term Pennsylvania Governor who had a troubled tenure as the United States Attorney General, has lost his commanding lead over Mr. Wofford, a 65-year-old former college president and aide to President John F. Kennedy who was appointed as interim Senator in May. Indeed, the poll found that the two candidates are now in a virtual dead heat a week from Election Day.
"Should the trend depicted in the poll continue," the Post-Gazette said today, "this Senate race will be remembered as one of the most startling upsets in recent Pennsylvania politics."
The poll, based on telephone interviews with 738 registered voters around the state from Thursday through Sunday, also showed that Mr. Wofford made sharp gains among almost all demographic groups, including Republicans. The poll had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus four percentage points.
Another poll released today, by KYW-TV in Philadelphia, found that amid rising concern over the economy and health care, Mr. Thornburgh's lead stands at five percentage points in a poll of 810 registered voters with a margin of sampling error of plus or minus four percentage points. The poll was conducted by telephone from Saturday through Monday.
The Thornburgh campaign dismissed the results of the polls as conflicting with their own, which they say shows Mr. Thornburgh with a 12-percentage-point lead. The campaign predicts Mr. Thornburgh will win by 6-point margin. "We're not running scared, absolutely not," said Michelle Davis, Mr. Thornburgh's campaign manager.
This unexpectedly close campaign, the only Senate race in the nation this year, is sounding national themes and setting the stage for national elections next year. Pennsylvania, which has not sent a Democrat to the Senate in 30 years, has become a kind of test market for Democratic and Republican campaign ideas.
And Mr. Wofford, who is running his first race for elected office, is clearly giving the Democrats some good pointers for 1992. He has shown that national health insurance has finally matured as a popular, vote-getting issue after it had apparently died along with the 1988 Presidential aspirations of Gov. Michael S. Dukakis, who ineffectively promoted the issue.
Some political analysts say Mr. Wofford is also demonstrating the vulnerability of President Bush's domestic policies. They say he has severely wounded Mr. Thornburgh by linking him to unpopular Bush Administration policies, like Mr. Bush's veto of legislation to extend unemployment benefits.
 
Handling of S.& L. Crisis
 Mr. Wofford has also attacked the Administration's and Mr. Thornburgh's handling of other issues that touch a raw nerve with voters. The Wofford campaign advertisements, for example, contain blistering condemnations of Mr. Thornburgh's prosecution of the savings and loan scandal and accuse him of a conflict of interest for failing to appoint a special prosecutor to investigate Neil Bush, the President's son, for his role as a director of a failed savings and loan.
Mr. Thornburgh, put on the defensive from the outset, may have spent more time denying allegations than he has spent in defining his campaign, one based on his record as governor. Nor has he been able to present a stirring vision of what he would do as senator. He has talked about bringing jobs to Pennsylvania by developing abandoned industrial sites for commercial uses, for example, but he has waffled on whether he would support a trade agreement with Mexico that is widely considered detrimental to the state's remaining manufacturing jobs.
"Advertising is going to decide this race," said Kathleen Hall Jamieson, a specialist in political communications and dean of the Annenberg School for Communications at the University of Pennsylvania. And Mr. Thornburgh is losing the advertising competition, she said. "If you knew nothing about either candidate except what you see in the ads, you would think that Thornburgh has been tied to everything you didn't like about George Bush and that Wofford was tied to Governor Casey's unpopular tax increase. But Wofford is also running a gutsy, one-issue positive ad campaign on health care insurance, and Thornburgh doesn't have anything that articulates his sense of the future."
David Buffington, a Republican and editor of the politically neutral Pennsylvania Report, a political newsletter, predicted last Friday that Mr. Wofford would win the race.
"The candidates' negative campaigning has done what negative campaigning is supposed to do: produce a large number of undecided voters," he said. "We asked ourselves, 'Which campaign is strategically and logistically better able to capture these undecided voters?' And we're convinced it's the Wofford campaign."
Major media endorsements for Mr. Wofford have been pouring in from Philadelphia, Pittsburgh and Harrisburg, while Mr. Thornburgh has only been able to capture endorsements in smaller cities like Erie, York and Lancaster. The Patriot-News, Harrisburg's only daily newspaper and one that almost always endorses Republicans in statewide races, caused the most stir with its unexpected endorsement of Mr. Wofford.
"It came down to a case of whether you want a candidate who was going to address some problems we have in the country or somebody who will defend the status quo," Herb Field, author of the editorial, said in an interview.
Philadelphia's popular all-news radio station, KYW-AM, left the choice up to its listeners. But Mr. Wofford himself could not have framed the choice more to his liking. "Basically," the station said, "this race boils down to whether you like President Bush's policies for Pennsylvania and the nation, or whether you want a change."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS PEOPLE;
American Tobacco Has New President

BYLINE: By KIM FOLTZ

 BODY:
American Brands Inc. has appointed Donald S. Johnston president and chief operating officer of its American Tobacco Company, which sells such well-known cigarettes as Tareyton, Pall Mall and Carlton.
The title of president had been held by Charles H. Mullen, who continues as the company's chairman and chief executive. Mr. Johnston had been the executive vice president for sales at American Tobacco. 
 The company's parent, American Brands, is a consumer products company that also does business in distilled spirits like Gilbey's gin and vodka, hardware and home-improvement products, office products and insurance.
American Tobacco, based in Stamford, Conn., has annual sales of more than $1.6 billion and is No. 5 in the industry.
Analysts said the company is generally doing well, despite the slump in the domestic tobacco industry. But they said its market share could shrink during the next several years if it does not breathe some life into its venerable brands like Pall Mall.
"American Tobacco's core brands are aimed at older smokers who have been quitting smoking more rapidly than their younger counterparts," said Ronald Morrow, an analyst at Smith Barney. "The challenge is to stimulate growth by attracting consumers in the 25- to 40-year-old segment."
Mr. Morrow said American Tobacco had been successful in the highly competitive low-priced cigarette market with its Bull Durham and Montclair. "The growth this year has been at the lower-priced end," he said. The brands accounted for about 30 percent of the company's unit volume during the last quarter, compared with 17 percent for the same period last year.
Like its competitors, American Tobacco has been plagued by high-profile lawsuits charging that tobacco companies are liable for cancer caused by smoking. The company has one suit pending.
Mr. Johnston, who is 53 years old, joined American Tobacco in 1986 as executive sales director. Before that he worked for 19 years in a variety of executive positions at the Brown & Williamson Tobacco Corporation, which markets Kool and and other cigarettes.
He received a degree in marketing and economics from Northeastern University. He also has an M.B.A. from the University of Southern California.
He lives in Shelton, Conn., with his wife, Betty, and their two sons, Brian and Bruce.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Some Delta Debt Gets Lower Rating

 BODY:
The Standard & Poor's Corporation yesterday downgraded its rating on $2.2 billion of Delta Air Lines senior debt.
While lowering the rating to BBB+ from A-, S.& P. also canceled its credit watch on Delta, which was issued on Aug. 16. The airline's $950 million worth of equipment trust certificates, which cover many of its planes, were also downgraded to A- from A. S.& P. noted that Delta had acquired Pan American World Airways' European routes and shuttle when its own earnings were under pressure because of the weak economy. 
 S.& P. said Delta's rating outlook was stable, however. "Delta reamins one of the strongest U.S. airlines financially, benefiting from a solid competitive position, modern fleet, positive labor relations and a good service reputation," the ratings agency said.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Tumbles as Fed Lets A Key Interest Rate Decline

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar tumbled yesterday after the Federal Reserve allowed a key short-term interest rate to decline.
Gold prices slipped domestically after rising overseas. 
 The dollar fell in early trading amid pessimism about the economy. But it slid further when the Fed let the Federal funds rate, an indicator of the central bank's monetary policy, fall to 5 percent from 5.25 percent.
 
Timing Is Surprise
 Traders interpreted the move as a change in Fed policy. They were surprised, reasoning that the Fed should have waited at least until Friday's release of October employment data before acting to stimulate the economy.
"The move itself wasn't a big surprise, the timing was," said Mike Faust, a currency analyst for MMS International Inc. in New York.
Lower interest rates reduce the value of American investments, thereby squelching demand for the dollar.
Some traders began selling dollars even before the Fed funds rate fell, believing a Fed move was increasingly likely in light of recent economic data.
A Commerce Department report yesterday that new-home sales fell 12.9 percent in September added to the pessimism. That followed release of a Conference Board survey on Tuesday showing consumer confidence plunged to recessionary levels in October.
 
Yen Also Hurt
 The dollar closed down against all major currencies except the Japanese yen, which has been hurt by fears that the new Japanese Government might also lower interest rates.
In Tokyo, the dollar fell 0.80 yen, to a closing 131.05 yen. Later, in London, it was quoted at the same rate. In New York, the dollar closed at 131.05 yen, up from late Tuesday's 130.60.
In London, the pound rose to $1.7425 from $1.7230 late Tuesday. Sterling also climbed in New York, closing at $1.7440, compared with $1.7290 on Tuesday.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late rates Tuesday, included: 1.6705 German marks, down from 1.6860; 1.4645 Swiss francs, down from 1.4760; 5.7050 French francs, down from 5.7565; 1,250.00 Italian lire, down from 1,261.00, and 1.1230 Canadian dollars, down from 1.1234.
Gold for current delivery closed at $359 on the New York Commodity Exchange, down 60 cents from late Tuesday. The Republic National Bank quoted a late bid of $357.60, off $1.30.
Silver was mixed. It slipped in New York, closing down 0.4 cent, at $4.094 on the Commodity Exchange. But silver rose in London to a late bid of $4.11 an ounce from $4.10 late Tuesday.

HEADLINE: Business and the Law;
Holding Directors Liable for Losses

BYLINE: By Barnaby J. Feder

 BODY:
LITIGATION accusing corporate directors of negligence and other misconduct is fairly common in the United States, so it would be unthinkable for someone to accept a director's post if an American company did not carry insurance as protection from lawsuits.
But this is not so in Israel, where the first significant case is unfolding in which damages are being sought from directors for failing to prevent the financial looting of their company. 
 The special twist, though, is that the directors being sued include wealthy foreign investors, among them several prominent Americans. These directors were among the foreign investors recruited in 1977 to supply about two-thirds of the capital to convert a small Jerusalem bank into the commercially oriented North American Bank.
The foreign directors say that they were encouraged by Israeli financial authorities to leave the management of the bank to Israelis and that the suit is a betrayal of their willingness to respond to the country's pleas for financial support.
"The message is that if you lend your name to anything in Israel, you can no longer be sure that some lawyer and court won't come after you with huge liabilities," said Nathan Lewin, a Washington lawyer who is representing Erwin Sussman and the estate of the late Ira Guilden in a countersuit filed in Federal District Court in Manhattan.
The countersuit accuses the Israeli Ministry of Finance, the Bank of Israel and Bank Hapoalim Ltd. of scheming to mislead the foreign directors about the condition of North American Bank during Israel's 1983 banking crisis and with subsequently covering up their actions.North American Bank had six branches in Israel and an office in New York, and was on the verge of becoming Israel's second merchant bank when the Tel Aviv Stock Exchange crashed in 1983. To bail out the banking system, Israel nationalized many banks and paid the equivalent of billions of dollars to shareholders.
But North American was not among those rescued in such a straightforward fashion. Instead, according to the counterclaim, the Bank of Israel gave $10 million to Bank Hapoalim, which then lent the money to North American through Hapoalim's New York branch. The suit charges that the intent was to allow North American's officials to buy enough stock to keep the price up, thus reassuring investors and helping the Bank of Israel avoid the cost of adding North American to its bailout list.
The countersuit contends that the Hapoalim loan simply allowed North American Bank officials who were embezzling the equivalent of tens of millions of dollars to continue to do so. Moreover, the countersuit contends, Israeli authorities were aware of the criminal activity and made no move to stop it or warn stockholders until August 1985, when the Bank of Israel took control of North American Bank's operations. The bank was put into bankruptcy in 1988 and four of its officers have been convicted of crimes.
The Israeli lawsuit, begun in 1989 by Yosef Segev, a lawyer chosen by the bank's receiver, seeks damages of 135 million shekels -- or about $56 million -- from 24 defendants. The foreign directors are being cited for negligence and other defendants for more serious misconduct. But the suit seeks to make each defendant fully liable for all of the damages. Mr. Segev asserts that the directors' names helped lure depositors to the bank and that many of them had financial dealings and investments with the bank.
The trial in Israel was adjourned this summer and is scheduled to resume on Nov. 13. The Israeli defendants in the countersuit are challenging the Federal court's jurisdiction, an issue that will probably not be decided before early next year.
In addition to Mr. Sussman and the Guilden estate, foreign defendants include David Goan of Switzerland, whose family is prominent in Jewish organizations; Joel Herzog, Mr. Goan's brother-in-law and the son of Chaim Herzog, the President of Israel; Joseph Nakash, the founder of the Jordache clothing empire, and Norman Allan, a wealthy investor from Birmingham, Mich.
Mr. Sussman invested nearly $2 million in the bank, which he lost along with almost $1 million more in investments recommended by the bank's officers. He said he and the Guilden estate had spent nearly $2 million on lawyers so far.
Ira Guilden, who died in 1984 at the age of 89, played a central role in recruiting the foreign investors for North American Bank and served as chairman. Paul Guilden, Ira Guilden's son and executor, said no one involved could have interpreted his father's role as chairman as anything other than an honorary one. "He didn't even speak Hebrew," Mr. Guilden said.

HEADLINE: CONSUMER RATES;
Tax-Free Fund Rates Up; Taxables Are Off

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH M. FOWLER

 BODY:
Yields on tax-exempt money market funds tended to move up again slightly for the week that ended yesterday, while yields on taxable funds continued to decline, according to IBC/Donoghue's, which monitors the funds and issues The Money Fund Report.
Walter S. Frank, chief economist for IBC/Donoghue's, reported that total assets of the tax-exempt funds declined a slight $36 million, to $89.5 billion for the week. The average days to maturity of holdings in the tax-exempt portfolios dropped by one day, to 60 days. 
 "There are not enough investors out there," Mr. Frank said. "Yields will have to go higher to induce purchases."
As for the taxable market, he said, "yields have been declining in ancticipation of further easing by the Federal Reserve, and if we do get that, we would expect large flows from institutional investors into money market funds to take advantage of the higher yields they afford relative to open-market securities such as commercial paper and bank products."
The seven-day compound average yield for tax-exempt funds rose to 3.94 percent from 3.83 percent a week earlier, while the seven-day simple yield rose to 3.86 percent from 3.76 percent.
For 30-day yields, the compound yield was 4.02, down 11 basis points from 4.13 percent the previous week; the 30-day simple yield declined to 3.94 from 4.05 a week ago, also down 11 basis points. A basis point is one-hundredth of 1 percent.
The 30-day yields reflected the declining rates of a few weeks ago, while the seven-day rates tended to reflect the large supply of municipal offerings in the market.
Among state-specific tax-exempt funds, the top New York fund once again was the Salomon Brothers New York Municipal Money Market Fund, with a seven-day compound yield of 4.51, compared with 4.40 percent the previous week.
In Connecticut, the leader was the Dreyfus Connecticut Municipal Money Market Fund, with a yield of 4.32, versus the 4.14 percent posted by the previous week's leader.
Calvert Tax Free Reserves, N.J., led the way in New Jersey once again, with a yield of 4.49 percent, up from 4.41 percent a week ago.
The highest-yielding tax-exempt fund among those with assets greater than $100 million continued to be Strong Municipal Money Market with a yield of 5.01 percent, up from 4.92 the previous week.
Assets of taxable funds, Mr. Frank said, went down by $131 million, to $462.2 billion, for the week. But institutional assets went up by $474 million, so that the outflow was totally on the retail side. "This broadly continues the pattern we have had recently," he said.
Average maturities of portfolio holdings were unchanged, at 63 days. The seven-day compound yield was down by 5 basis points, to 5.10, and the seven-day simple yield for the taxable funds dropped by 5 points, to 4.97 from 5.02 percent.
The average compound 30-day yield was 5.16 percent, down from 5.21, or 5 basis points, and the 30-day simple yield was also down 5 basis points to 5.03 percent.
The top-yielding fund among the taxables once again was the Riverside Capital Money Market Fund of Columbus, Ohio, with a seven-day compound yield of 6.49, up from 6.36 percent.
By comparison, bank money market accounts, a main competitor of money funds, dropped 2 basis points, to 4.92 percent from 4.94 percent a week earlier, according to The Bank Rate Monitor's national index.

HEADLINE: Personal Income and Spending Up

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Consumer spending rose nine-tenths of 1 percent in September, helped by an advance of five-tenths of 1 percent in personal income, the Commerce Department said today.
The report followed by a day the release of a new survey showing consumer confidence plunging to recessionary levels in October, which could portend problems for the recovery, since consumers represent two-thirds of the nation's economic activity. 
 The Commerce Department said consumer spending totaled $3.86 trillion at a seasonally adjusted annual rate, up from $3.83 trillion in August. The gain was the largest since a rise of 1.1 percent in March. It also was the fifth consecutive gain.
Personal income was at an annual rate of $4.85 trillion, up from $4.82 trillion a month earlier and it matched an increase of five-tenths of 1 percent in June. Income slipped two-tenths of 1 percent in July and rose four-tenths of 1 percent in August.
The department reported on Tuesday that consumer spending, when adjusted for inflation, had risen at a 3.8 percent annual rate during the third quarter. It was the largest gain since mid-1989 and helped lift the G.N.P. 2.4 percent at an annual rate, the first quarterly gain in a year.
But later in the day, the business-sponsored Conference Board in New York released a survey showing its index of consumer confidence dropped 12.5 points in October to 60.4 -- a level it considers recessionary.
The difference between incomes and spending meant Americans' savings rate edged down to 3.7 percent from 4.1 in August.
A key component of the income category -- wages and salaries -- rose $12.1 billion, a bit slower than the $12.9 billion the previous month.
The spending and income figures were not adjusted for inflation. When adjusted, spending still advanced six-tenths of 1 percent, wiping out a three-tenths of 1 percent loss in August. Disposable incomes rose two-tenths of 1 percent, the same as the previous month.
Spending on durable goods rose $7.7 billion, after falling $10.6 billion in August.
Spending on non-durable goods like food and fuel dipped $100 million after a $200 million drop a month earlier.
Spending on services jumped $25.6 billion, nearly doubling the $13 billion advance the previous month.

HEADLINE: Energy Bill Is Stirring Debate on Wildlife Areas

BYLINE: By KEITH SCHNEIDER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
More than a decade after Congressional passage of a plan to reduce America's dependence on imported oil, the Senate is set to debate another energy proposal with familiar provisions.
At its core, the measure, which is closely modeled after a plan offered earlier this year by the White House, seeks to end the nation's economic vulnerability by clearing the way for the oil industry to drill in sensitive areas like the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge in Alaska and by making it easier for the nuclear industry to build new reactors. 
 To help make such steps politically palatable, the bill includes conservation measures aimed at making appliances and buildings more efficient and requiring some fleets of vehicles to use fuels other than gasoline.
 
Threat of Filibuster
 But the energy bill faces a very difficult path. Democratic leaders and some Republicans said from the start that they would not sponsor the President's energy package; several Democrats said earlier this month that they would stage a filibuster when the version completed by the Senate Energy and Natural Resources Committee reaches the Senate floor, which could be as early as Thursday.
The dispute that has attracted the most attention is drilling in the Arctic refuge, one of the nation's last wilderness regions. National environmental organizations have staked their reputations and political influence in Congress on blocking any exploration there, saying industrialization would harm a landscape that is almost untouched.
The White House, meanwhile, has been directing a public relations campaign. The Energy and Interior Secretaries urged the bill's passage during news conferences; a White House ceremony was held last week and other public gatherings are scheduled this week.
"There is no silver bullet to solving our energy problems," Senator Macolm Wallop, Republican of Wyoming who co-sponsored the energy bill, said at a news conference on Tuesday. "It is inconceivable to me that America will benefit by paying more money into foreign coffers for oil.
Senator Wallop added, "You cannot imagine an energy bill that does not call for some new energy production."
 
Bipartisan Opposition
 Still, several Republicans are not in line. One, Senator William V. Roth Jr. of Delaware, sponsored the 1980 legislation that established the 19-million-acre Arctic refuge. Members of Senator Roth's staff said he opposes any industrial intrusion in the region.
Senator Frank H. Murkowski, Republican of Alaska and an avid supporter of the bill, argues that environmentalists are being "dishonest" in their arguments about the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge. He argues that only 10,000 to 15,000 acres of the refuge would be affected by the drilling. Improved drilling techniques and new environmental technologies will make exploring for oil in the refuge at least as safe as the industrialization of nearby Prudhoe Bay, where wildlife has been unaffected and the fragile tundra largely unharmed, he said. The risks of environmental damage, he added, are far outweighed by the benefits of gaining oil reserves estimated as high as nine billion barrels.
"Environmentalists are using this issue to build their memberships, using hyperbole to cause fear without any real costs to them," Senator Murkowski said in an interview. "It's a win-win situation for them. Even if the refuge is opened, they can say they were rolled by big oil."
 
An Elemental Debate
 But for national environmental groups and their supporters in the Senate the issue is how far Americans are willing to go to gain the oil. They oppose opening the Arctic refuge to exploration and putting a wild ecosystem in danger of permanent harm to sustain a petroleum-based economy that they view as wasteful and polluting.
Just as in the 1970's, when Congress responded to the Arab oil embargo with a succession of laws to eliminate price controls on petroleum products, establish mandatory fuel-efficiency standards for cars and invest billions of dollars in the development of alternative energy sources, the debate this year essentially involves two competing views of American life.
What is missing, though, say leaders on both sides, is any sense of urgency about the problem. Gasoline prices are stable and low. Oil supplies are ample, and it appears they will be for the foreseeable future, according to energy industry analysts. Thus, while the nation's vulnerability to supply cuts remains unchanged, the public's attention span on the issue is short.
The sponsors of the Senate energy package, including its author, Senator J. Bennett Johnston, Democrat of Louisiana, are promoting a plan that asks for virtually no change on the part of the American consumer. Senator Johnston and his supporters contend that increasing supplies and some conservation measures are enough to keep energy prices moderate and supplies adequate.
At a news conference on Tuesday, Senator Johnston acknowledged that his bill would not substantially reduce the nation's reliance on imported oil, which accounts for roughly half of the domestic supply. But, he added,, a new study by the Technology Assessment Office, a Congressional research arm, showed that passage of the bill was the only way to keep the reliance on imported oil from getting worse and the only way to keep the nation's economy growing. More than 60 percent of imported oil is used for transportation, mostly in cars and light trucks.
 
Disagreement Among Opponents
 Opponents of the measure differ strongly on what is needed and have a far more optimistic opinion about the number of changes most Americans are willing to make to gain independence from foreign oil. Citing data from surveys and the Energy Department's own internal memorandums on the issue, opponents of the bill say Americans are eager to accept more efficient cars, more efficient buildings and to pay for more research and development on renewable energy supplies.
The Johnston bill, opponents say, achieves none of these objectives, but will fill the coffers of oil companies while ruining the nation's second-largest wildlife refuge, reward inefficiencies in the nuclear industry, drain the Treasury of billions of dollars and do nothing to change the basic structure of the transportation industry.
"It is a comprehensive energy bill," Senator Paul Wellstone, a Democrat of Minnesota, said on Tuesday. "There's something in it for every oil company."

HEADLINE: Small Stocks in Rally, and Dow Gains 9.84

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO

 BODY:
Stocks closed higher yesterday for the third straight trading session, led by a strong rally in small stocks. Blue-chip issues gained modestly, and the Dow Jones industrial average rose 9.84, to 3,071.78.
The Nasdaq composite index of over-the-counter trading rose to a record of 541.32, up 6.81 for the day. The Russell 2000 index of small issues also reached a high of 183.35, up 1.45. 
 Volume on the New York Stock Exchange was heavy at 200.8 million shares, up from 192.8 million shares on Tuesday. Gaining issues outpaced losers by 977 to 654.
The Big Board composite index was up 0.90, to 216.48, with the American Stock Exchange composite up 2.57, to 384.18.
 
Dow in Narrow Range
 The Dow moved in a narrow 21-point range thoughout the day, from a low of 3,052.55 to a high of 3,074.02.
"It was wild," said Paul J. Kronlokken, a technical analyst for Piper Jaffrey "The Dow must have made three and a half round trips today as it went from top to bottom."
Birinyi Associates, which tracks program trades, said four buy and four sell programs had little impact on the Dow, accounting for only a 0.90-point loss.
Talk of an easing of interest rates by the Federal Reserve fueled buying. A report that September new- home sales were down a large 12.9 percent suggested that the housing industry might need additional help from the Fed in the form of lower rates.
"We're pretty much near the last batch of earnings reports," Mr. Kronlokken said, so the market is beginning to look more to economic data. October employment and purchasing managers reports are scheduled for Friday.
"There has not been any clear leader in this market, with a lot of internal rotation as investors move from one issue to another," Mr. Kronlokken said.
 
Kellogg Shares Drop
 Kellogg shares fell 3, to 102 1/4. Analysts said investors reacted to the low-double-digit growth forecast that the company's chairman-elect, Arnold Langbo, gave security analysts in New York Tuesday.
Dial slipped 1/2 to 43 3/4 yesterday, but was as high as 45. The company announced plans on Tuesday to restructure, including the spinoff of its commercial lending and mortgage insurance businesses, to concentrate on consumer products like its Dial soap.
Stephen A. Smith, an analyst at Bear Stearns, raised his rating for USX-Marathon to a "strong buy" from "hold." The stock of the oil unit of the USX Corporation rose 7/8, to 29 3/4.
USX was also up 1/8, to 29 3/8, on better-than-expected third quarter results, as was Nucor, a steel company, which was up 1/4, to 80 1/4.
Chrysler was up 1 5/8, to 12 3/4. The auto maker reported a loss yesterday for the quarter, but it was less than expected.
Synergen shares soared 8 3/4, to 55 1/4, as the company's stock split went into effect and investors anticipated the release on Nov. 7 of test results of its drug to treat septic shock.
American Brands rose 1 1/8, to 42, as Allan Kaplan, an analyst at Merrill Lynch, upgraded his intermediate rating to "above average," from "neutral." He noted that on Tuesday the company raised its quarterly dividend to 43.75 cents, from 38.5 cents, more than expected.
International Paper was up 1 3/8, to 76 3/4, as Kathryn McAuley, an analyst at Brown Brothers Harriman, repeated a "buy" rating. She cited strong earnings and low debt.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Big Losses At Armco, Bethlehem

BYLINE: By JONATHAN P. HICKS

 BODY:
Two big steel companies -- Bethlehem and Armco -- yesterday reported huge losses for the third quarter, while a third, Wheeling-Pittsburgh, reported a drop in sales and a small profit. The companies attributed the results to falling steel prices, higher costs and sagging demand brought on by the recession.
The Bethlehem Steel Corporation, the nation's second-largest steel producer behind the USX Corporation, said it lost $61 million for the quarter, in contrast to net income of $10 million in the period last year. Armco Inc., which is the nation's fifth-largest steelmaker, said it lost $26.9 million, deeper than the $8.8 million loss in the period last year.
Following are details of the companies' results:
 
Bethlehem
 Bethlehem's sales slipped in the quarter, to $1.12 billion, compared with $1.2 billion in the third quarter of 1990. Walter F. Williams, Bethlehem's chairman and chief executive, said, "Realized prices were lower in all major product lines in the third quarter and in the first nine months of 1991 than in the year-earlier periods, and, on average, were at the severely depressed levels of the early 1980's."
Indeed, steel prices have tumbled in the last year, with steel producers cutting prices to keep market share. As a result, the average price for a ton of hot-rolled sheet steel -- the basic product used in everything from pipes and automobile parts to traffic signs and file cabinets -- has slid on spot markets from $345 at the beginning of last year to about $280 now. And steel buyers have said they could usually get prices that are even slightly lower.
Bethlehem reported an operating loss of $48 million in its basic steel operations, compared with operating income of $27 million for the third quarter of last year. The company said the results were negatively affected by higher labor costs, citing a wage increase that went into effect as part of a pact with the United Steelworkers of America.
In its steel-related operations, which include freight car manufacturing operations and shipbuilding, Bethlehem reported an operating loss of $3 million, compared with $12 million in the year earlier period.
The company, based in Bethlehem, Pa., said it incurred "unusually high operating costs" because of modernization and repair of a coke oven and blast furnace at its mill in Sparrow's Point, Md. The company said it estimated that the work amounted to about $50 million in the third quarter. On the New York Stock Exchange, Bethlehem's shares gained 75 cents, to $15.625.
 
Armco
 Armco said its sales for the quarter dropped to $387.6 million, from $418.1 million in the third quarter of last year. Included in the quarterly loss is an equity loss of $31.9 million from flat-rolled joint venture between Armco, based in Parsippany, N.J., and the Kawasaki Steel Corporation of Japan.
However, Armco said operating income improved to $13.9 million for the quarter, from $3.4 million in the quarter lst year. "This is encouraging," said Robert L. Purdum, Armco's chairman and chief executive. "But continuing softness in the economy concerns us as we look through the first half of next year." Mr. Purdum added that "the near-term outlook for most of Armco's major markets remains weak." Armco's shares lost 12.5 cents, closing at $5.125.
 
Wheeling-Pittsburgh
 The Wheeling-Pittsburgh Steel Corporation, in Wheeling, W. Va., which earlier this year emerged from bankruptcy, reported net income of $392,000, or 2 cents a share. The figure compared with net income of $22.3 million in the comparable period last year. Analysts stressed that the comparisons were not revealing because the company was in bankruptcy. Sales for Wheeling-Pittsburgh, the nation's eighth-largest steel producer, dropped to $244.6 million, from $286.5 million. The company's shares fell 25 cents, to $7.25.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Chrysler's Loss Is Not as Deep as Feared

BYLINE: By DORON P. LEVIN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: DETROIT, Oct. 30

 BODY:
The Chrysler Corporation said today that it lost $82 million in the third quarter, significantly less than analysts had predicted. The results touched off strong buying of Chrysler stock, which rose $1.625 to finish at $12.75 on trading of 3.3 million shares on the New York Stock Exchange.
Chrysler's loss was on revenue of $7.5 billion, compared with a loss of $214 million a year ago on revenue of $6.5 billion. For the nine months the auto maker has lost $892 million on revenue of $21.1 billion, compared with a nine-month profit last year of $37 million on revenue of $23 billion. 
 Like General Motors and Ford, Chrysler has suffered during the recession, and its latest losses again stemmed mostly from lower sales of cars and trucks. But Chrysler has been much weaker financially than G.M. and Ford, so the improved results were claimed as a triumph by the company's management.
 
Projections Proved Wrong
 "In the market we're in today, holding losses to $82 million is an outstanding performance and clearly reflects the efficiencies we've gained by restructuring Chrysler into a lean and highly productive company," said Chrysler's Chairman, Lee A. Iacocca, in a statement.
Auto industry analysts had expected Chrysler to lose about $190 million in the period, which is the weakest because of slow production related to the introduction of new models.
Worldwide factory sales of vehicles to dealers, on which revenue and earnings are based, were 453,349 during the third quarter, compared with 412,723 a year ago.
Harvey Heinbach, an automotive analyst for Merrill Lynch & Company in New York, explained that he had forecast large losses based on the number of vehicles Chrysler produced in the period. But, he said, a greater proportion of them than earlier in the year were sold at retail to individual customers, as opposed to rental fleets, yielding a greater profit. Top Chrysler financial executives briefed automotive analysts this morning in New York.
Moreover, Mr. Heinbach said, Chrysler benefited from increased prices on its 1992 model trucks and mini-vans and from aggressive cost-cutting, which executives say has reduced fixed costs by $3 billion in the last two years. Cash incentives to customers averaged about $1,300 a vehicle in the quarter, about the same as last year.
Chrysler's selling, general and administrative costs for the third period fell to $448 million, compared with $465 million. But executives told financial analysts that the number would rise by $50 million in the fourth quarter to pay for heavier-than-normal advertising outlays.
 
More Losses Expected
 Analysts expect Chrysler to lose money again in the fourth quarter, or possibly to break even. On Tuesday, Chrysler told analysts it was cutting 56,000 cars and trucks from its fourth-quarter production schedule because of weak demand. That will lower the quarterly schedule to 501,000 cars and trucks.
Executives also said that the proportion of low-profit fleet sales would rise in the fourth quarter. Fleet sales constituted 16 percent of total sales in the third quarter, compared with 27 percent in the second quarter.
Wendy Needham, an automotive analyst for Smith Barney in New York, said she predicted a "small fourth-quarter loss."
The Chrysler Financial Corporation, the company's finance subsidiary, earned $81 million in the third quarter, compared with $83 million last year.

HEADLINE: New-Home Sales Post Sharp Drop

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
Despite a decent rise in household incomes last month and the lowest mortgage rates in 14 years, sales of new homes fell sharply in September. Since the housing sector has led the way since January in pulling the nation out of recession, economists see the plunge as strong evidence that Americans' confidence in the sluggish recovery is fading.
Home sales plunged 12.9 percent last month, the biggest drop in two and a half years, to their lowest level since the depths of the recession in January, the Commerce Department reported yesterday. 
 "The picture is really, really bleak," said Michael D. Hobbs, a contractor in New Canaan, Conn. "There was a real flurry of activity a few months ago, but that certainly has not been sustained."
 
People Are Nervous
 Bruce Steinberg, an economist at Merrill Lynch, said, "The fact that people aren't willing to lock themselves into 30 years of mortgage payments shows that they're very nervous about layoffs."
Ronald R. Foell, president of Standard Pacific, a home-building company based in Costa Mesa, Calif., said: "Only those people who really need housing or are very secure in their jobs are buying. Everybody else seems to be staying on the sidelines." Mr. Foell's company is one of the lucky few whose sales actually climbed in September.
After a spate of weak economic data, including Tuesday's gloomy consumer confidence report from the Conference Board, the Federal Reserve apparently eased short-term interest rates once more yesterday -- the 11th time it has done so in two years. The move helped to lift the price of short-term securities, but was not as well received among long-term investors. [Page D16.]
 
One of Economy's Supports
 Unless home-buying snaps back soon, the wobbly economy will lose one of its few supports, said Karen Horn, the chief executive of Bank One of Cleveland. More than one-fourth of the summer rise in the gross national product -- 2.4 percent at an annual rate adjusted for inflation -- was a result of a 21 percent spurt in housing starts.
The latest reports suggest that housing will not help the economy much in the fourth quarter. Builders typically start digging foundations, hiring workers and paying suppliers when sales of lived-in and new houses are rising.
Not only have sales of existing homes been dropping for three months, but housing starts had slipped in August and applications for new building permits have slackened.
"The fourth quarter looks bad," said Alan Lerner, managing director at Bankers Trust, "but we're not likely to see another recession." He expects growth to slow to a 1.5 percent or so annual rate this fall compared with 2.4 percent in the summer. "That will feel like a recession."
Sales of new homes fell from 512,000 in August to 446,000 in September, not far above January's deeply depressed level of 414,000. Adding to the picture of weakness, what was originally reported as a strong 6.7 percent gain in August turned out, after a revision, to have been a modest 1.8 percent rise. That leaves September sales a mere 8 percent higher than at the depths of the recession, in contrast to August sales, which were 24 percent higher.
The housing recovery stumbled everywhere. Sales of new homes fell the most in the West and the Northeast, by 16.6 percent and 12.9 percent respectively. But even the South and Midwest, which had been the most buoyant, experienced double-digit declines. Median sales prices slipped to $120,200, down $600 from the previous month. And the inventory of unsold houses on the market, 295,000, or 8.3 months of supply, ended up the highest since January.
 
Mortgage Rates Are Favorable
 Whatever reasons account for the slump in the housing market, Mr. Lerner said, mortgage rates are not one of them. The rate on 30-year fixed-rate mortgages was 8.92 percnet at the end of the month, down from nearly 10.5 percent two years ago.
The housing slump could also dampen consumer spending. Rising housing sales produce all kinds of other consumer outlays from new wall telephones to wall-to-wall carpeting. Other harbingers of spending like confidence and car sales have also looked discouraging. At best, they suggest that consumer spending will rise a lot more slowly than the 3.8 percent annual rate of the summer quarter.
The only hint of good news was in the September report on personal income and consumption in Septemer, also from the Commerce Department yesterday, which was stronger than expected. Spending on goods and services jumped by nine-tenths of 1 percent.
In fact, the level of consumer spending in September is so far above the average for the third quarter that it implies an increase at a 2 percent annual rate in consumer spending in the fourth quarter, even if consumption remains flat through December. That level would be maintained unless consumption actually declined in the coming months, or the Government revised away the September increase, Mr. Steinberg said.
But income growth is not strong enough to support increases in spending at the September pace. After-tax income has been climbing more slowly than spending, and consumers have squeezed the saving rate down to 3.7 percent, close to a postwar low. "That can't go on forever," Mr. Steinberg said.
Future spending gains require more job growth, one reason that economists from Wall Street to Washington are waiting so anxiously for tomorrow's report on October employment.

HEADLINE: Investors Turn Sights On Short-Term Bonds

BYLINE: By ALISON LEIGH COWAN

 BODY:
Investors are pouring into short-term bond funds and other higher-yielding products, fund managers said this week, looking for higher yields than the 5 percent or less from bank certificates of deposits and money market funds.
"Folks have got the 5 percent blues and are looking for other places to put their money," said William Corneliuson, the vice chairman of the Strong Funds in Milwaukee. 
 The trend is most evident, he said, in short-term bond funds. The funds -- which focus on investing in securities that mature in less than three years -- tend to be less volatile than long-term bond funds, and yet offer better yields than comparable bank C.D.'s or money-market funds.
"It's a way to enhance the yield and do it with a controlled level of risk," Mr. Corneliuson said.
The trend is hard to detect in the latest industry figures, released yesterday by the Investment Company Institute, because the trade association does not break out the short-term funds separately. But many mutual fund sponsors interviewed this week said they had noticed the trend.
The current period is a likely time for the shift to take place, many mutual fund executives said, because the six-month bank certificates of deposit that are bought around tax season and the 12-month certificates that many investors began buying after the October 1987 stock market crash need to be rolled over. "A lot of that money has been continually rolling over, but the rates that investors can earn are so low, they're saying 'I think I'll take a chance and move my money into bonds or the stock market,' " said Michael J. Hines, a vice president for marketing at Fidelity Investments in Boston
According to the institute, bond funds on the whole attracted $7.9 billion of net new capital in September, after adjusting for redemptions and transfers among funds. That was up 13.9 percent over August, when $7 billion flowed into bond funds, and the highest monthly figure since March 1987.
All 11 categories of bond and mutual funds tracked saw net cash flow rise during the month, although the September increases for corporate and municipal bond funds fell short of the August increases.
Equity funds attracted $3.1 billion of net new capital, up from $2.8 billion in August, continuing a rebound that has been apparent since the end of the Persian Gulf war. The one category of equity funds that showed a decline was option-income funds, which sell options against the stocks in their portfolios to generate additional income. That group experienced a net outflow of $19.5 million.
Although the October results will not be in for another month, Mr. Hines of Fidelity, predicted that it would be "the strongest month we've seen all year on a net sales basis."
In his view, investors will move increasingly to longer maturities in search of higher yields. And he said he detects a strong preference to be diversified. He pointed to Fidelity's asset manager fund, which has grown in popularity because it invests in stocks, bonds and money market securities.
"Investors are diversifying," he said. "Government economic policy has got them concerned, and it's making them more conservative. And when they do get conservative, mutual fund investors want to be more diversified than usual."

HEADLINE: Trade Official Assails Europe Over Ecology

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Carla A. Hills, the United States trade representative, today criticized the agriculture policy of the European Community as environmentally destructive.
In an apparent response to growing attacks on American trade policies as ecologically risky, she painted the Bush Administration as sensitive to the environment. 
 While in a meeting with reporters, she echoed American criticism of the community's farm supports as the cause of overproduction and an international trade war of export subsidies. Mrs. Hills cited environmental concerns as reasons for cutting European agricultural subsidies as part of ongoing talks on freeing world trade.
 
'They're Killing the Land'
 "They've broken the bank, and they've got this mountain of overproduction and they're killing the land with these supports that generate production," she said in answer to a question about European agriculture subsidies. "They're mowing down the hedgerows so there's no refuge for small animals, they're using so much fertilizer that they're poisoning the groundwater and they're having this enormous production that is decreasing the world market price."
The Washington office of the European Community declined to comment.
But Jaap Mol, the economics minister at Embassy of the Netherlands in Washington, pointed to the policy paper on trade and environment recently issued by that nation's Minister of Foriegn Trade and circulated to other countries in the European Community. "There is no policy from the United States Administration," he said.
The Administration and most foreign countries have opposed the explicit inclusion of environmental standards in trade agreements. But environmentalists have expressed growing fears that the most polluting industries will locate their factories in countries with lax regulations and export to regions with more pollution controls.
Last spring, a coalition of environmental groups and organized labor posed a serious threat to the Administration's eventually successful drive to keep sole authority to negotiate trade agreements, submitting them to Congress only for final approval.
A much more emotional issue this fall has been a decision by a panel of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade disallowing an American ban on imports of Mexican tuna. The United States banned the tuna imports because Mexican fishing vessels kill more dolphins than American vessels.
One widely distributed poster in Washington shows a monster, labeled "GATT-zilla," clutching a forlorn dolphin in one paw, a can of DDT in the other and crushing the Capitol building under its feet. The GATT's executive council must confirm the panel's report before the United States can be ordered to repeal the ban, and the council postponed consideration of the report at a meeting earlier this month.
 
Reviewing the Issues
 Mrs. Hills said that at her initiative, a GATT working group will begin reviewing environmental issues.
"I do think that these issues are going to intersect more and more with trade during this decade, and that we're going to have to analyze them and come up with a multilateral way of dealing with them," Mrs. Hills said.
But she added that trade sanctions were not always the best way to tackle environmental issues. The Administration is conducting talks on environmental issues.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

THE ECONOMY
New home sales fell a sharp 12.9 percent in September to their lowest level since the depths of the recession in January. The drop, the biggest in two and a half years, came despite the lowest mortgage rates in 14 years. Economists see the housing plunge as evidence that confidence in the sluggish recovery is fading. [D1.]
Consumer spending rose nine-tenths of 1 percent in September, helped by a one-half percent gain in personal incomes. [D5.] 
 Investors are pouring money into short-term bond funds and other higher-yielding products seeking higher yields than those on certificates of deposits and money market funds. [D7.]
The Senate will debate an energy proposal allowing oil drilling in sensitive areas like the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge and making it easier for the nuclear industry to build new reactors. [D8.]
The S.E.C. adopted new rules to help investors understand a complex transaction called a limited partnership roll-up. [D10.]

Citicorp named Robert A. McCormack, who cleaned up the bank's last big loan debacle with third world debt, to work out its domestic commercial real estate problems. [D1].

HEADLINE: Race for Senate Shows Big Split On Health Care

BYLINE: By MICHAEL deCOURCY HINDS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PITTSBURGH, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Two days ago, Harris Wofford toured a Harrisburg hospital to dramatize his call for national health insurance, a stand that has contributed to the Democrat's stunning rise in polls taken for Pennsylvania's Senate race.
Today his Republican opponent, former Gov. Dick Thornburgh, toured a hospital, too, and also talked about the way he would resolve the nation's health care problems. Saying there are no easy answers, he argued for a redoubled effort to contain medical costs through competition and deregulation, and he referred derisively to national health insurance as "an untried, untested, radical and costly" alternative. 
 After Mr. Thornburgh's news conference inside Children's Hospital on Fifth Avenue, Mr. Wofford, the state's interim Senator, held his own across the street. "This morning at this scene Dick Thornburgh perpetrated the crime of fraud," said Mr. Wofford, referring to a prepared statement. "He appeared here in an attempt to portray himself as a supporter of health care, but the reality is that Dick Thornburgh is Public Enemy No. 1 when it comes to national health care."
 
A Surprise Move
 To the surprise of many political analysts, Mr. Thornburgh did not try to defuse the campaign issue by softening his opposition to national health insurance.
"Given the growing popularity of national health insurance," said Randall M. Miller, a political analyst, "I had expected Thornburgh would have made a death bed conversion to support national health insurance, at least in principle." Professor Miller is a historian and political campaign specialist at St. Joseph's University in Philadelphia.
Mr. Miller and other analysts explain the rising interest in health-care insurance by pointing to Pennsylvanians' concern about the state's sluggish economy, job insecurity and fear of losing health benefits along with the job.
The national recession has hurt Pennsylvania, especially because the state lost about 400,000 well-paying manufacturing jobs in the last decade and added about 500,000 jobs in services industries, where pay and benefits are much lower. Unemployment is pushing 7 percent, and 401,000 workers were unemployed in September.
 
Sharpen Distinctions
 Today was the first time in the campaign that Mr. Thornburgh has spoken at length about health care insurance, but he said he was merely fleshing out a position paper that his campaign had released in September. He even suggested that he might not be discussing health care at all if it was not for his opponent. "Because the issue has risen to such prominence because of my opponent's call for a nationalized health care system, I want to sharpen some of the distinctions between" the two positions, he said.
Mr. Thornburgh also downplayed the importance of poll results released on Tuesday, saying that he had twice won the governor's seat by narrow margins, in 1978 and 1982, and that this race would be no different. Those polls indicated that the race was too close to call, with the candidates lost in the statistical shadow cast by the margins of sampling error in the polls.
The Thornburgh campaign dismissed the polls as inaccurate, but even as Mr. Thornburgh spoke this morning, another poll was being released that corroborated the findings of the two earlier polls.
Today's poll, conducted by Millersville University of Pennsylvania in Lancaster and WHTM-TV in Pittsburgh, found that 44 percent favored Mr. Thornburgh and 41 percent favored Mr. Wofford, and that the results fell within the poll's margin of sampling error of plus or minus 4 percentage points. The poll was based on telephone interviews with 549 registered voters across the state over the weekend. The poll also found that, by a 52-to-42 margin, more Pennsylvanians view Mr. Wofford as more qualified to work on health care problems than Mr. Thornburgh.
 
Hospital Tour
 Mr. Thornburgh briefly toured the Children's Hospital here today, making a cursory inspection of a high-tech heart monitor attached to a small boy. Mr. Thornburgh then walked, hand in hand, with his wife, Ginny, through a waiting room, where they stopped to chat with parents.
Mr. Thornburgh was also accompanied by Dr. Louis W. Sullivan, the Secretary of Health and Human Services. At a news conference in the hospital, Dr. Sullivan endorsed Mr. Thornburgh's ideas on making health care more affordable and accessible. "I view it as a very forward looking, practical, workable health plan," he said.
But Mr. Thornburgh's plan, like Mr. Wofford's plan, is little more than a statement of philosophy and goals. A five-page document, entitled "Dick Thornburgh's Plan for Affordable Health Care," recommends many things, such as promoting competition, encouraging preventive medicine, reforming malpractice medical insurance and reducing unnecessary medical services. Mr. Thornburgh gave few details on how to accomplish this agenda, but estimated that it would save as much as $75 billion a year.
He also proposed goals for reforming the Government-subsidized health-care programs, Medicare and Medicaid. He would reduce premiums paid by the elderly with savings reaped by reducing fraud and abuses. He also said he would work to have Pennsylvania's Medicaid program expanded to cover more of the state's working poor. Asked how the financially strapped state would finance such an expansion of service, Mr. Thornburgh replied, "I don't know what the cost would be in Pennsylvania, and it certainly ought to be met out of the record tax increases that the Administration my opponent served in has imposed on Pennsylvanians."
 
'Innovation' Is Taking Place
 Mr. Wofford, who served as state Secretary of Industry and Labor, has said that Gov. Robert P. Casey had already appointed him interim Senator by the time Republicans and Democrats enacted the $3.3 billion tax increase. Mr. Wofford was appointed as interim Senator in May after Senator John Heinz, a Republican, was killed in an airplane crash over suburban Philadelphia.
Mr. Thornburgh described the "the big difference" between his ideas and Mr. Wofford's this way: "He calls for a massive, Federal bureaucracy to run a centrally directed health-care system out of Washington, D.C., and, in effect, micro-manage our entire health-care system. My call is for a partnership that brings together government at Federal level and government at state level, where a lot of the innovation is taking place, including employers, who have stake in the cost of health insurance, the insurers and health care professionals."
Paul Begala, Mr. Wofford's campaign manager, said that Mr. Thornburgh was using the same argument that "his ideological ancestors" had used to oppose other national benefit programs like Medicare and Social Security that benefit the middle class.
"Mr. Thornburgh doesn't understand the issue," said Mr. Begala. "Respected studies show that $67 billion to $242 billion a year could be saved with national health insurance. It will save money, that is why we need it."
Some political analysts said today that Mr. Thornburgh made a wise political decision to address the health care insurance issue, which has emerged as Mr. Wofford's central campaign theme. But they said his response today may be too little, too late and too confusing.
"To the man on the street who is concerned about being financially ruined by an illness in his family, Thornburgh's talk about cleaning up a little fraud, or lessening the bureaucracy sounds a lot like gobbledygook," said G. Terry Madonna, who conducted the Millersville/ WHTM-TV poll and is director of the university's Center for Politics and Public Affairs.
National health care insurance is taking center stage in this Pennsylvania election, the only Senate race in the nation this year, and some experts think the issue could spill over into next year's elections as a major bone of contention between the parties.
