SECTION: Section A; Page 17; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 3499 words

HEADLINE: THE MIDDLE EAST TALKS;
Excerpts From Speeches in Madrid: 'Long and Painful History' of Struggle

 BODY:
Following are excerpts from speeches yesterday by President Bush, President Mikhail S. Gorbachev and the Egyptian Foreign Minister, Amr Moussa, at the opening session of the Middle East peace conference in Madrid. Mr. Bush's speech was transcribed by Reuters. Mr. Gorbachev's remarks were transcribed by The Associated Press as he spoke through an interpreter. The A.P. provided excerpts from Mr. Moussa's speech, which was translated by Cable News Network.
 
PRESIDENT BUSH
 We come to Madrid on a mission of hope -- to begin work on a just, lasting and comprehensive settlement to the conflict in the Middle East. We come here to seek peace for a part of the world that in the long memory of man has known far too much hatred, anguish and war. I can think of no endeavor more worthy, or more necessary. 
 Our objective must be clear and straightforward. It is not simply to end the state of war in the Middle East and replace it with a state of non-belligerency. This is not enough; this would not last. Rather, we seek peace, real peace. And by real peace I mean treaties. Security. Diplomatic relations. Economic relations. Trade. Investment. Cultural exchange. Even tourism.
 What we seek is a Middle East where vast resources are no longer devoted to armaments. A Middle East where young people no longer have to dedicate and, all too often, give their lives to combat. A Middle East no longer victimized by fear and terror. A Middle East where normal men and women lead normal lives.
 Let no one mistake the magnitude of this challenge. The struggle we seek to end has a long and painful history. Every life lost -- every outrage, every act of violence -- is etched deep in the hearts and history of the people of this region. Theirs is a history that weighs heavily against hope. And yet, history need not be man's master.
 I expect that some will say that what I am suggesting is impossible. But think back. Who back in 1945 would have thought that France and Germany, bitter rivals for nearly a century, would become allies in the aftermath of World War II? And who two years ago would have predicted that the Berlin wall would come down? And who in the early 1960's would have believed that the cold war would come to a peaceful end, replaced by cooperation -- exemplified by the fact that the United States and the Soviet Union are here today -- not as rivals, but as partners, as Prime Minister Gonzalez pointed out.
 No, peace in the Middle East need not be a dream. Peace is possible. The Egyptian-Israeli peace treaty is striking proof that former adversaries can make and sustain peace. And moreover, parties in the Middle East have respected agreements, not only in the Sinai, but on the Golan Heights as well.
 The fact that we are all gathered here today for the first time attests to a new potential for peace. Each of us has taken an important step toward real peace by meeting here in Madrid. All the formulas on paper, all the pious declarations in the world won't bring peace if there is no practical mechanism for moving ahead. . . .
Peace will only come as the result of direct negotiations, compromise, give-and-take. Peace cannot be imposed from the outside by the United States or anyone else. While we will continue to do everything possible to help the parties overcome obstacles, peace must come from within.
 
 
Living With Consequences
 We come here to Madrid as realists. We do not expect peace to be negotiated in a day, or a week, or a month, or even a year. It will take time. Indeed, it should take time -- time for parties so long at war to learn to talk to one another, to listen to one another. Time to heal old wounds and build trust. In this quest, time need not be the enemy of progress.
 What we envision is a process of direct negotiations proceeding along two tracks, one between Israel and the Arab states, the other between Israel and the Palestinians. Negotiations are to be conducted on the basis of U.N. Security Council Resolutions 242 and 338.
 The real work will not happen here in the plenary session, but in direct bilateral negotiations. This conference cannot impose a settlement on the participants or veto agreements. And just as important, the conference can only be reconvened with the consent of every participant. Progress is in the hands of the parties who must live with the consequences.
 Soon after the bilateral talks commence, parties will convene as well to organize multilateral negotiations. These will focus on issues that cross national boundaries and are common to the region: arms control, water, refugee concerns, economic development. Progress in these fora is not intended as a substitute for what must be decided in the bilateral talks. To the contrary, progress in the multilateral issues can help create an atmosphere in which longstanding bilateral disputes can more easily be settled. 

 	 	 		. . .	 	For Israel and the Palestinians, a framework already exists for diplomacy. Negotiations will be conducted in phases, beginning with talks on interim self-government arrangements. We aim to reach agreement within one year. And once agreed, interim self-government arrangements will last for five years. beginning the third year, negotiations will commence on permanent status.
 No one can say with any precision what the end result will be. In our view, something must be developed, something acceptable to Israel, the Palestinians and Jordan, that gives the Palestinian people meaningful control over their own lives and fate and provides for the acceptance and security of Israel.
 We can all appreciate that both Israelis and Palestinians are worried about compromise, worried about compromising even the smallest point for fear it becomes a precedent for what really matters. But no one should avoid compromise on interim arrangements for a simple reason: nothing agreed to now will prejudice permanent-status negotiations. . . .
Real peace -- lasting peace -- must be based upon security for all states and peoples, including Israel. For too long the Israeli people have lived in fear, surrounded by an unaccepting Arab world. Now is the ideal moment for the Arab world to demonstrate that attitudes have changed, that the Arab world is willing to live in peace with Israel and make allowances for Israel's reasonable security needs.
 We know that peace must also be based on fairness. In the absence of fairness, there will be no legitimacy, no stability. This applies above all to the Palestinian people, many of whom have known turmoil and frustration above all else. Israel now has an opportunity to demonstrate that it is willing to enter into a new relationship with its Palestinian neighbors: one predicated upon mutual respect and cooperation.
 
 
What Peace Will Bring
 Throughout the Middle East, we seek a stable and enduring settlement. We've not defined what this means. Indeed, I make these points with no map showing where the final borders are to be drawn. Nevertheless, we believe territorial compromise is essential for peace. Boundaries should reflect the quality of both security and political arrangements. The United States is prepared to accept whatever the parties themselves find acceptable. What we seek, as I said on March 6, is a solution that meets the twin tests of fairness and security.
 I know -- I expect we all know -- that these negotiations will not be easy. I know, too, that these negotiations will not be smooth. There will be disagreement and criticism, setbacks -- who knows -- possibly interruption. Negotiation and compromise are always painful. Success will escape us if we focus solely upon what is being given up.
 We must fix our vision on what real peace would bring. Peace, after all, means not just avoiding war and the costs of preparing for it. The Middle East is blessed with great resources: physical, financial and, yes, above all, human. New opportunities are within reach, if we only have the vision to embrace them. . . .
The alternative to peace in the Middle East is a future of violence and waste and tragedy. In any future war lurks the danger of weapons of mass destruction. As we learned in the gulf war, modern arsenals make it possible to attack urban areas, to put the lives of innocent men, women and children at risk, to transform city streets, schools and children's playgrounds into battlefields.
 Today, we can decide to take a different path to the future -- to avoid conflict. I call upon all parties to avoid unilateral acts, be they words or deeds, that would invite retaliation or, worse yet, prejudice or even threaten this process itself. I call upon all parties to consider taking measures that will bolster mutual confidence and trust, steps that signal a sincere commitment to reconciliation. . . .
 And we will call upon our friends and allies in Europe and in Asia to join with us in providing resources so that peace and prosperity go hand in hand.
Outsiders can assist, but in the end, it is up to the peoples and governments of the Middle East to shape the future of the Middle East. It is their opportunity and it is their responsibility to do all that they can to take advantage of this gathering, this historic gathering, and what it symbolizes and what it promises.
 No one should assume that the oppportunity before us to make peace will remain if we fail to seize the moment. Ironically, this is an opportunity born of war -- the destruction of past wars, the fear of future wars. The time has come to put an end to war. The time has come to choose peace. 

 	 	 		. . .	 	We have seen too many generations of children whose haunted eyes show only fear -- too many funerals for their brothers and sisters, the mothers and fathers who died too soon -- too much hatred, too little love. And if we cannot summon the courage to lay down the past for ourselves, let us resolve to do it for the children.
 May God bless and guide the work of this conference, and may this conference set us on the path of peace. Thank you.
 
PRESIDENT GORBACHEV
 I welcome here the presence of the President of the United States of America, the Arab delegations and observers, the Israeli delegation, representatives of the European Community, and of the United Nations Secretary General. The composition of the participants, as well as the nature and objectives of this conference, are eloquent testimony to the fact that we're participants in an event of major importance in new world politics.
The road to this point was strewn with thousands of victims and with devastations and calamities suffered by whole peoples. It was marred by hatred and atrocities, and many were the crossroads on the path that were fraught with danger of global conflagration. This conflict, the longest in the latter half of the 20th century, bears the heavy stamp of the so-called cold war, and it was not until an end was put to that that ending this conflict became a tangible possibility too.

 	 	 		. . .	 	It was the will of history that, without an improvement and then a radical change in Soviet-U.S. relations, we would never have witnessed the profound qualitative changes in the world that now make it possible to speak in terms of an entirely new age, an age of peace in world history. Movement in that direction has begun, and it is only in this context that we can understand the fact that a tangible hope has emerged for an Arab-Israeli settlement.

 	 	 		. . .	 	Today, we have a unique opportunity, and it would be unforgivable to miss this opportunity. Success is in everybody's interests, not only because the rights of the peoples and nations and of the individual are increasingly recognized today as the universal foundation for our world order, but also for another reason of particular urgency and gravity, and that is the fact that the Middle East has become one of the most heavily armed regions in the world, where lethal weapons and nuclear technologies are building up and where other weapons of mass destruction are also to be found.
 
Vast Mideast Potential
 There is justified cause for alarm. The international community is entitled to expect that this conference will come up with decisions that will put this concern to rest. In my view, the conference can only succeed if no one seeks any victory for one side over the other, but all seek a shared victory over a cruel past. I'm speaking of peace, rather than merely a cessation of the state of war, and a durable peace implies the implementation of and respect for the rights of the Palestinian people.
We have restored diplomatic relations with Israel. Now that deep-rooted democratic changes are taking place in our country and in the world and now that a real process toward settling the Middle East crisis is getting under way, the absence of relations with Israel was becoming senseless. We hope and will try to make sure that this will be of benefit to the peoples of our two countries and the entire Arab world. Peace in the Middle and Near East would benefit all. . . . The region has vast potential. Turning to constructive pursuits, it will help not only to resolve the problems of the nations that live there, but would also become an important pillar of support for global international progress and prosperity. We must break the fetters of the past and do away with hostility, militarism, terrorism, hostage-taking, and those actions that turn people into refugees.
Our country, as a participant in the Middle East process and a neighbor that has maintained longstanding and extensive ties with the nations of the region, has a special stake in the success of this conference. . . . The pace we set and the way we tackle the problems that have emerged at the present stage of world development will have a strong bearing on the settlement of conflicts in the Middle East and elsewhere.

 	 	 		. . .	 	The acceleration of historical evolution when based on democracy is truly amazing. The tremendous social energy of the masses of people is being released, and typical of the way this is made manifest is the dramatic growth of national self-awareness and national consolidation, particularly where national feelings were long ignored or suppressed.
This is, in general, a positive process that holds out great promise for the future. It will add greatly to the creative potential of the world community in all its diversity. We now have a far better chance of keeping this process on a civilized course. At the micro-level of world politics, there is a recognition of the complex implications of national aspirations and a willingness to put out the fires of inter-ethnic and international conflicts as witnessed in the Middle East, in Southern Africa, in Cambodia, in Korea, Afghanistan, and Central America.

 	 	 		. . .	 	Of late, the world has been confronted by yet another crisis of tremendous proportions. What I have in mind is my own country. It became inevitable as a result of latent contradictions building up over a long period of time. A great country is going through a great transformation.
It's a painful and arduous process which has brought about personal tragedies and inter-ethnic and regional conflicts. Much in the world depends on how our crisis will be resolved.
Once this crisis is overcome, our union will acquire new qualities and potential as a world power based materially on a market economy as part of the world economy, politically on democracy as part of universal democracy, and intellectually on the new thinking.

 	 	 		. . .	 	This job is for us to do. Our peoples will have to go through a difficult period of transition, but it's important that, under the new conditions created because it was our country that launched the initiative to end confrontation and to join up with the rest of the world, that the world too has not remained indifferent to our great cause . . . restore if room. The world community is becoming increasingly aware that what is happening in the Soviet Union has a larger bearing than any regional conflict on the vital interests of the greater parts of today's world.
 
Peacetime Challenges
 Today we hear not only murmurs of approval and good wishes, we are also beginning to see practical support as well. And this is a very significant sign of the movement toward a new era, a new age. And President Bush's initiative regarding nuclear arms, with our reciprocal initiatives, constitutes a major step and a prescient symbol of this movement.
For almost half a century, nuclear arms used to be the axis upon which world politics turned. The task is now by changing the nature of those politics to replace it with something completely new and more attuned with the wheels of 21st-century history. Mankind faces many peacetime challenges All of them are formidable ones. And they include all what we call global problems: the environment, energy, food supplies, population, all the problems of development, and the total abolition of the nuclear threat, which is spreading to the edges from the former center of world confrontation.
All these challenges can only be met through joint efforts. Therefore it is essential to fracture the logic of so many millennia, and particularly the logic of this century, persistently and patiently, to shape a new logic of interdependence, interaction and cooperation.
But this task is an extremely difficult one. We see both in our country and elsewhere, even here at this conference, ghosts of the old thinking, sometimes unnoticed, are still present among us. When we rid ourselves of their presence we will be better able to move toward a new world order. . . . And in respect of our individual vision of this new order, it appears that we should now promote this objective process itself, relying on the relevant mechanisms of the United Nations, the C.S.C.E., the European Community's modernized, old, and completely new structures of regional security and cooperation, as well as the institutions created for crisis prevention and conflict settlements.
Ladies and gentlemen, it is for the delegations directly participating in the conference to sort out the details of this enormous task. As co-chairman of the conference, we will be in regular contact with our American counterparts, and we will do our utmost to find solutions for which your peoples in the entire world have long been yearning.
In my address I've mentioned the more general problems of the global process. I did this in order to emphasize once again the important international context of this conference and of the challenge which you are facing. I wish you every success.
 
MR. MOUSSA
 . . . Arab rights to Arab territories cannot be compromised. Recognition of the legitimate rights of the Palestinian people is the prime assurance for peaceful coexistence between Israelis, Palestinians and, indeed, the Arabs in their respective homelands.
Arabs did not come to relinquish their rights . . . They came to search in good faith, with mutual trust, a common ground. . . . Firstly, the legal status of the Palestinian people should not be challenged. They are not mere inhabitants, residents of conquered territories. They are people with a history, a culture, a distinct national identity on an equal footing with other peoples in the world.
Secondly, the West Bank, Gaza and the Golan Heights are occupied Arab territories . . . They have their legitimate owners, and claims not based on principles of legitimacy and international law have no place in the world of today.
Thirdly, settlements established in territories occupied since '67, including Jerusalem, are illegal, and more settlements will foreclose potential progress toward real peace and cast doubt on the credibility of the process itself. Settlements have to be halted for they obstruct peace, undermine the groundwork for negotiations on the final status of the occupied territories, and erode the will to coexist.
Fourthly, the holy city of Jerusalem has its special status. It should remain free, accessible and sacred for followers of Islam, Christianity and Judaism. The occupying power should not exercise monopoly or illegal sovereignty over this holy city. It should not persist in unilateral decisions declared to annex the holy city as this lacks validity or legitimacy. The status of the holy city should be subject to negotiation and settled by agreeing to the context of legitimacy established by internationally accepted resolutions . . .
This is a moment of truth, commitment and hope. We've opted for peace, true peace. The path of peace is thorny. The march is most tiring and arduous, and the challenge is colossal and immense, but the objective is lofty, noble and worthy of our pilgrimage to peace.

PUB-DATE: October 31, 1991

SECTION: Section A; Page 5; Column 1; Foreign Desk

LENGTH: 714 words

HEADLINE: JOBS ARE AT RISK IN HAITI SANCTIONS

BYLINE: By HOWARD W. FRENCH, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PORT-AU-PRINCE, Haiti, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Amid the drone of assembly lines on vast shop floors and the purring, air-conditioned cool in back-room offices, the impending effects of a stringent United States economic embargo imposed on Haiti on Tuesday seemed present on every face in this city's sprawling industrial zone.
More than 20 years ago, Haiti began developing light-assembly industries in the region, in which textiles and simple electronic components are sent from the United States for assembly and then shipped back. 
 Now, many of the owners and the employees of the 160 or so companies in this field fear that the sanctions will strangle Haiti's fragile export industries. The sanctions were imposed in an effort to help restore constitutional order after the military overthrow of the country's first democratically elected President.
In the month since the ousted President, the Rev. Jean-Bertrand Aristide, was forced into exile in Venezuela, Haitian industrialists say that more than 1,200 of the assembly industry's 32,000 or so jobs have been lost as companies like GTE and piece-work concerns have left in the face of the political turmoil and economic uncertainty brought on by the coup.
 
Workers Back Sanctions
 Investors and economists say that at least 200,000 people live directly from the income of workers in the assembly industry, and that countless more jobs in the hemisphere's poorest country depend upon them indirectly. With exports of about $300 million, the assembly industries provide as much as 40 percent of Haiti's foreign exchange earnings.
But even if the sanctions cost them their jobs, many workers, whose wages typically average about $5 a day, say they continue to support moves for the return of Father Aristide, including the sanctions.
"We have been living under an embargo all our lives," said one woman who sold food outside a factory, echoing popular attitudes toward the embargo. "We are willing to suffer more to bring back Aristide."
Business executives fear the worse.
"This could be the death of the economy of Haiti," said Jean Baker, a children's clothing manufacturer who employs 1,000 people. "We are facing an economic catastrophe with major social and economic repercussions and time is against us."
 
Embargo Effective Nov. 5
 Asked how soon the embargo would force him to begin laying off workers, Mr. Baker said, "We have to start as of today."
The sanctions bar American imports and exports of all goods from Haiti, with the exception of selected foods and medicines, which were exempted for humanitarian reasons. Although the sanctions take effect Nov. 5, the executive order that decreed them gives Haitian exporters like Mr. Baker 30 days to ship goods that are already in production.
Mr. Baker urged rapid negotiations with the Organization of American States mission that is arriving in Haiti next week to seek Father Aristide's return, vowing to "get on a plane and meet the O.A.S. in Jamaica, Washington, or wherever, as soon as possible." But other prominent business leaders bitterly accused the United States of unfairly punishing them.
"This is a cyclone that is going to strike Haiti," said Albert Herry, a French factory manager who has worked in Haiti for 21 years. "Bush has closed his ears."
 
Striking Back at U.S.
 Others said their country must answer with countermeasures of its own, like rescinding agreements that allow the United States Coast Guard to repatriate thousands of Haitians seeking to reach the shores of Florida by boat.
"Before there is no food and everyone goes crazy here, the United States is going to see an immigration wave that makes the Vietnamese boat people look like nothing," said one businessman, who deplored the coup but was angry about the sanctions.
While people awaited the effects of the new trade sanctions, a suspension of shipments of petroleum products this month by Venezuela, Haiti's principal supplier, has already caused many disruptions.
Snarled lines of motorists waiting to buy gasoline, sometimes as long as a half-mile, have become common throughout the city. Petroleum industry experts say that unless current supplies are renewed, gasoline supplies may run out in a few days. Fuel oil, needed to generate electricity, is expected to last through November.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Cuomo to Call Back Legislature to Cope With Budget Deficit

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo pegged the shortfall in the state's budget at $689 million today and said he would summon the Legislature back in special session to deal with the problem.
At the same time, New York City officials said they faced at least a $200 million gap in their own budget, even without the threat of further state cuts. [Page B7.] 
 The dire fiscal news, which in both cases was attributed to lower tax revenues and increased social-service spending brought on by the recession, was the strongest indication yet that the sharp cuts each government made earlier this year have failed to restore the spending balance pledged by Governor Cuomo and Mayor David N. Dinkins.
Republicans seized on the budget gap to attack Mr. Cuomo, who is still mulling whether to run for President. His chief Republican adversary, Ralph J. Marino, the State Senate majority leader, made a pointed observation that it would be hard to make a "meaningful run" for the White House with the budget problems still unresolved.
The Governor said he had not ruled out any option to close the budget gap, including new taxes or further cuts in state assistance that had been pledged this year to New York City and other struggling localities. Last December, the Legislature took away nearly $200 million they had earlier voted to give to local school districts.
Releasing the $689 million deficit projection for the state's $29 billion budget, the Governor'a Budget Director, Patrick J. Bulgaro, said he had found that "the weakness in the national economy has continued to erode state revenue collections and is now driving up demand for certain collections."
Mr. Bulgaro attributed roughly half of the shortfall to lower than projected revenues from personal income taxes, and the other half to higher spending on Medicaid, welfare and tuition-assistance programs brought on by bulging caseloads.
The Governor has consistently portrayed the problems as similar to those in other states and rooted in failed Federal policies, so presumably he could cite a strong rationale for running for President despite his problems at home. Some of his advisers have felt compelled to point out in recent days that Franklin D. Roosevelt won the Presidency even though his state was overwhelmed by the Depression.
 
Time to Decide
 Governor Cuomo said he would take up to a week to decide how he would recommend closing the state budget gap, the state's second consecutive huge midyear deficit, and he said that he did not know when he would ask lawmakers to return to Albany but that it would be "as soon as possible."
Both Mr. Marino and Assembly Speaker Mel Miller, a Democrat, said they would be extremely reluctant to pursue either local aid cuts or new taxes. Mr. Cuomo said he had ordered state agencies to prepare for cuts of up to 4 percent in their budgets, but this would produce only about a third of the savings needed to close the $689 million gap.
The Republican-controlled State Senate has vowed to concentrate virtually all of the cuts in Medicaid and welfare spending, a course that both the Governor and the Assembly Speaker, Mr. Miller, have attacked as singling out the state's poorest and most infirm residents as a target.
 
A 'Stretchout' Solution
 Mr. Miller, meanwhile, suggested today that a "stretchout" solution could be found, in which part of the deficit could essentially be rolled over into next year, but that approach might be dismissed by other officials as fiscally irresponsible. Thus, even as the magnitude of the problem was brought into sharp focus, the means to solve it remained very much unclear.
The timing of the special session is unclear. "At this moment you have to assume," the Governor said, "that the Legislature has to come back. I'll try to suit their convenience to the extent that I can, but it has to be done as soon as possible."
The outcome could be complicated not only by Mr. Cuomo's ruminations about or actual pursuit of the Presidency but also by the trial of the Assembly Speaker, Mel Miller, on Federal fraud charges, scheduled to begin on Nov. 12.
Mr. Miller denied that there would be any such problems, saying in a telephone interview today, "Don't worry about it -- it's not your problem." He said that if there was a special session and if he was in a courtroom, he would designate one of his Democratic colleagues to lead the Assembly in his place.
 
Heaviest Pressure
 The heaviest pressure still falls on Mr. Cuomo, who is expected to at least start the search for a solution by recommending, within the next week, his plan for closing the gap.
He did not elaborate on any plans today, responding to a question about possible cuts in local aid or social services: "I would never say it's impossible. I wouldn't say anything. I don't know what we're going to do."
Whatever the Governor proposes, he is likely to strain relations with his Democratic allies and to come under relentless attack from Republicans, especially if he continues to be seen as a potential Presidential candidate.
"It's no wonder Governor Cuomo is predicting another budget gap," said C. D. Rappleyea Jr., the Republican leader in the Assembly. "He had no plans, and no plans to make plans, to deal with the deficit as it happened."
But many of Mr. Cuomo's fellow Democrats dismissed the Republican complaints as predictable partisan sniping. Several said they did not believe that the attention he will have to focus on filling the hole in his budget should prove fatal to a Presidential bid.
"So what? He can make a TV commercial in Albany," said Speaker Miller.

HEADLINE: In Louisiana, Duke Divides Old Loyalties

BYLINE: By DON TERRY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BATON ROUGE, La., Oct. 30

 BODY:
When David Duke entered a hotel ballroom here today, Jo Ann Jernigan, a retired nurse and lifelong Democrat, jumped to her feet and applauded.
"I've got to see my candidate," she said Mr. Duke, the Republican candidate for governor and former grand wizard of the Ku Klux Klan. "He's so cute. How can someone who looks like that be bad?" 
 Mrs. Jernigan's friend, Sara M. Spencer, a retired teacher and lifelong Republican, remained seated and covered her eyes with her hand.
"I can't even bear to look at him," she said. "He's a phony. He would destroy this state. He wants to set the blacks back 100 years."
The tight and tough 1991 Louisiana governor's race, said one man here, "has flip-flopped the state, turning Democrats into Republicans and vice versa." It has also divided many longtime friends like the two white women from Monroe attending the state convention of the American Association of Retired Persons here.
And today in a packed ballroom at the Baton Rouge Hilton, those divisions were clearly on display. Mr. Duke, a State Representative from Metairie, and his Democratic opponent in the Nov. 16 runoff election, Edwin W. Edwards, a three-term governor, sat three feet apart at a forum and exchanged insults as well as ideas while 500 retired people frequently booed and hissed the two men's responses.
"In Louisiana, politics can get very, very personal," said a Duke worker, James A. McPherson. "This is their third meeting. It gets hot every time."
Ignoring the pleas of the moderator not to discuss the personal backgrounds of their opponent, both men did exactly that.
Mr. Edwards, a flamboyant, wealthy politician seeking a comeback after surviving two trials on corruption charges, avoided mentioning Mr. Duke's past ties with the Klan and neo-Nazi groups. He did say that Mr. Duke had failed to pay state income taxes for three years "until they caught you."
Mr. Duke said he did not have to file because he did not make enough money. But before he could finish, he was pelted with boos by the largely white crowd that generally seemed supportive of Mr. Duke.
"Isn't it refreshing," he said, trying to recapture the audience, "that you have a politician who doesn't have money everywhere. I'm not like Mr. Edwards. I only have one house."
Mr. Edwards shot back that it appeared "you never had a job."
After the forum, Gloria Richardson, a retired secretary, sighed deeply.
"I'm one of those people, and there are a lot of us, who don't like either one of them," she said. "I still don't like them. I have a big problem."
Mr. Duke seemed to win the most applause today. His anti-welfare pitch has struck a responsive cord in this economically troubled state.
During the 60-minute forum he answered questions about health care, crime, agencies for the elderly and tax reform with attacks on welfare and what he described as an increase in the number of "illegitimate children."
"The welfare system expands everyday," he said. "Why should your tax dollars go to people to buy lottery tickets?"
Even Mr. Edwards admitted that the audience appeared to side with Mr. Duke . He said he expected to have a rough reception because "these are retirees who are having a hard time."
"They and others have been beguiled by his simplistic approach to government," Mr. Edwards said. "Duke is holding out false hope and a cruel hoax."
 
Edwards Courts His Foes
 For the most part, this has been a relatively uneventful week of campaigning, but the action has been fast and furious behind the scenes.
Mr. Edwards has spent considerable time and effort courting the support of his once bitter political foe, Gov. Buddy Roemer. Mr. Roemer placed third, behind Mr. Edwards and Mr. Duke, in the state's open primary on Oct. 19.
Mr. Edwards met with Mr. Roemer at the Governor's mansion for an hour on Tuesday. A spokesman for the Governor, Rusty Jabour, said Mr. Roemer pressed for assurances that his efforts, especially in education, the environment and governmental ethics, would be continued in an Edwards Administration.
Mr. Roemer, a Democrat-turned-Republican, has disavowed Mr. Duke, as has the White House and the Republican National Committee. The Governor has not returned telephone calls from Mr. Duke and refuses to meet with him.
 
Anti-Government Approach
 But Governor Roemer has yet to say publicly whether he will vote for Mr. Edwards in the runoff.
Today, Mr. Duke said an endorsement of Mr. Edwards by Governor Roemer would "drive more people to my side" because "people see me as one person standing up against" special interests and big government.
But Mr. Edwards did receive the endorsement Tuesday of a former Louisiana Governor and political enemy, Dave Treen, a Republican. The Times-Picayune, in New Orleans, quoted Mr. Treen as saying, "David Duke must be defeated."
 
'We'd Be X-Rated'
 "His true agenda is repugnant in the extreme," the newspaper quoted Mr. Treen as saying.
Mr. Edwards also received the endorsements of two former candidates who finished well behind in the primary.
The state's business leaders have come out firmly against Mr. Duke, citing fears that a Duke victory would destroy Louisiana's tourism industry, smother economic development, spark boycotts and bloody the state's image around the world.
On Thursday, the Louisiana Council for Fiscal Reform, a business group, endorsed Mr. Edwards. The chairman, James R. Moffett said, "If Duke is elected, we can kiss economic development good-bye for at least four years. Louisiana wouldn't just be red-lined by business around the nation and around the world, we'd be X-rated."
The Times-Picayune has published several editorials titled "The Choice of Our Lives," questioning Mr. Duke's past and worrying about the state's future under a Duke Administration.
While Mr. Duke quit the Klan in 1980 and now dismisses his membership as youthful enthusiasm, he reportedly celebrated Hitler's birthday into the 1980's and remains active in the National Association for the Advancement of White People, an organization he created.
 
Almost Neck-and-Neck
 The election appears to be extremely close, according to the first statewide poll to be conducted since the primary.
The poll, commissioned by The Shreveport Times and published on Tuesday, said that of the 817 voters surveyed on Thursday through Saturday, 46 percent said they would vote for Mr. Edwards, 42 percent for Mr. Duke and 12 percent were undecided. The poll, conducted by Mason-Dixon Opinion Research of Columbia, Md., has a margin of sampling error of plus or minus three and a half percentage points.
For now, two longtime friends like Mrs. Spencer and Mrs. Jernigan can still laugh about their political differences in the bitter campaign.
"She drove here with me," Mrs. Spencer said, patting her friend's hand. "I told her she'll have to run along side the car when we go home. No Duke supporter is riding with me."
Mr. Jernigan laughed. She said she waited in line for two hours to get into the ballroom to hear the two candidates.
"I've stood in longer lines at Disney World," she said. "But I don't think Duke's past is that atrocious. People change. I don't have the same ideas I did 20 years ago either."
"Hush up, it does matter," Mrs. Spencer said. "I think our reputation is on the line. I don't want either one of them but I have to make a choice for my state. I lived through World War II and I won't vote for a Nazi."

HEADLINE: Gorbachev and Mitterrand Meet, for a Little Embarrassment

BYLINE: By ALAN RIDING, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SOUSTONS, France, Oct. 30

 BODY:
After attending the opening of the Middle East peace conference in Madrid, President Mikhail S. Gorbachev met President Francois Mitterrand in southern France tonight to smooth over relations ruffled by the French leader's response to the failed Soviet coup in August.
After Mr. Gorbachev was detained at his summer dacha in the Crimea, Mr. Mitterrand was strongly criticized at home for referring to "the new Soviet leaders" and suggesting that it would be possible to do business with them if they continued to carry out economic and political change. 
 In his just-published account of his imprisonment, "The August Coup," Mr. Gorbachev also wrote of the moments after his release: "Francois Mitterrand was due to call me. He didn't do it, and I regret it to this day. As soon as I got back to Moscow, I asked to be put into contact with him."
Tonight, in a televised interview broadcast from Mr. Mitterrand's country home at Latche, the two men, sitting side by side and occasionally looking embarrassed, went out of their way to explain that any misunderstandings had been dealt with.
Mr. Mitterrand insisted that no foreign leader spoke with Mr. Gorbachev by telephone until he was freed on the evening of Aug. 21. He added that it was "bad luck" that unlike President Bush, he could not reach the Soviet leader "during the half hour" between his release and his departure for Moscow.
Mr. Gorbachev then puzzlingly questioned the authenticity of the lines referring to Mr. Mitterrand that the interviewer read from his book. "I don't think that what you said was in my book," he said. "There is no criticism of the President of France. If anything like this is in my book, it's not my book."
Clearly flustered, Mr. Mitterrand then chipped in to suggest that the American and French texts were different. "In the English text, which was the initial text, there is no such quote," Mr. Mitterrand said. But the text published in the United States does include the reference to the telephone call that never came.
 
'Problem Resolved'
 Finally, the Soviet President recalled that the first foreign representative he met after his release was from the French Embassy in Moscow. "For me, that was an important sign of the position of France," he said, adding curtly, "Problem resolved."
Political experts said the fact that Mr. Mitterrand invited Mr. Gorbachev and his wife, Raisa, to spend the night in his 18th-century farmhouse, just three miles from this summer vacation resort, was seen as an effort to underline their intimacy.
In talks tonight and on Thursday morning, the two men are also to discuss the Middle East and the Soviet Union's pressing economic needs.
The French leader said he had also won support for his idea of a meeting of the four nations with nuclear weapons in Europe -- the United States, the Soviet Union, France and Britain -- to discuss control of Soviet strategic weapons.

HEADLINE: House Votes to Add $1 Billion To Programs Aiding Children

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
In an unexpected move Tuesday night, the House of Representatives voted to add more than a $1 billion to children's programs by declaring child poverty a national emergency.
Legislatively, the bill's prospects seem dim. But politically, the bill adds a new wrinkle to the partisan maneuvering over domestic issues as next year's Presidential election approaches. 
 For its Democratic authors, among the bill's pleasing outcomes would be a Presidential veto embarrassing to the White House, which has supported emergency money for foreign aid but has resisted, in the name of fiscal responsibility, declaring emergencies at home.
 
'Our Own Children'
 "It's inappropriate to declare Turks and Kurds an emergency, and not our own children," said Representative Patricia Schroeder, the Colorado Democrat who served as the provision's chief supporter.
Democratic Presidential candidates have made the same arguments about Mr. Bush's veto of a bill that would have provided $6 billion in extended unemployment benefits to American workers.
But Republicans are not the only ones put in a difficult political posture by Tuesday's House vote. Senior Congressional Democrats, including Representative Jamie L. Whitten of Mississippi, chairman of the Appropriations Committee, also opposed Ms. Schroeder's proposal. For Mr. Whitten, it complicates an already-troubled effort to dispense money to victims of hurricanes, fires, and other natural disasters.
The problem is last year's budget agreement, which dictates that any increases in domestic spending must be offset by corresponding cuts in other domestic programs. The only exception is for situations Congress and the President jointly designate as emergencies.
So far, President Bush has agreed to declare a budgetary emergency for only two bills, both of which provided foreign aid. Of the $1.1 billion supplied by the two bills, about $650 million went to Israel, $200 million went to Turkey and $140 million went to Kurdish refugees.
For several months, Mr. Whitten has been unsuccessfully trying to persuade the White House to use the emergency provisions to finance about $2.5 billion for farmers and other victims of natural disasters. The White House has told Congress to take the money from other domestic accounts.
So Mr. Whitten's bill already seemed headed for a veto when Ms. Schroeder slipped in an amendment on the floor that added the money for children's programs. Her provision, which passed by a vote of 243 to 180, adds $1.2 billion for Head Start programs, $100 million for child nutrition under the Women, Infants, and Children program, and $90 million for immunizations.
The legislation now travels to the Senate.

HEADLINE: THE MIDDLE EAST TALKS;
How Sweet A Victory?

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MADRID, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Many months after the event, George Bush and the United States today plucked the fruits of victory in the Persian Gulf war, but it is still much too early to predict how sweet they will prove to be.
Critics have suggested that the United States achieved far too little in the war, because Saddam Hussein was not overthrown, Iran remained as hostile and Kuwait as undemocratic as ever, and Saudi Arabia shed neither its isolation nor its archaic ways. Iraq was thrown out of Kuwait, some said, but very little else changed. 
 This morning it was clear that a very great deal had changed, even if most things remained shrouded in ambiguity and doubt as deep as the smog that blanketed Madrid. It was not only the energy and the diplomatic skills of Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d that created the remarkable tableau, with mortal enemies arranged around the same table, that Mr. Bush saw before him in the splendid Royal Palace this morning; all of his labors would have counted for very little without seismic shifts in the global order of things.
 
The Domestic Issue
 So far, so good. But taking the next enormous steps toward lasting peace in the Middle East may require the deep personal involvement of Mr. Bush over many months, and that may lie beyond his domestic political reach.
Dramatic and momentous as the peace conference seems to Arabs and Israelis, as well as American Jews and Arab-Americans and foreign policy specialists all over the world, it seems distant to average Americans, an event unlikely to influence their own well-being directly. So Mr. Bush's undoubted triumph in bringing the talks into being may not earn him commensurate gains at home.
The situation is profoundly different from that which used to confront American policy makers and American voters as they looked toward the Middle East. No longer is there a threat that an Arab-Israeli conflict could escalate into a nuclear war between the United States and the Soviet Union. Though American politicians are not so impolitic as to say so, Israel is therefore much less of a strategic asset to the West than it used to be.
No longer is there an imminent threat of an oil embargo, with dire consequences for the world economy. Only the plight of the remaining American hostages touches most Americans deeply, and their number is slowly shrinking.
To many policy makers, the catastrophic situation in the Soviet Union, of which President Mikhail S. Gorbachev spoke so candidly today, and the emerging shape of Eastern and Western Europe, seem deserving of higher priority. To many voters -- most of them, according to recent opinion polls -- Mr. Bush seems far too inattentive to domestic economic problems.
As if to emphasize his intention to mind the store, he climbed back aboard Air Force One for the trip home without even waiting for the end of the first day's speeches here.
Facing a re-election campaign that will in a sense begin at a big Bush-Quayle fund-raiser in Texas later this week, faced with the growing conviction in the electorate that he spends too much time on foreign policy already, faced with growing evidence that further economic woes may lie ahead rather than the robust recovery he had hoped for, the President is seriously considering the curtailment of his trip to Asia and the Pacific next month.
From that perspective, it seems doubtful that Mr. Bush will want to put the Middle East at the top of his agenda, at least until next November has passed, in the way that Jimmy Carter focused intently on the peace process between Israel and Egypt in 1977 and 1978. And without sustained Presidential intervention, a decisive breakthrough in these talks may prove elusive.
 
Cold War Symbols
 The end of the cold war was symbolized by the presence here of Mr. Gorbachev as a co-sponsor. The conflict between the Arabs and the Israelis, the Soviet leader acknowledged, "bears the heavy stamp of the so-called cold war, and it was not until an end was put to that, that ending this conflict became a tangible possibility."
But the United States won the cold war as well as the gulf war, so it was President Bush, and not Mr. Gorbachev, who spoke first today and who set out the general terms within which the peace talks will proceed.
The Soviet Union is no longer the protector of Syria, any more than it was the protector of Iraq. The United States is the only credible outside power in the region. The gulf war also changed the internal military balance, not only by removing Iraq from the equation, at least for the moment, but also by demonstrating that high-tech American weapons, more available to Israel than to the Arabs, again at least for the moment, were the future of warfare.
So Syria is here, and the Palestinians and the Jordanians -- and because they are here, the Israelis did not feel that they could stay away without suffering further erosion in their relations with Washington.
 
Saudi as an Observer
 The Bush Administration was even able, in the end, to deliver Saudi Arabia to Madrid, with Prince Bandar bin Sultan, the Saudi Ambassador to Washington, flamboyantly garbed in the white robe of the desert, slipping into an observer's chair at the last moment. There could have been few more arresting demonstrations of his country's rapidly evolving regional role.
Mr. Bush sought to strike a balance in his address by endorsing the Arabs' demand for territorial concessions from Israel and at the same time defining "peace" in terms that delighted the Israelis. But the United States will have to play a less even-handed role than those comments implied if the conference is to succeed.
As Michael Mandelbaum of the Council on Foreign Relations pointed out, Israel "will be asked to give up a tangible security asset" -- territory that it has occupied as a buffer against its enemies -- "in exchange for something that is intangible and revocable: in effect, for a promise."
Only if the United States offers assurances of various kinds to Israel, many analysts believe, will Israeli public opinion propel the hard-line government of Prime Minister Yitzhak Shamir toward genuine bargaining. The President pledged today that if necessary Washington would "extend guarantees, provide technology and support, if that is what peace requires."
 
'Peace Is Possible'
 Mr. Baker and others are ready, once the various participants have spelled out their positions in full, and especially if they reach an early deadlock, to try to inject some new ideas into the negotiations subtly, while at the same time carefully avoiding the elaboration of settlement plans that would only turn into targets for one side or the other to shoot down.
"We want to make them do the bargaining," a high-ranking American official said, "and we think each of them is sufficiently worried about looking like villains to the rest of the world to take this seriously."
"Peace is possible," Mr. Bush said, noting that France and Germany had become allies after a hundred years of war, and that the Soviet Union stood alongside the United States as a sponsor here despite the legacy of the cold war. Prime Minister Felipe Gonzalez of Spain recalled how Christians, Jews and Arabs had lived together in harmony in this country until the Moors and the Jews were driven out in the year Christopher Columbus set sail.
It is a matter of mustering the will to try, a matter of taking chances, as Robert Schuman and Jean Monnet did in seeking European unity after World War II. One thing that could help to scatter the ghosts of distrust and hatred would be some sort of gesture from one side or the other, like President Anwar el-Sadat's trip to Israel in 1977, that would help to "break the fetters of the past," in Mr. Gorbachev's phrase. But there was no sign of that from either side today.
Instead, presented by the American organizers with an opportunity to fraternize during the 30 minutes that they waited in the marble hall for Mr. Bush and Mr. Gorbachev to arrive, the Arab delegates turned their backs on the Jews, and vice versa, except for the Egyptians, who have a special status. If the very convening of the conference was a good omen, the frostiness of the participants' demeanor constituted an offsetting note of pessimism.

HEADLINE: Washington Talk;
Gas Mileage and Safety, Or Statistics in Collision

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
As Congress takes up a proposal to increase gas mileage requirements sharply, all sides in the debate have churned out so much devilishly detailed data to support their respective views that the basic facts have become impossible to determine.
There is a lot riding on fuel economy in automobiles: smog, the global climate, the trade deficit, the job market, the Middle East and the size of the family car to the highway mortality rate. 
 But the issue immediately before Congress is whether automobile manufacturers ought to be required by law to increase the average gas mileage of cars from today's 27.5 miles per gallon to 34 miles a gallon in 1996 and 40 miles a gallon in 2001, as proposed by Senator Richard H. Bryan, Democrat of Nevada. A House bill would go even further. The Bush Administration supports a Senate energy bill that would leave fuel economy standards to the discretion of the Transportation Department.
To appreciate the complexities of the debate, examine one proposition seized on by the legislation's opponents, which include the auto industry and the Administration -- the claim that saving gas would cost lives.
"Everybody has got their numbers and statistics," said Brian O'Neill of the Insurance Institute for Highway Safety, one of many sources of the statistics on fatalities and car size.
The institute has produced statistics showing that every one-mile-per-gallon improvement in fuel economy translates into a 3.9 percent increase in the death rate from auto accidents.
Reasoning that to meet strict mileage standards auto makers would have to sell smaller cars than they do now, Transportation Department officials last year labeled the Bryan legislation the "Highway Fatality Act." They cited what they said was irrefutable statistical evidence based on laws of physics to show that, all other things being equal, smaller cars are more dangerous than larger ones.
According to the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration, which is part of the Transportation Department, smaller cars built partly to comply with existing mileage statutes have already cost "nearly 2,000" lives a year since 1974.
Now the data of the insurance institute and the highway safety agency alike have been challenged.
Last week the General Accounting Office reported that its own statistical findings "support the view that the automobile weight reductions since the mid-1970's have had virtually no effect on total highway fatalities."
And the Office of Technology Assessment said two weeks ago that even if cars get smaller they are likely to get safer. "Given enough time, auto makers can significantly improve fleet fuel economy without downsizing, though with some weight reduction, and without any likely safety impact," the agency said.
The accounting office did not address the question of whether tougher gas mileage laws would force auto makers to build smaller cars. The technology agency's report said that if the Bryan bill is approved its 1996 deadline for reaching 34 miles per gallon "probably is unattainable without a significant shift in sales to smaller vehicles."
Not surprisingly, the G.A.O. report was made public by Senator Bryan, who said it was "clear and devastating to the bogus arguments against improving automobile fuel economy standards." The technology agency's report was issued by Senator J. Bennett Johnston, a Louisiana Democrat who favors its less aggressive schedule for tightening fuel efficiency.
The insurance institute then cranked out an analysis disputing the G.A.O. findings, just as it had earlier rebutted the statistics used in the technology agency's report.
And where does the Administration stand in these data wars?
Transportation Department officials contend that both the technology agency and the accounting office studies are "flawed." The department has its own study under way, being done by the National Academy of Sciences. Luckily for the Administration, which does not want the energy bill to include any fixed target for fuel efficiency, that study will not be completed until the end of the year.

HEADLINE: Bridge

BYLINE: By Alan Truscott

 BODY:
With millions of bridge hands played around the world each year, there is no sure way to determine which declarer has exercised the greatest skill. However, the International Bridge Press Association makes an attempt. A committee headed by Dirk Schroeder of Germany reviewed a number of candidates, and at a meeting in Yokohama, Japan, early this month the diagramed deal was named "Hand of the Year." It was played in Athens in April at the European Economic Community Championships. The declarer was Schmuel Friedman, playing for Israel in a match against Belgium.
Three no-trump was an obvious contract, but after a spade lead it was not easy to find nine tricks. After winning in dummy South led a club to the jack and was happy and surprised to find that he had won the trick. There were now eight tricks in view, with various chances of a ninth. Looking at all four hands, it is easy to find the ninth in the diamond suit, but that was not obvious to South. 
 Continuing clubs immediately would have allowed East to win and return a spade, with the ninth trick still in doubt. A better plan was to cash the spade winners, lead to the club ace and play a club, but that plan was due to fail if West began with five spades and the heart ace.
South found an even better play. He took one more round of spades, led to the club ace and continued that suit, throwing the remaining high spade from the dummy. This kept the spades under control, and if East were able to lead a third spade he would give South the extra entry he needed to establish his fifth club. And if East returned a red suit he would give South his ninth trick in that way.
Two new duplicate games are now active in the New York metropolitan area: Y.W.C.A., 515 North Street, White Plains, at 8 P.M. Fridays and 1:15 P.M. Saturdays; Barbizon Hotel, 140 East 63d Street, Manhattan, 12:30 P.M. Monday, 7:15 P.M. Wednesdays and 7:15 P.M. Fridays.

HEADLINE: Tartikoff Begins Charting New Course For Paramount Films

BYLINE: By BERNARD WEINRAUB, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Oct. 30

 BODY:
Brandon Tartikoff, the chairman of the Paramount Pictures Corporation, said today that the dismal financial showing of the studio's latest releases, "Frankie and Johnny" and "The Butcher's Wife," had led to a fundamental reassessment of the kinds of films he would make as he solidified control of one of Hollywood's most powerful studios.
Instead of movies intended for an older audience, Mr. Tartikoff said he planned to make films that appeal to younger audiences, who make up the bulk of moviegoers today. He cited movies with music, like "Saturday Night Fever" and "Footloose," major successes that drew huge young audiences. 
 "Paramount has a successful tradition of being the studio known for the modern-day musical," he said in a surprisingly frank telephone interview. "You can almost call 'Top Gun' a musical, too. It had a wall-to-wall soundtrack of music and action. Paramount owns the modern-day musical crowd, just as Disney owns the animation crowd."
"While there is a place for the adult film, or for pictures that don't necessarily have to be made for the most frequent moviegoers -- 25 and under -- you don't want an abundance of pictures clearly tilted to viewers 25 and up," Mr. Tartikoff said.
He cited recent Paramount films including "Regarding Henry" and "Soapdish," as well as "The Butcher's Wife" and "Frankie and Johnny," that were made before he took over Paramount and that appealed to a limited audience.
"Some of these were very satisfying pictures," he said. "But this was making movies primarily for women over 25. In a recession, the people most affected will be the least frequent moviegoers -- the older audience. If you aim for this audience, you better hit the bull's-eye, or else they say, 'Hey, I'll wait for the video.' "
 
'Frustrated? You Bet'
 Mr. Tartikoff, the former president of NBC Entertainment, who took over Paramount Pictures nearly four months ago, is plainly disturbed that the studio did not hit the bull's-eye with "Frankie and Johnny," a gentle love story about a short-order cook and a waitress. The film, which received generally good reviews and has two major stars, Michelle Pfeiffer and Al Pacino, cost about $30 million. According to Daily Variety, it dropped off 30 percent in revenues last week and has so far grossed an extremely disappointing $13 million.
"Are we considering what happened or didn't happen to this movie?" Mr. Tartikoff said. "You bet we are. Are we frustrated? You bet we are. It's a terrific movie. If you woke me up from a deep sleep after three or four weeks into the run and I asked how 'Frankie and Johnny' was doing, and you told me $40 million gross, I would have expected it. Some of us are scratching our heads."
He added: "We ask ourselves, is the recession so deep now that a picture like this has a much tougher time getting an audience because it is a one-quadrant kind of picture -- that is, viewers 25-plus -- or is it the picture itself not appealing to the audience?"
Mr. Tartikoff said the televised hearings before the confirmation of Supreme Court Justice Clarence Thomas, as well as the World Series, may have diminished moviegoing on recent weekends.
About "The Butcher's Wife," a comedy with Demi Moore, which cost at least $20 million and received mixed reviews, Mr. Tartikoff said: "We knew it was a soft comedy. We did not have expectations as high as 'Frankie and Johnny.' " He said it was hoped that future Paramount films would appeal to "the widest possible audience."
"Look at a movie like 'Ghost,' " he said. "A 10-year-old kid will see it. My mother will see it. Men will see it. Ethnic audiences will see it."
 
Departures Expected
 Mr. Tartikoff declined to discuss what is virtually an open secret in Hollywood: that he is about to remove his two top movie executives at Paramount, David Kirkpatrick, the head of the motion picture division, and Gary Lucchesi, the president of production. The two are holdovers from the administration of Frank Mancuso, who was dismissed in March after a series of lavish films, including "The Godfather Part III" and "Days of Thunder," that were financial disappointments.
But the two executives' departures, expected for several months, have not been announced, and the huge studio is somewhat adrift, say agents, producers and writers. Scott Rudin, producer of the new "Addams Family," is believed to be a front-runner for Mr. Kirkpatrick's job.
Many agents, producers, writers and actors have generally refrained from dealing with Paramount -- unless they have personal connections with Mr. Tartikoff -- because of the looming turmoil and the fact that the executive who may approve a project today may be out the door tomorrow.
Mr. Tartikoff said heatedly that the talk of paralysis at Paramount was "wrong." Last week, he met stock-market analysts with Martin Davis, chairman of Paramount Communications, and disclosed plans for 30 films. As a result, three Wall Street investment firms raised their evaluation of Paramount stock.
 
Early Decisions
 Among his accomplishments at Paramount, Mr. Tartikoff can already count the wooing of Eddie Murphy into a contract extension. Mr. Murphy was openly unhappy with the money and scripts Paramount had offered before Mr. Tartikoff took over. Even though the actor's career has stumbled of late, it would have been a major setback for Mr. Tartikoff if he had broken off his longstanding relationship with the studio.
Moreover, Mr. Tartikoff has played hardball with temperamental actors. When Alec Baldwin, who created the role of the Navy officer in "The Hunt for Red October," demanded changes in the starting date of the sequel, "Patriot Games," Paramount startled the actor -- and the industry -- by replacing him with Harrison Ford.
Producers and studio executives say it is far too early to assess Mr. Tartikoff as a movie mogul. "All he needs is a couple of hits; he doesn't need an Academy Award," said Howard Rosenman, the producer of the new films "Father of the Bride" and "Shining Through."
Mr. Tartikoff acknowledges that he has puzzled the industry with some of his pronouncements and decisions, including his first move as chairman: to make a cheap, quick Christmas movie, "All I Want for Christmas," which opens Nov. 8. Another early decision was to make "Wayne's World," a "Saturday Night Live" spinoff scheduled to open in February.
"We know this is a very tough market now, and I can't tell you I've learned all the lessons yet," he said. "There's this divided debate going on. Where does the economy leave off and the quality of the pictures take over? Is it the product or the pocketbook? We don't know."

HEADLINE: Record Number In U.S. Relying On Food Stamps

BYLINE: By FOX BUTTERFIELD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BOSTON, Oct. 30

 BODY:
As a tangible sign of the deepening economic troubles afflicting American families, the number of people receiving Federal food stamps jumped to a record level in August, meaning that almost one in 10 Americans now depend on the Government to put food on the table.
According to figures made public today by the United States Department of Agriculture, 23.6 million Americans received food stamps in August, an increase of more than 3 million over the same month last year. 
 To specialists, these numbers suggest that the economy is slipping back into recession. But for many working Americans, they confirm that poverty and hunger are no longer just problems of the inner city or the hollows of Appalachia and instead have now spread out into middle class suburbs.
In Sudbury, a middle-class suburb 15 miles west of Boston, Brian Carnevale, the assistant manager of the local Star Market, said he has seen a fivefold increase in customers using food stamps over the past year. "They are just average customers," he said, "a lot of people who worked in the high-tech industry who have recently lost their jobs."
 
Recipients Defy Stereotypes
 "They are young and old, people in business suits," Mr. Carnevale said, standing inside his large red brick store with a white steeple to match the traditional New England architecture of the neighborhood. "And you're going to see more and more of them," he predicted, noting that nearby electronics companies like the Raytheon Corporation and the Digital Equipment Corporation are in the midst of the worst layoffs in their history.
But the voices of people who are getting food stamps tell a more vivid story than do the dry numbers.
In Passaic, N.J., today, Linda Porter, a 20-year-old single mother of two boys, aged 2 and 4, was buying groceries with food stamps. "Believe me, they help," she said. "You can buy the food you need and still get to keep some cash in your pockets."
"I used to be ashamed, but not anymore," Ms. Porter said. "They really come in handy."
In Manhattan, Nora Quinones, who works as a seamstress in the garment district, is getting food stamps for the first time. She said it was because she was recently divorced and needs the extra help to feed her 8-year-old son. "I have to pay the rent," she said.
In Connecticut, the number of households receiving food stamps increased by 33 percent in the past year, according to the state's department of income maintenance; in New Jersey, the increase in households was 17 percent in the past year. New York State keeps its figures by people, not households, and showed a 12 percent increase in the past year.
While some of the heaviest concentrations of people enrolled in the food stamp program remain in economically depressed metropolitan areas, the sharp rise in middle-class recipients has struck many experts.
"We're getting calls from people who have always had a full work history and never needed public assistance of any kind," said Karen Plattes, the coordinator of the food stamp outreach program for Project Bread, a private, nonprofit agency in Boston.
"Many of them are reluctant to apply," Ms. Plattes said. In fact, she said many of those who have asked her for help applying for food stamps had used up their unemployment benefits as much as six months earlier, meaning they had been out of work for a year.
 
Sharp Rise Since 1970
 The number of Americans getting food stamps has sharply climbed since the early days of the program. In 1970 only about 2 in 100 Americans received them. In 1980 the number had reached 9 in 100. Now it is close to 10 of 100.
The specialists point out that more than 80 percent of those getting food stamps are families with children, making it a major antihunger program for children.
In Ms. Plattes's office, which serves towns and cities throughout Massachusetts, one of the states hardest hit by the recession, the number of calls for assistance in getting food stamps has doubled over the past year, to about 1,200 a month from about 600 a month, she said.
One of those who called, a woman from the affluent oceanside suburb of Marshfield, 20 miles south of Boston, earned $90,000 last year, Ms. Plattes said. The woman had lost her job and had no money in the bank. She would have qualified for food stamps, except that her car was too expensive, Ms. Plattes said.
Recipients of food stamps can have assets of only about $2,000, excluding their homes. The value of their cars is not counted if the vehicle is worth less than $4,500.
To qualify for food stamps, a program started by President Lyndon B. Johnson in the 1960's, families also cannot have an income that exceeds 100 percent of the Federal poverty guidelines, or $1,117 a month for a family of four, according to the Department of Agriculture.
The average benefit for each person for each meal, assuming three meals a day, is only 65 cents, according to figures compiled by the Senate Agriculture Committee.
A family could receive both unemployment benefits and food stamps if it qualifies. Unlike unemployment benefits, which run out after six months, there is no limit on the amount of time a family can receive food stamps.
A spokesman for the Department of Agriculture's Food and Nutrition Service, Phil Shanholtzer, said that Congress had provided a $1.5 billion "cushion" in the budget, in case of an increase in the number of people using food stamps, and he said that amount should cover the sharp rise in recipients this year. The program cost $19.6 billion in fiscal year 1991, and food assistance programs now make up more than half of the Agriculture Department's budget, he said.
The Federal Government pays the entire cost of food stamp benefits, and administrative costs are shared by Washington and the states.
Mr. Shanholtzer insisted there "is probably no single explanation" for the big increase in food stamp recipients this year. He said "the economy is certainly a factor in some areas." But he said there were some other possible causes, including the Government's efforts to make the application process easier and changes in the immigration laws, which had created more permanent resident aliens, who are eligible for food stamps.
But some Democrats were quick to jump on the new figures as a sign that the Bush Administration has failed the economy. "This is proof of what most Americans already know, and the Administration wants to ignore," said Senator Patrick J. Leahy, Democrat of Vermont, the chairman of the Senate Agriculture Committee.
Until this spring, the number of people receiving food stamps had hit its peak in March 1983, at 22.6 million, the Agriculture Department figures show. Last March it reached 22.9 million, and it remained relatively stable until the new high of 23.6 million in August.
