HEADLINE: Campaign on Term Limits Taps a Gusher of Money

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY EGAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SEATTLE, Oct. 30

 BODY:
For all the debate over what placing limits on politicians' terms of office would do for American democracy, the money now pouring into the campaign for the most restrictive such measure ever to go before a state's voters tells an altogether different story.
Billed by its supporters as a prairie fire of populism, the term limit proposal on the Nov. 5 ballot in Washington State has been bankrolled by a group founded by a handful of wealthy, out-of-state industrialists with a conservative political agenda. Two brothers, David H. Koch and Charles G. Koch, listed by Fortune magazine as among the 20 richest men in the world, are the main financial backers of the group leading the push for term limits. 
 On the other side, money against the term limits is coming from some of the nation's most powerful lobbies, among them the National Rifle Association, Philip Morris Companies, and Anheuser-Busch Companies, representing guns, tobacco and alcohol interests.
The Washington measure, Initiative 553, would limit members of the United States House of Representatives to three two-year terms -- a law that would throw out all eight members of the state's Congressional delegation three years after the vote. It would end the legislative career of House Speaker Thomas S. Foley, restrict United States Senators to two terms and force 74 of the 98 members of this state's House of Representatives to step down after the 1994 session.
Although three states, California, Oklahoma and Colorado, have already voted to limit terms, only Colorado's law applies to Federal officials. Only in Washington would the limits be retroactive. Mr. Foley, joined by many legal scholars, contends that the states cannot limit the terms of Federal office holders, although the precise language in the Constitution is unclear on that point.
As the term limits movement sweeps across the West, pushed by a mood of voter disgust, the lowest approval ratings for Congress in modern times and scandals about bounced checks and meals not paid for, the money going into the campaigns has challenged the arguments of both sides.
 
A Grass-Roots Beginning
 The movement in this state began as a genuine grass-roots effort, drafted in the Tacoma living room of Sherry Bockwinkel, who has been active in liberal political campaigns. But the initiative, which needed at least 150,000 voter signatures by July to qualify for the November ballot, was going nowhere until a group called Citizens for Congressional Reform stepped in with thousands of dollars and expert political help.
That group, based in Washington, D.C., was started with several hundred thousand dollars donated by David Koch, who with his brother Charles controls Koch Industries of Wichita, Kan., the nation's largest privately held oil company. The Justice Department has been investigating the company after allegations that it stole millions of dollars in oil from Indian-owned lands, an allegation that David Koch says is groundless.
Money from Citizens for Congressional Reform was used to pay professional signature gatherers from out of state who got 40 cents a signature -- something that had never before been done with a voter initiative that usually has a number on the ballot each November. As of early this week, $543,000 of the $740,000 raised by the term limits drive, or 73 percent, has come from one source, Citizens for Congressional Reform and its state branch. Less than 10 percent of the supporters' money is from Washington State, said Paul Gillie, an analyst with the state Public Disclosure Commission, to which contributions must be reported. The political parties are not among the top contributors, commission figures showed.
"I ignited the spark, and the fire is raging on its own," David Koch said in an interview.
 
Benefit to Republicans Seen
 Mr. Koch also helped to start Citizens for a Sound Economy, the parent organization of the term limits organization. This group, whose chairman is James C. Miller 3d, who was budget director under President Ronald Reagan, has pushed for less government regulation, fewer taxes and converting some public industries to private enterprises.
Mr. Miller said in an interview that term limits were likely to result in reduced Federal spending and in the election of more Republicans. But his group, he said, is nonpartisan.
"This is a sagebrush revolution," he said. "It is not an attempt to get Republican majorities. And it would be a tragedy if it loses because of a last-minute attempt to discredit the process."
Thomas Mann, director of government studies at the Brookings Institute, said the term limits proposal here, and the ones likely to be on the ballot in a dozen states next year, were a false form of populism. "There is a hidden agenda here that has been lost in all the rhetoric," he said. "The money is coming from groups whose primary interest is to weaken government -- a carefully calculated effort to go after those institutions dominated by Democrats."
Ms. Bockwinkel, a self-professed liberal Democrat, scoffs at such assertions. Even though conservative millionaires are responsible for her initiative's getting on the ballot, she said, nobody knows how government will change with term limits. She expects to see more "genuine Democrats," as she calls the more liberal members, in office.
Dr. David J. Olson, a professor of political science at the University of Washington, has done a study of the campaign here, and he concluded that genuine populism had little to do with the drive.
"This effort would never have made it on the ballot without the assistance of one out-of-state group," he said. "That is extraordinary for Washington State. This is an elite-centered campaign on both sides."
Dr. Olson said the contributions against term limits tended to bolster an argument that the so-called special interests which dominate Congressional campaigns have the most to lose with the loss of long-term incumbents.
The top contributor against the initiative has been Philip Morris U.S.A., which makes Marlboro cigarettes. Philip Morris gave $25,000, or about 10 percent of what opponents of term limits have raised.
 
Opponents Have Definite Goals
 When asked why a tobacco company based in New York City was so interested in defeating term limits in Washington State, Victor Han, a company spokesman, said they were asked by the opponents to contribute. In addition, he said, the company believes "voters should be able to choose the leaders they want."
Another major contributor, the National Rifle Association, gave $10,000 to try to defeat Initiative 553. A council of unions, a lawyers' group and Kaiser Aluminum, a large employer with plants in the state are among the top 10 contributors against the initiative. Also contributing to opponents of the initiative is the state's largest employer, the Boeing Company. Paul Binder, a spokesman for Boeing, the largest employer in the state, said the company feared terms limits could reduce the power of Washington representatives.
No matter the outcome of the vote next Tuesday, the initiative has already had a domino effect on politics in Washington, the second most populous state in the West. Last week a seven-term Representative from northwestern Washington, Al Swift, who is an influential Democrat in the House, announced that he would leave Congress after one more term beginning next year. He said he made his announcement now so he would be free to campaign against the initiative without being accused of just helping himself.

HEADLINE: SOCCER;
Making Giants Stadium a Part of the '94 World Cup

BYLINE: By ALEX YANNIS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: EAST RUTHERFORD, N.J., Oct. 30

 BODY:
The world governing body of soccer is determined to make Giants Stadium an integral part of the 1994 World Cup tournament despite the need for drastic changes there and a lack of funds to implement them.
That message was made abundantly clear late this afternoon by members of an inspection committee from FIFA, the governing body, who spoke enthusiastically about a special concept planned for the stadium.
 
Contributions Cited
 "This stadium must be part of the tournament," said Guido Tognoni, FIFA's press officer and a member of the inspection committee. "We're making a lot of inspections and have nice things to say about everyone, but this site cannot be left out of the world's biggest sports event." 
 Tognoni said the metropolitan area had contributed a great deal to soccer in this country. He added that the World Cup in the United States would not be the same without representation from the stadium, the home of the Cosmos, the most successful team in the old North American Soccer League.
 
A Test in Kansas City
 In order to meet the required 80-by-100-meter field dimensions required for World Cup soccer games, an elevated platform will be built above the existing football field. The platform, which will reach the level of the first row of seats (about 10 feet above the football field) will support 6 to 10 inches of soil and a natural grass field.
 Security for players and officials will be provided, according to the plan, by dasher boards similar to those in a hockey rink around the perimeter of the grass field.
"It's a radical and unique situation," said Hugo Salcedo, who worked for FIFA's organizing commitee in the 1986 World Cup in Mexico and last year in Italy.
The estimated cost for the field modifications is $2.6 to $3 million, according to a Pam Scott, a Kansas City architect whose firm came up with the idea of the platform.
Scott said the idea had been successfully tested with a 40-foot-by-40-foot platform complete with grass at Arrowhead Stadium in Kansas City, but that it had not been tried at larger dimensions.
The question of who would finance the project was referred to George Zoffinger, the New Jersey Commissioner of Commerce and Economic Development. He said the money would come from the private sector, not from the New Jersey Sports and Exposition Authority, which runs the Meadowlands complex.
 
Public Funds Can't Be Used
 "Governor Florio has made it clear that the state will not be able to use public funds for this," Zoffinger said. "The money will have to be raised from the private sector."
When he was told that some had estimated the cost at $5 million, Zoffinger said, "We're operating with the idea that this will cost $3 million." He added that the money had to be raised by Feb. 1.
Michael Rowe, the executive vice president of the exposition authority, said Giants Stadium would lose $3 million in revenues from events such as concerts during the time needed for renovation of the stadium and for whatever World Cup games would be played there. The tournament is scheduled to be played from June 17 to July 17, 1994 at several sites. The inspection committee is touring stadiums throughout the country in an effort to select the venues.
 
Yale Bowl to Be Inspected
 The committee inspected Veterans Stadium in Philadelphia Tuesday and will see the Yale Bowl in New Haven on Thursday. Members said it was possible for both Giants Stadium and Yale Bowl to be used.
The United States World Cup Organizing Committee has maintained since the start of the bidding process that the games would be staged in between 8 and 12 cities.
Ross Berlin, who is in charge of the venues, reiterated that again today, but Tognoni said that FIFA wanted the 1994 tournament to be played in 12 cities.

LENGTH: 1021 words

HEADLINE: PARENT & CHILD

BYLINE: By Lawrence Kutner

 BODY:
HIS mother was a well-known surgeon. His father was a university professor. His older siblings were either doctors or lawyers. The family had similar expectations for him.
But after the young man graduated from a prestigious medical school, he decided to become a carpenter. 
 The concept of upward mobility has been intregral to this country. Generations of American parents have assumed that their children would equal or surpass them financially, socially and professionally. If a young adult fails to do so or does not want to, it is often seen as a sign that something is wrong.
"The remarkable thing is that the dream of upward mobility didn't die during the Great Depression," said Dr. Christopher Lasch, a professor of history at the University of Rochester who has studied changes in social expectations.
But the assumption that the next generation will be better off is no longer valid. Growing numbers of adults who were raised in middle-class families are finding it difficult to reach their parents' level of financial and professional success. The former guarantors of success, like getting an education and having family connections, do not work as well now and, in some cases, are being rejected by the younger generation.
"Just because you've paid for your child's college degree doesn't mean that it's going to have the occupational payoff that you expected," said Dr. Laurence Steinberg, a professor of psychology at Temple University. "This is in stark contrast to the parents' generation's experience, especially those parents who began their careers in the 50's and 60's."
Although many people blame changes in the economy for the shift in financial and professional achievement, psychologists and other family experts say it is much more complex. Some young adults deliberately pursue a different path that is less financially rewarding.
"It's not just that opportunities aren't as abundant as they were or that it's more difficult to buy a house," Dr. Lasch said. "Professional and business careers have lost some of their luster. The new generation is questioning whether the results are worth the struggle, the costs and the pain."
When a child chooses a less prestigious career, parents might interpret it as a sign of their own failure. "Many times, parents see this as a rejection of all that they've worked for," said Dr. Harold D. Grotevant, a professor of family social science at the University of Minnesota. "But it means that you've raised children who are independent enough to know what's best for them."
No government or private agency keeps statistics on downward social mobility. It is most obvious, however, among the children of high-achieving parents, those who would have the easiest time succeeding because of their families' money, connections and power.
Dr. Steven Berglas, a clinical psychologist at the Harvard Medical School, has found that children are sometimes intimidated by the professional and social success of their parents. Rather than compete with parents they see as infallible, they sabotage themselves. "I sometimes encourage parents to do what I call a 'strategic pratfall,' " Dr. Berglas said. "Let your child see that you do some things poorly, especially if it's something that your child does better than you. That helps him realize that you each have your own talents."
Choosing not to follow the example of successful parents is a way for children to assert their individuality. "You can't be a Carnegie and beat out Andrew," Dr. Berglas said. "You have to follow a different track."
 
What is Success? Generations Disagree
 PSYCHOLOGISTS are quick to emphasize the difference between young adults who are lazy and those who have values and aspirations different from their parents'. Here are some things to keep in mind if you are concerned about your child's choice of career.
Expect your children to experiment with different careers.
The first decision a child makes is not a permanent commitment. "I'd be careful as a parent not to do things that make it look like you won't tolerate that type of experimentation; it will backfire," said Dr. Laurence Steinberg, a professor of psychology at Temple University.
Look at your assumptions about what constitutes success.
"Another way to think about upward mobility is to look at life satisfaction instead of money, power or prestige," Dr. Steinberg said. "If you can persuade yourself that you've done a great job as a parent because your child is happier or more satisfied than you were at that age, that will help."
Remember that you cannot deny with your words what you assert with your actions.
Pay attention to any discrepancy between what you say to your child about work and the way you treat your own career.
"Every child I've treated for underachievement has had parents who said that they never pushed their children to achieve," said Dr. Steven Berglas, a clinical psychologist at the Harvard Medical School. "But the children pick up those expectations by watching their parents work 60-hour weeks. That tells children that career success is critical."
Encourage your child's financial independence.
Parents sometimes need to tread a fine line here. Even the most talented artist or musician will probably need an outside source of income for a few years. But paying part of young adults' rent can make them feel even more dependent at a time when they are struggling with their identity. And immature young adults whose financial needs are met by their parents have little motivation to break out of a childhood role.
"You have to distinguish between a child who is pursuing a bona fide occupation versus one who's just goofing off," Dr. Steinberg said.
Don't focus on your children's careers as reflections of their worth.
This is difficult for parents who have devoted much of their lives to their own careers.
"There's overwhelming evidence that children take on the fundamental values of their parents," said Dr. Harold D. Grotevant, a professor of family social science at the University of Minnesota. "And that's what's really important."

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING -- ADDENDA;
Miscellany

BYLINE: By Stuart Elliott

 BODY:

* A commercial that advocates an extension of unemployment benefits has appeared on four television stations in Washington and Charleston, W. Va., this week, after being refused by six others in Houston and Washington. The spot, produced by Citizen Action, a consumer group, depicts a newly unemployed husband and wife writing a letter to President Bush asking him not to veto the bill granting added weeks of unemployment benefits. Several of the stations that declined the spot say they do not accept issue-oriented advertising.
 
* Kentucky Fried Chicken, Dunkin' Donuts and Nestle Food have agreed to stop advertising and marketing campaigns suggesting that certain of their products are healthy, low-fat alternatives to their regular fare. The agreement was reached with Robert Abrams, New York State attorney general.
 

* A set of advertising industry "green principles" is scheduled to be presented today at the Advertising Age Green Marketing West conference in San Francisco. Along with support of recycling and environmental education programs within agencies, the initiatives ask signers to encourage their clients and vendors "to make a commitment to environmental responsibility." The statement has been signed by top executives from Saatchi & Saatchi Worldwide, Altschiller Reitzfeld/Tracy-Locke and other agencies, and is being circulated at the conference.
 
* TBWA Advertising Inc., New York, and Carat, London, have formed a media joint venture called Eurospace. It will provide media planning, buying and research services to all of the agency's clients in France, Spain, Italy, Germany, Holland and Belgium as well as selected clients in the United Kingdom.

SECTION: Section A; Page 27; Column 5; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 722 words

HEADLINE: In the Nation;
Finally Facing Facts

BYLINE: By TOM WICKER

 BODY:
When Alan Greenspan offered an optimistic economic forecast in July, he was careful to hedge; recovery, he said, "could be muted or even falter." This was scarcely noticed in the euphoria of the Fed chairman's conclusion that the recession was over and election year 1992 would see a growth rate of 2 to 3 percent.
More than three months later, Mr. Greenspan has confirmed the hedge rather than the prediction. He said the economy was "demonstrably sluggish" though moving forward; and the optimism of July was notably missing from his October speech to business executives in Rhode Island. 
 This follows President Bush's brief show of concern for the domestic situation, and his renewed call for his favorite panacea, a capital gains tax reduction. The onset of 1992 may more nearly have been at work than real alarm over a lagging economy, but let us judge not that we be not judged; he may have decided belatedly that he wants to be "the economy President" too.
Numerous polls show that the public is not optimistic about the economy, wants something done and is beginning to question why Mr. Bush does not spend as much time on the Middle West as on the Middle East. These concerns surely are not lost on Mario Cuomo, as he ponders anew the question of running for President.
On Capitol Hill, too, the economic climate suddenly is being viewed with nearly as much alarm as is usually lavished on the deficit. Numerous tax-cut proposals have been offered, mostly by Democrats, for "jump-starting" the economy. You're nearing Social Security if you can remember when it was called "pump-priming."
By whatever name, economic stimulus is needed; but tax cuts are not well designed for this job. For one thing, they take a lot of time to be enacted and to have stimulative effect; for another, they can't stimulate if they have to be offset by cuts in government spending, as the present "budget agreement" requires and as current proposals recognize.
Mr. Greenspan is being urged, particularly by the Bush Administration, to cut interest rates further; but that won't help much either. The real problem is that too many potential consumers are out of work or fearful of layoff, hence have no money to spend or are guarding what they have. Too many businessmen, in such a climate, are afraid even to seek loans to start or expand businesses and provide new jobs.
As John Kenneth Galbraith, the liberal economist, put it in a recent speech to the National Press Club, interest rates can work against inflation because "then you are pulling on the string. Against recession, you are shoving on the string."
But at least Congress, the Fed and even the White House are finally facing the facts that almost any householder or businessman or laid-off worker has known for months -- the recession is not over, the recovery, if any, is indeed muted and faltering, a lot of Americans are in bad shape and afraid things are getting worse, and something needs to be done.
The economy I described in this space in August is more "sluggish" now: "Jobs are disappearing, layoffs are up, consumption is down, unemployment is nearly 7 percent, retail sales are lagging, real estate is a disaster area, banks are failing, credit is tight, wages are stagnant. . . ."
Outside the Beltway, moreover, states and cities are being hit from one side by recession and falling revenues, and from the other by Federal aid drastically reduced in obeisance to right-wing theology. The result, from New York to California, is rising state and local taxes, declining state and local services and spending, extensive state and local worker layoffs -- all deflationary.
If Congress could get over its deficit hysteria, if the Fed were not so spooked by the ghost of inflation, if Mr. Bush really meant business, the dreaded S-word -- spending -- might not seem so evil. Billions poured, for example, into highways, bridges, sewers and other needed infrastructural improvements would provide jobs, increase purchasing power, reduce outlays for unemployment, stimulate business, raise revenues, train workers, take the idle off the streets. In the largely forgotten long term, that kind of investment in America might even reduce a deficit swollen in the short term by recession.
If that be heresy, what has orthodoxy done for you lately?

SECTION: Section A; Page 26; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 264 words

HEADLINE: A Vote for Democracy

 BODY:
America's active participation in democracy is dwindling. Barely half the eligible voters took part in the 1988 Presidential election -- the lowest rate in 64 years. The turnout in non-Presidential years is smaller still.
A bill that would help reverse this ominous trend is now threatened with a Senate filibuster. The bill won't even make it to the floor unless the favorable votes of 60 senators can be mustered today to close off debate on a motion to proceed. Senators who care about enlarging democracy will vote yes. 
 Why is turnout so low? Uninspiring campaigns are surely a factor. But the problem begins with simple arithmetic: Only 60 percent of eligible voters are registered. The "motor-voter" bill sponsored by Senator Wendell Ford, Democrat of Kentucky, could boost that figure tremendously -- to an estimated 90 percent -- by requiring states to enable qualified citizens to register when they apply for a driver's license. States would also be required to permit registration by mail and to make registration forms available at public agencies like welfare and unemployment offices.
Action on the measure, similar to one passed by the House last year, was blocked by Senate Republicans this summer by a single vote. But obstructionism needn't prevail if the lonely Republican supporters pick up a few allies, like Arlen Specter of Pennsylvania and Bob Packwood of Oregon.
Opponents of the "motor-voter" measure exaggerate its costs and the chance for increased voting fraud. What cannot be exaggerated is the cost, to democracy, of standing still.

HEADLINE: Bond Proposition Will Help New York Recover

 BODY:
To the Editor:
On Election Day, Nov. 5, the people of New York State have the opportunity to put New York on the road to economic recovery. Proposition 1 on the ballot will allow the State Legislature to increase the Job Development Authority's authorization of state bonds from $600 million to $900 million. This power to extend low-interest loans to companies looking to expand in New York State has proved to be a valuable economic tool in stimulating economic growth. 
 The reluctance to reform the Medicaid and Social Service programs has produced billion-dollar budget deficits that were filled with tax increases and cutbacks in state services. This trend has got to stop. Increasing the Job Development Authority's bonding authorization is a good first step to reverse that trend. Since the agency's inception almost 150,000 people have become employed by companies assisted by its loans. New York can be an attractive place to do business. But in these tough economic times many companies do not have the necessary capital to expand and create new jobs.
Approving Proposition 1 is a necessary first step for putting New York State on the road to economic recovery.
 
RALPH J. MARINO
Majority Leader, State Senate
Albany, Oct. 22, 1991
